The Logical Necessity of a First in a Sequence
Al-Baqillani's logical refutation of denying a first in a sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's argument that any sequence of occurrences must have a first, and denying that leads to sophistry.

Core Idea
Acknowledging a sequence implies a first; denying it is self-contradictory.

This passage from al-Baqillani's 'al-Naqd al-Kabir' tackles a key logical principle: if you admit that one thing comes after another, you have implicitly admitted a beginning. The argument is presented as a refutation of those who deny that certain divine acts have a first. Let's see how al-Baqillani exposes the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the sequence of letters (sin, ba, mim) and the logical flow.
The letters are used as an example of a sequence.
2
Understand the argument structure
Notice the conditional: if someone acknowledges one thing after another, they acknowledge a first. Then if they deny the first, they contradict themselves.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the conclusion
Al-Baqillani concludes that such a person has joined the sophists (safasata) and cannot be guided by proof.
Sophists reject self-evident truths.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this argument applies to theological debates about the eternity of attributes or the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
14 - كتاب «النقض الكبير» ومنه هذا النص الذي أورده إمام الحرمين في الشامل قال أبو بكر الباقلاني في «النقض الكبير : من زعم أن السين من الله بعد الباء والميم بعد السين الواقعة بعد الباء لا أول له فقد خرج عن المعقول، وجحد الضرورة وأنكر البديهة، فإن اعترف بوقوع شيء بعد شيء فقد اعترف بأوليته، فإن ادعى أنه لا أول له، فقد سقطت مما جنته وتعين لحوقه بالسفسطة . وكيف يرجى أن يرشد بالدليل من يتواقح في جحد الضروري.
14 - The book of 'The Great Refutation' and from it is this text which al-Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] cited in 'al-Shamil'. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said in 'al-Naqd al-Kabir': 'Whoever claims that the sin from God after the ba and the mim after the sin occurring after the ba has no first has exited from reason, denied the necessary, and rejected the intuitive. For if he acknowledges the occurrence of something after something, he has acknowledged its firstness. If he then claims that it has no first, he has fallen into what he has committed and it becomes necessary for him to join the sophists. How can one who is impudent in denying the necessary be guided by proof?'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from al-Baqillani's 'al-Naqd al-Kabir', as reported by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni in his work 'al-Shamil'. It is a logical refutation of someone who posits that a sequence of letters (sin, ba, mim) from God has no beginning. The argument proceeds in steps: 1) If someone claims that sin comes after ba, and mim comes after sin (which comes after ba), then there is a sequence. 2) Admitting that one thing occurs after another implicitly acknowledges a first in that sequence (the ba). 3) If the same person then denies that the sequence has a first, they contradict themselves. 4) Such a contradiction leads to sophistry (safasata), which is the rejection of self-evident truths (badiha). The rhetorical question at the end emphasizes that someone who brazenly denies what is necessary cannot be guided by rational proof. This argument likely targets a theological opponent who denied that the divine attributes or the Qur'anic letters had any beginning, possibly related to the createdness of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
النقض الكبير
السين
الباء
الميم
البديهة
السفسطة
الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for a sequence of occurrences to have no beginning.
Correction: Al-Baqillani argues that if you admit one thing comes after another, you necessarily admit a first in that sequence. Denying the first is a self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, if someone acknowledges that one thing comes after another, what do they implicitly admit?
A) That the sequence has no end
B) That there is a first in the sequence
C) That God is eternal
D) That the letters are uncreated

Key Takeaway
Acknowledging a sequence necessarily implies acknowledging a first; denying this leads to logical contradiction and sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your faith or daily life do you implicitly acknowledge a 'first' (a starting point or origin) but then act or speak as if that first doesn't matter? What would it take for you to honor that first fully?
?
Al-Baqillani says that denying the obvious (badiha) puts one outside reason itself. When have you rationalized away something you knew deep down to be true because accepting it would require you to change? Will you stop this denial today?

The Maker of the World is One
Baqillani proves oneness through the necessity of impotence in a multiplicity of gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's oneness based on the impossibility of conflicting wills among multiple gods.

Core Idea
Two gods would conflict; conflict proves impotence; impotence is impossible for God.

This passage from Imam Baqillani lays out a concise logical proof for tawhid. By considering what would happen if there were two gods with opposing wills, Baqillani shows that only one eternal Creator can exist. The argument rests on a simple but powerful principle: the impossibility of contradictory wills being simultaneously fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a conditional structure: if A then B.
2
Analyze the hypothetical scenario
Consider the scenario Baqillani presents: two gods willing opposite things for the same body. Ask yourself: can both wills be fulfilled? If not, what does that imply about each god's power?
The key is that the scenario is logically possible, not that it actually happens.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow the argument step by step from the impossibility of fulfilling both wills to the necessity of impotence. Then connect impotence to being created, and finally to the impossibility of multiple gods.
The argument ends with a statement about God's nature: He cannot be impotent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في أن صانع العالم واحد وليس يجوز أن يكون صانع العالم اثنين ولا أكثر من ذلك والدليل على ذلك أن الاثنين يصح أن يختلفا ويوجد أحدهما ضد مراد الآخر فلو اختلفا وأراد أحدهما إحياء جسم وأراد الآخر إماتته لوجب أن يلحقهما العجز أو واحداً منهما لأنه محال أن يتمّ ما يريدان جميعاً لتضاد مراديهما فوجب أن لا يتمّا أو يتم مراد أحدهما فيلحق من لم يتم مراده العجز أو لا يتم مرادهما فيلحقهما العجز والعجز من سمات الحدث والقديم الإله لا يجوز أن يكون عاجزاً
Chapter: Discourse that the maker of the world is one. It is not permissible that the maker of the world be two, nor more than that. The proof of that is that two can differ, and one can be the opposite of the other's will. If they differ, and one wills to revive a body and the other wills to make it die, then impotence must befall both or one of them, because it is impossible that both wills be fulfilled due to their opposition. Therefore, either neither is fulfilled or the will of one is fulfilled, so the one whose will is not fulfilled is afflicted with impotence, or neither's will is fulfilled, so both are afflicted with impotence. Impotence is a characteristic of originatedness, and the eternal God cannot be impotent.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the oneness of the world's maker (tawḥīd). Baqillani argues that if there were two gods, they could disagree in will—e.g., one wants to give life, the other death. Since both wills cannot be simultaneously realized, either both fail (showing both are impotent) or one fails (showing that one is impotent). Impotence (ʿajz) is a mark of being originated, and God, being eternal, cannot be impotent. Therefore, there can only be one God.

Key Terms
واحد
اثنان
إرادة
عجز
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if two gods always agreed, there would be no problem.
Correction: Baqillani's proof works because the possibility of disagreement is enough to show that plurality is impossible; agreement is not guaranteed, and logical possibility of conflict is sufficient.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani conclude if two gods will opposite things?
A) Both wills are fulfilled
B) One god overpowers the other
C) Impotence must befall at least one
D) The world would cease to exist

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of two conflicting wills proves that the Creator must be one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself worrying more about what people think than what Allah wills, are you not, in that moment, acting as if there is another 'god' whose will competes with His? What specific fear will you surrender to Allah today to prove your tawhid is real?
?
Baqillani’s proof shows that two conflicting wills cannot both be fulfilled. In your daily life, whose will often wins—yours or Allah’s? What is one area where you know Allah’s command but choose your own desire, and how will you reverse that today?
?
If you truly believed that God alone has the power to grant life and death, would you still hold grudges or fear failure? What does your anxiety about these things reveal about the state of your belief in His oneness, and what concrete step will you take to align your heart with this proof?

The Creator's Life Proven Through Attributes
Baqillani proves the creator is living through his attributes of power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core proof that the creator must be living because he possesses agency, knowledge, and power.

Core Idea
True agency, knowledge, and power all require life.

In this brief passage, Imam al-Baqillani presents a concise q&a proof for the creator's life. He argues that since the creator is an agent, knowing, and powerful, he must necessarily be living. This lesson unpacks that deduction and notes the beginning of a further argument about well-ordered actions. Although the text is cut off, the essential link is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the author's direct wording.
Notice that the passage is incomplete; the final sentence breaks off.
2
Identify the question being posed
The passage starts with an imagined question: 'What is the proof that the maker of things is living?' This sets up the reasoning.
3
Note the three attributes used
Baqillani lists three attributes of the creator: agent (fāʿil), knowing (ʿālim), and powerful (qādir). These are the premises of the proof.
These attributes are well-established from previous arguments.
4
Understand the logical inference
The proof states that an agent, knowing, and powerful being must be living. This is a necessary connection in Baqillani's reasoning.
Consider why life might be required for these attributes to be real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن صانع العالم حي فإن قال قائل : فما الدليل على أن صانع الأشياء حي؟ قيل له : الدليل على ذلك أنه فاعل عالم قادر والفاعل العالم القادر لا يكون إلا حياً. يُبين ذلك أنه لو جاز أن تظهر الأفعال المحكمات ممن ليس بحي ولا عـالـم ولا
Chapter: That the maker of the world is living. If someone says: What is the proof that the maker of things is living? It is said to him: The proof of that is that He is an agent, knowing, powerful, and the agent, knowing, powerful is only living. That makes clear that if it were permissible that well-ordered actions appear from one who is not living, nor knowing, nor...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer proof that the maker of the world is living (ḥayy). Baqillani presents the question and then the response: the maker is an agent (fāʿil), knowing (ʿālim), and powerful (qādir), and such a being must be living. He begins to elaborate that if well-ordered actions (afʿāl muḥkamāt) could come from one who is not living, knowing, and powerful, that would be impossible, but the text cuts off before completing the argument.

Key Terms
حي
فاعل
عالم
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is not necessarily implied by being an agent, knowing, and powerful.
Correction: Baqillani argues that in reality, true agency, knowledge, and power cannot exist without life; they are attributes that only a living being can possess.

Checkpoint
What initial proof does Baqillani give for the creator's life?
A) The creator is powerful and knowing
B) The creator must be first cause
C) The world has order
D) The creator is eternal

Key Takeaway
The creator's life is proven by his attributes of agency, knowledge, and power, which are only possible for a living being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in a living God—but do you live as though He is truly alive and aware of every thought, word, and action? What would change today if you truly internalized that His life is not a distant concept but a present reality?
?
If the creator’s life is proven through His perfect knowledge and power, how does your own lack of knowledge or power in certain areas of your life remind you of your dependence on Him? In what specific situation do you need to stop acting autonomously and rely on the Living One?
?
The proof in this lesson is rational, but does your heart feel the weight of serving a Living God vs. a lifeless idea? When you pray, do you sense you are addressing a Living Being who hears and responds, or are your words empty? Commit to one change in your prayer to make it more real.

The Creator Is Alive
Baqillani proves the Creator is alive through a reductio argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator's life is necessarily implied by His knowledge and power, and how Baqillani uses a reductio argument to prove this.

Core Idea
Only a living being can be truly knowing and powerful.

Baqillani presents a concise but powerful proof that the Creator is alive. He begins with the premise that the Creator is all-knowing and all-powerful, which are attributes that can only belong to a living being. To reinforce this, he imagines a world where lifeless things could produce ordered actions — a scenario that would destroy our ability to trust any action as coming from a living agent. This simple yet devastating argument shows why atheistic or deistic positions that deny God's life are rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Get a sense of the dialectical style: question, answer, clarification, conclusion.
2
Identify the main proof
Find the core statement: the Creator is knowing and powerful, and such a being must be alive. Notice the logical necessity: 'فالفاعل العالم القادر لا يكون إلا حياً'.
This is the central syllogism.
3
Examine the reductio
Baqillani argues that if lifeless beings could produce ordered actions (أفعال محكمات), then we would not be able to distinguish actions of living vs. dead. Trace this hypothetical and its absurd consequence.
The reductio strengthens the proof by showing the alternative is self-defeating.
4
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider the implication: if the opponent's view were true, even the opponent's own questioning would be unreliable. This shows the argument is not just theoretical but undermines all discourse.
Baqillani calls this 'ignorance and denial'.
5
Conclude with the necessity
The only consistent conclusion is that the Creator is knowing, powerful, and alive (عالماً قادراً حياً). This is necessary (وجب). Reflect on how this fits into the broader Islamic understanding of God.
This conclusion is a cornerstone of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن صانع العالم حي فإن قال قائل : فما الدليل على أن صانع الأشياء حي؟ قيل له : الدليل على ذلك أنه فاعل عالم قادر والفاعل العالم القادر لا يكون إلا حياً. يُبين ذلك أنه لو جاز أن تظهر الأفعال المحكمات ممن ليس بحي ولا عالم ولا قادر لم ندر لعل ما يظهر من الناس من الكتابة والصياغة وسائر التصرف يظهر منهم وهم موتى جماد عَجَزة، ولم ندر لعل السائل لنا عن هذا السؤال المناظر لنا على تصحيح مذهبه وإبطال قولنا ميت أو موات ؛ وهذا تجاهل من راكبه وجَحد لما نحن إلى إثباته مضطرون فوجب أن يكون الصانع عالماً قادراً حياً .
Chapter that the maker of the world is alive. If someone says: What is the proof that the maker of things is alive? It is said to him: The proof for that is that He is an actor, knowing, powerful, and the actor who is knowing and powerful is only alive. That is clarified by that if it were permissible that well-ordered actions appear from one who is not alive nor knowing nor powerful, we would not know how what appears from people of writing, crafting, and other actions appears from them while they are dead, inanimate, or incapable. And we would not know how the questioner asking us about this question, the disputant with us for correcting his doctrine and invalidating our statement, is dead or inanimate. And this is ignorance from its rider and denial of what we are compelled to affirm. So it is necessary that the maker be knowing, powerful, alive.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Creator is alive. Baqillani argues that the Creator is described as all-powerful and all-knowing, and such a being cannot be dead; only a living being can have knowledge and power. He strengthens this by a reductio ad absurdum: if we allow that well-ordered actions could come from a lifeless being, then we could not trust that our own actions (like writing) come from living beings, nor could we trust the opponent who argues with us. Thus, common sense compels that the Creator must be alive.

Key Terms
حي
فاعل
عالم
قادر
أفعال محكمات
موات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes that knowing and powerful imply life without proof.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it relies on the self-evident truth that knowledge and power are attributes of living beings. The reductio further shows that denying life leads to absurdity, reinforcing the necessity.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani demonstrate the absurdity of the Creator not being alive?
A) He shows dead people can act
B) He argues that if dead could act, we wouldn't know if our actions come from living
C) He quotes scripture
D) He uses logical necessity alone

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be alive because only a living being can possess knowledge and power, and the alternative leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only a living, knowing, and powerful being can produce well-ordered actions, then what does your own ability to think, reason, and act say about the One who gave you these capacities? How does this truth demand you live today?
?
The proof shows that denying the Creator's life leads to absurdity—you wouldn't even be able to trust your own reasoning. Are there areas in your life where you act as if the world runs on random, dead forces? What would it mean to fully live as if every moment is sustained by a living, knowing, powerful Creator?
?
Baqillani forces the opponent to consider that his own questioning would be meaningless if the source of his intellect were not alive. When you question Allah's commands or His wisdom, are you not implicitly relying on the very life He gave you? How will you respond to that reality?

The Creator is Knowing: Proof from Well-Ordered Actions
Baqillani proves the Creator's knowledge via the analogy of well-ordered acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the well-ordered nature of creation proves the Creator's knowledge.

Core Idea
Well-ordered actions can only come from a knower.

Baqillani anticipates an objection about the proof for God's knowledge. He responds with a powerful analogy: just as human crafts (like writing or weaving) require knowledge, the far more precise and ordered actions of Allah clearly indicate that He is knowing. This lesson unpacks that concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice how Baqillani structures the objection and his response.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Find the question posed by the objector and how Baqillani directly addresses it with evidence.
3
Understand the analogy
Compare the human examples (crafting, commerce, writing, weaving) with the divine actions. Why does the analogy work?
The key is that order and precision are signs of knowledge.
4
Apply the argument
Reflect on why this argument is considered a sound logical proof in Islamic theology.
Consider how it avoids anthropomorphism by focusing on the attribute of order.
5
Review key terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms used, especially أفعال محكمات (well-ordered actions) and أدق (more precise).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن الصانع عالم فإن قال قائل : وما الدليل على صحة ما تذهبون إليه في أنه عالم؟ قيل له: يدل على ذلك وجود الأفعال المحكمات منه لأن الأفعال المحكمات لا تقع منا على ترتيب ونظام كالصياغة والتجارة والكتابة والنساجة إلا من عالم وأفعال الله تعالى أدق وأحكم فكانت أولى بأن تدل على أنه عالم .
Chapter that the maker is knowing. If someone says: What is the proof for the correctness of what you hold regarding that He is knowing? It is said to him: That is indicated by the existence of well-ordered actions from Him, because well-ordered actions do not occur from us in arrangement and order, like crafting, commerce, writing, and weaving, except from a knower, and the actions of Allah, exalted, are more precise and more ordered, so they are more deserving of indicating that He is knowing.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Creator is knowing. Baqillani uses the observed order and precision of human actions as evidence that they require knowledge. Since God's actions are even more precise and ordered (the universe), they must come from a knower. The analogy shows that human crafts like writing and weaving only proceed from those with knowledge; thus, the divine craftsmanship implies divine knowledge.

Key Terms
عالم
أفعال محكمات
ترتيب
نظام
أدق
أحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument anthropomorphizes God by comparing His actions directly to human actions.
Correction: The argument does not claim that God's actions are like human actions in essence; it uses the known principle that order implies knowledge as a logical inference, not a physical similarity. The analogy is based on the concept of order itself, which is a sign of knowledge regardless of the agent.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence that the Creator is knowing according to Baqillani?
A) The complexity of the universe
B) The fact that actions without order can come from anyone
C) Human well-ordered actions require knowledge, so God's more ordered actions require more knowledge
D) The Quran mentions God's knowledge

Key Takeaway
Baqillani proves that the Creator is knowing by arguing that the well-ordered nature of creation mirrors human ordered actions, which necessarily come from a knower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate order in your own body—the beating of your heart, the coordination of your limbs—does it stir in you a living awareness that every detail is crafted by a Knowing Creator? Or do you let familiarity steal that awe? Commit today to pause at least once and whisper, 'O Knower, I see Your knowledge in this.'
?
The passage argues that even simple human tasks like writing a letter require knowledge. Think of the last time you wrote an email or a message. Did you recognize that your ability to do so is a borrowed knowledge from the All-Knowing? If so, how can you use that pen today to write something that pleases Him?
?
If Allah's actions are 'more precise and more ordered' than the most meticulous human craft, then every trial and blessing in your life is precisely placed. Are you willing to stop questioning the order of His decree and instead say, 'My Lord, I trust that Your knowledge is perfect even when I don't understand'? What is one specific area of your life where you need to submit to His perfect order today?

Proving Divine Attributes: Hearing, Seeing, and Speech
Baqillani proves the Creator has hearing, seeing, and speech by elimination of defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani proves that the Creator necessarily possesses the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech, by reasoning from the absence of defects.

Core Idea
The Creator’s perfection requires all positive attributes; their opposites are impossible for the Eternal.

Imam al-Baqillani presents a concise but powerful argument for three essential divine attributes: hearing, seeing, and speech. He begins from the already-established attribute of life and shows that a living being must be capable of these attributes. If the Creator lacked them, He would be described by their opposites – defects that prove contingency. Since the Eternal cannot be contingent, He must be hearing, seeing, and speaking.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure. The passage begins with a hypothetical questioner, then provides the proof.
The question format is common in kalam works to address potential doubts.
2
Identify the core premise
The proof hinges on the fact that the Creator is already established as alive (hayy). Identify where this premise is stated and why it matters.
Baqillani assumes the proof of life was given earlier.
3
Understand the argument from opposites
If the Creator were not described by these attributes, then He would necessarily have their opposites (e.g., deafness, muteness). These opposites are defects (afat) and indicate contingency (hadath). Why are defects signs of contingency?
Contingent things are subject to imperfections; the Eternal must be perfect.
4
Apply the conclusion
Since it is impossible for the Eternal to have any defect, He must have the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech. Confirm that you can articulate this argument in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن الصانع سميع بصير متكلم فإن قال قائل : فما الدليل على أنه سميع بصير متكلم؟ قيل له : الدليل على ذلك أنه قد ثبت أنه حي بما وصفناه والحي يصح أن يكون متكلماً سميعاً بصيراً ومتى عري من صحة وصفه بها فلا بد من هذه الأوصاف مع أن يكون موصوفاً بأضدادها من الخرس والسكوت والعمى والصمم وكل هذه الأمور آفات قد اتفق على أنها تدل على حدث الموصوف بها فلم يجز وصف القديم بشيء منها فوجب أن يكون سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً .
Chapter that the maker is hearing, seeing, speaking. If someone says: What is the proof that He is hearing, seeing, speaking? It is said to him: The proof for that is that it has been established that He is alive by what we described, and the living being can be speaking, hearing, seeing. And when it is stripped of the validity of describing him with these, then it is necessary that he be described with their opposites: muteness, silence, blindness, and deafness. And all these matters are defects that it has been agreed that they indicate the contingency of the one described with them. So it is not permissible to describe the Eternal with any of them. So it is necessary that He be hearing, seeing, speaking.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Creator has attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech. Baqillani's deduction: The Creator is already proven to be alive. A living being is capable of having these attributes. If He did not have them, then He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are deficiencies and signs of contingency. Since the Eternal cannot have defects, He must possess these attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributes like hearing, seeing, and speech are anthropomorphic, implying a body like humans.
Correction: These attributes are affirmed in a way befitting God, without likeness to creation. The argument here is based on logical necessity, not physical similarity.

Checkpoint
What would the Creator necessarily have if He were not hearing?
A) Seeing
B) Deafness
C) Silence
D) Power

Key Takeaway
The Creator must possess the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech because their opposites are defects that indicate contingency, and the Eternal cannot be contingent.

?
The proof says God cannot have defects because He is Eternal. But you live each day as if you will never die, ignoring your own defects of sin. What one defect—a sin you've normalized—will you work to remove today, knowing that your Lord is perfect and you are not?

Proof That the Maker is Knowing Through Well-Ordered Actions
Baqillani presents the argument from well-ordered actions for the Maker's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the order and precision of human actions as a premise to argue that the Maker's actions, being even more subtle, necessarily indicate His knowledge.

Core Idea
Well-ordered actions prove a knowing maker; God's actions are the most ordered.

Baqillani now addresses a common question: how do we know that the Maker is knowing? The answer lies in observing the well-ordered actions we see in creation. By reflecting on what makes human actions skilled and deliberate, we can ascend to a higher proof for divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument flow.
Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the objection
The objector asks: 'What is the evidence that the Maker is knowing?' This sets up the need for a proof.
3
Analyze the premise about human actions
Baqillani states that well-ordered actions (like crafting, trade, writing, weaving) only occur from a knowing agent in human experience.
This is the key empirical premise.
4
Apply the priority argument
Since God's actions are even more subtle and precise than human actions, they are more deserving of indicating knowledge. Recognize this as an 'awla' (priority) argument, not a simple analogy.
This strengthens the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أن الصانع عالم. فإن قال قائل: وما الدليل على صحة ما تذهبون إليه في أنه عالم؟ قيل له: يدل على ذلك وجود الأفعال المحكمات منه، لأن الأفعال المحكمات لا تقع منا على ترتيب ونظام كالصياغة والتجارة والكتابة والنساجة إلا من عالم، وأفعال الله تعالى أدق وأحكم فكانت أولى بأن تدل على أنه حي عالم.
Chapter that the Maker is knowing. If someone says: What is the evidence for the correctness of what you hold about Him being knowing? It is said to him: The evidence for that is the existence of well-ordered actions from Him. Because well-ordered actions do not occur from us with order and system like crafting, trade, writing, and weaving except from a knowing agent. And the actions of God Almighty are more subtle and more precise, so they are more deserving of indicating that He is living, knowing.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (istidlal) for the attribute of knowledge in God. It follows the structure of an objection and answer. The objector asks for the evidence. The answer first states that well-ordered actions (al-af'al al-muhkamat) from the Maker are the proof. Then it explains the premise: in human experience, actions done with order and system—such as crafting, trade, writing, weaving—only come from a knowing agent. Then it applies an argument from the greater priority: God's actions are even more subtle and precise than human actions, so they are even more indicative of His knowledge. The conclusion is that God is living and knowing (the attribute of life is also mentioned as a basis for knowledge).

Key Terms
صانع
عالم
الأفعال المحكمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is a simple analogy between human and divine actions.
Correction: It is a stronger priority argument (awla) because divine actions are more perfect than human actions, making the indication even stronger.

Checkpoint

A) Human actions are not well-ordered.
B) God's actions are more subtle and precise.
C) Humans sometimes err, but God never errs.
D) God's actions are infinite.

Key Takeaway
Well-ordered actions in the world, when compared to human crafts, prove that the Maker possesses perfect knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Proof of the Divine Will from Temporal Order
Baqillani proves the Maker's will from contingency of temporal order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable order of events in the world demonstrates that God possesses a will (irada).

Core Idea
The specific arrangement of events, which could have been otherwise, points to a willing Creator.

Baqillani presents a concise and powerful argument for the attribute of divine will. He draws our attention to the temporal sequence of actions in the world. Some events precede others, some follow. But this order is not necessary; each event could have occurred at a different time. This contingency of order demands a selector—an intention or will—that determines the actual sequence. In this lesson, we will unpack this proof and see how it establishes God as al-Murīd (the Willer).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the question-answer format and the key terms used.
Pay attention to the words 'قصد' (intention) and 'تقدم'/'تأخر' (precedence and delay).
2
Identify the key premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) actions from God exhibit temporal order (precedence and delay), and (2) this order is contingent—each action could have been earlier or later. Identify these in the text.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Given the contingency, the actual order must result from a choice (qasd). This choice is the will. Reflect on why without it, no order would have occurred.
The proof is a classic kalam argument from the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أنه مريد. فإن قال قائل: فما الدليل على أنه مريد؟ قيل له: وجود الأفعال منه وتقدم بعضها على بعض في الوجود وتأخر بعضها عن بعض في الوجود، فلولا أنه قصد إلى إيجاد ما أوجد منها لما وجد ولا تقدم من ذلك ما تقدم ولا تأخر منه ما تأخر مع صحة تقدمه بدلا من تأخره وتأخره بدلا من تقدمه.
Chapter that He is willing. If someone says: What is the evidence that He is willing? It is said to him: The existence of actions from Him, and the precedence of some of them over others in existence, and the delay of some of them after others in existence. For if He had not intended to bring into existence what He brought into existence from them, they would not have existed; nor would any of them have preceded nor delayed, despite the possibility of its precedence instead of its delay and its delay instead of its precedence.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the attribute of will (irada) in God. It follows the same question-answer format. The evidence is drawn from the observable order of created actions/events: some come before others, some after. The argument: (1) Actions exist from God. (2) These actions exhibit temporal order: some precede, some follow. (3) This order is not necessary in itself; each action could have been earlier or later. (4) Therefore, the actual order must be due to a choice (qasd) or intention of the agent, which is the will. (5) Without such intent, the actions would not have existed at all in this specific order. Thus, God must be described as willing (mureed). The proof hinges on contingency of temporal order: since alternatives are equally possible, the actual order requires a selector.

Key Terms
مريد
إرادة
تقدم
تأخر
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof only applies to voluntary human actions, but it applies to all temporal events in the world.
Correction: Baqillani's argument is general: any temporal sequence in creation that could have been arranged differently requires a will to account for its actual order.

Checkpoint
What is the key evidence Baqillani uses to prove that God is willing?
A) The complexity of creation.
B) The temporal order of actions, some preceding and some following.
C) The fact that God has power.
D) The existence of evil.

Key Takeaway
The contingency of temporal order in creation demonstrates that God acts with will and intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that without will, no particular order would exist. How does this challenge your view of 'bad luck' or 'unfortunate timing'? What would it mean to accept that everything in your life has a chosen order?
?
If God's will selects every sequence, then your current situation—every detail—is intended. Does this comfort you or unsettle you? What would change if you lived today fully aware that nothing is out of place?

Divine Anger and Pleasure as Will, Not Passion
Baqillani argues that divine anger and pleasure are will, not passions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani argues that God's anger and pleasure are identical to His will to reward or punish, and why passions are impossible for God.

Core Idea
God's anger and pleasure are His will, not emotions.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani responds to an objector who questions how Muslims can affirm that God is angry and pleased without attributing human-like passions to Him. The answer is a clear dichotomy: either these are will, or they are passions involving change. Since God is eternal and transcendent, only the first interpretation is possible. Let's follow the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the opening objection where someone asks whether Muslims affirm that God is angry and pleased. Notice how Baqillani affirms it but immediately explains what it means.
The answer defines anger and pleasure as will to reward or punish.
2
Identify the dichotomy
When asked for evidence, Baqillani presents a dichotomy: either anger and pleasure are will for benefit and harm, or they are passions (disposition changes). Understand these two options clearly.
The second option involves change of disposition (tab').
3
Understand why passions are impossible
Baqillani explains that passions imply change, pain, and dependence, which are impossible for the eternal, independent God. Reflect on why each characteristic is incompatible with divinity.
This safeguards divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في الرضا والغضب وأنهما من الإرادة. فإن قال قائل: فهل تقولون إنه تعالى غضبان راض وإنه موصوف بذلك؟ قيل له: أجل، وغضبه على من غضب عليه ورضاه عمن رضي عنه هما إرادته لإثابة المرضي عنه وعقوبة المغضوب عليه لا غير. فإن قال قائل: فما الدليل على أن غضب الباري جل وعز ورضاه ورحمته وسخطه هو إرادته لإثابة المرضي عنه ولمنفعة من رضي عنه وعقاب من غضب عليه وإيلامه وضرره؟ قيل له: الدليل على ذلك أن الغضب والرضا إما أن يكونا إرادة للنفع والضر، أو يكون الغضب الطبع ونفور النفس، والرضى السكون بعد تغير الطبع، ولا يجوز أن يكون الباري جل وعز ذا طبع يتغير وينفر ويسكن ولا ممن يألم ويرق من حيث ثبت قدمه وغناه عن اللذة وامتناع تألمه بشيء ينفر عنه ويتألم لإدراكه إذ ليست هذه الأشياء من جنسه وشكله أو مضادة له أو منافرة.
Chapter on pleasure and anger and that they are from will. If someone says: Do you say that He, exalted, is angry and pleased and that He is described with that? It is said to him: Yes, and His anger at whom He is angry and His pleasure with whom He is pleased are His will to reward the one pleased with and punish the one angered, nothing else. Then if someone says: What is the evidence that the anger of the Creator, majestic and mighty, and His pleasure, mercy, and displeasure are His will to reward the one pleased with and benefit the one He is pleased with, and punish the one He is angry with and cause him pain and harm? It is said to him: The evidence for that is that anger and pleasure are either will for benefit and harm, or anger is a natural disposition and aversion of the soul, and pleasure is composure after change of disposition. And it is impermissible that the Creator, majestic and mighty, possess a disposition that changes, turns away, and settles, nor that He be among those who feel pain and tenderness, because His eternity is established and His independence from pleasure, and the impossibility of His feeling pain from anything that turns Him away and causes Him pain upon perceiving it, since these things are not of His genus or form, nor opposite to Him, nor averse to Him.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a theological clarification regarding the attributes of anger (ghadab) and pleasure (rida) in God. It follows a double objection-answer structure. First, the objector asks if Muslims affirm that God is angry and pleased. The answer is yes, but these are interpreted as His will to reward or punish, not as passions. The second objection asks for evidence. The answer presents a dichotomy (qasama): anger and pleasure must either be (a) will for benefit and harm, or (b) passions involving a change in disposition (tab'). The latter is impossible for God because: (1) change of disposition implies temporality (hadath) and dependence on external stimuli, incompatible with God's eternity (qidam) and independence (ghina). (2) God cannot feel pain or tenderness because that requires similarity to created beings—things that cause aversion or pain are not of God's genus, form, nor opposite to Him. Therefore, the only plausible interpretation is that divine anger and pleasure are identical to His will to benefit or harm. The argument safeguards divine transcendence (tanzih) while affirming the scriptural terms.

Key Terms
غضب
رضا
إرادة
طبع
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming anger and pleasure in God implies He has human-like emotions and passions.
Correction: Baqillani explains that these attributes are His will to reward or punish, not passions, because passions involve change and dependence, which are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the evidence that God's anger and pleasure are will, not passions?
A) Passions are impossible for God because He is eternal and independent, and passions imply change.
B) Scripture says so.
C) Passions are only possible in humans.
D) Angels do not have passions.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that God's anger and pleasure are His will to reward or punish, not passions, thereby preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that God's anger is His will to punish, does it change how you view your own sins? Are you merely avoiding punishment, or are you truly seeking His pleasure as a reward?
?
If God's pleasure is His will to reward you, why do you often act as if you can earn His love through your deeds alone, forgetting that His pleasure is a gift of His will?
?
How does the truth that God does not feel emotional anger or sadness affect your relationship with Him? Do you relate to Him as a personal being who 'feels' toward you, or as the Transcendent Lord whose will determines your fate?

Proof that God is Willing (Mureed)
Baqillani proves God's will from the order and precedence of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temporal order of actions proves that God has a will.

Core Idea
The timing of events reveals a selecting will behind them.

Baqillani begins with an objection: what is the evidence that God is willing? He answers by pointing to the existence of actions and, crucially, their order—some precede, others follow. If there were no will, all actions would be simultaneous or non-existent, but their specific sequence points to a will that selects one time over another.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the answer does not just say 'actions exist' but specifically highlights their temporal order.
The objection expects a proof, and the answer provides a rational argument.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument: actions exist in a specific order (some precede, some delay). If God had no will, this order would not be determined—either all would happen simultaneously or none would exist. The actual order requires a selecting will.
3
Analyze the key conditional phrase
The phrase 'مع صحة تقدمه بدلاً من تأخره وتأخره بدلاً من تقدمه' (given the possibility of its precedence instead of its delay and its delay instead of its precedence) is crucial: it shows that the opposite order is equally possible, so the actual order must be chosen.
This eliminates necessity or chance as explanations.
4
Connect to divine attribute of will
Reflect on why this argument establishes God as 'Mureed' (Willing). The will is the attribute that specifies one possibility over another, and the temporal order is a clear example of such specification.
Will is not about creating actions but about choosing their timing and nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أنه مريد فإن قال قائل : فما الدليل على أنه مريد؟ قيل له : وجود الأفعال منه وتقدم بعضها على بعض في الوجود وتأخر بعضها عن بعض في الوجود فلولا أنه قصد إلى إيجاد ما أوجد منها لما وُجد ولا تقدم من ذلك ما تقدم ولا تأخر منه ما تأخر مع صحة تقدمه بدلاً من تأخره وتأخره بدلاً من تقدمه.
Chapter on that He is Willing. If an objector says: What is the evidence that He is willing? It is said to him: The existence of actions from Him, and the precedence of some over others in existence, and the delay of some after others in existence. For were it not that He intended to bring into existence what He brought into existence of them, they would not have existed, nor would what preceded have preceded, nor would what was delayed have been delayed, given the possibility of its precedence instead of its delay and its delay instead of its precedence.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a proof (objection and answer) for God being willing (mureed). It begins with the objection asking for evidence. The answer provides evidence from the existence of actions and their temporal order. The fact that some actions precede others and some are delayed indicates that there is a will (irada) that selects one time over another, since all times are equally possible. The argument is that if God did not will the specific timing, nothing would exist in that order; everything would be simultaneous or not exist. The phrase 'مع صحة تقدمه بدلاً من تأخره وتأخره بدلاً من تقدمه' emphasizes that the alternative ordering is equally possible, so the actual order must be due to will.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
تقدّم
تأخّر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the mere existence of actions proves God's will.
Correction: The proof specifically relies on the temporal order, not just existence. Existence alone could be necessary, but the order—given equally possible alternatives—requires a will.

Checkpoint
What is the evidence given by Baqillani that God is willing?
A) The existence of actions from Him and their temporal order.
B) The diversity of actions.
C) The perfection of actions.
D) The fact that actions are created.

Key Takeaway
The temporal order of actions, given the possibility of alternative order, proves a selecting will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Desire is Not an Attribute of God
Baqillani distinguishes between desire as will (acceptable) and desire as appetite (impossible).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'desire' (shahwa) cannot be applied to God, even if the intended meaning is His will.

Core Idea
Words matter: even a correct meaning cannot justify an incorrect name for God.

This passage tackles a subtle theological and linguistic issue. Someone might ask: does God have desire? The answer is not a simple yes or no. Imam al-Baqillani shows that while the concept of 'will' is affirmed for God, the term 'desire' is prohibited because it is not among His revealed names and carries implications of appetite and need, which are impossible for the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection, then a two-part answer.
The Arabic uses a conditional structure: 'if he intends... then...'.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'desire'
The answer distinguishes two meanings: (1) desire as will (irada) – correct in meaning but wrong wording; (2) desire as appetite – absolutely impossible. Understand each and why it matters.
3
Reflect on why wording matters
Even though 'will' is a divine attribute, calling it 'desire' is a mistake because it misleads and deviates from the community's usage. Ponder the importance of precise theological language.
This connects to the broader principle of tawqif (following revelation) in naming God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أنه لا يجوز عليه الشهوة فإن قال قائل : فهل تجوز عليه الشهوة؟ قيل له : إن أراد السائل بوصفه بالشهوة الإرادة لأفعاله فذلك صحيح في المعنى، غير أنه قد أخطأ وخالف الأمة في وصفه القديم بالشهوة إذ لم يكن ذلك من أوصافه وأسمائه وإن أراد بوصفه بالشهوة توقان النفس وميل الطبع إلى المنافع واللذات فذلك محال ممتنع عليه لما قد مر من قبل.
Issue concerning that desire is not permissible for Him. If an objector says: Is desire permissible for Him? It is said to him: If the questioner intends by describing Him with desire the will for His actions, then that is correct in meaning, except that he has made a mistake and opposed the community (umma) in describing the Eternal with desire, since that is not among His attributes and names. And if he intends by describing Him with desire the longing of the soul and inclination of temperament towards benefits and pleasures, then that is absurd and impossible for Him, based on what has preceded.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage deals with the term shahwa (desire). The objection asks whether desire is permissible for God. The answer distinguishes between two meanings: (1) desire as will (irada) – that is correct in meaning, but it is a mistake to use the term because it is not among God's established names and attributes; (2) desire as appetite (tawaqān al-nafs wa mayl al-tab‘) – that is impossible due to God's transcendence. The passage emphasizes linguistic and theological precision.

Key Terms
شهوة
إرادة
توقان النفس
ميل الطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if 'desire' means 'will', then it is permissible to call God 'desiring'.
Correction: Baqillani teaches that correct meaning does not justify wrong terminology; the term 'desire' is not among God's revealed names and using it violates divine honor and community consensus.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the ruling if someone uses the word 'desire' to mean God's will?
A) It is correct and allowed.
B) The meaning is correct but the wording is a mistake.
C) It is disbelief (kufr).
D) It is only allowed for prophets.

Key Takeaway
Even when the intended meaning is correct, using an improper name for God is forbidden because language and revelation matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described God with a term you knew was not from revelation, telling yourself 'I mean well'? How does this passage call you to repentance for such carelessness?
?
If even a correct meaning cannot justify an incorrect name for God, then what does that demand of your daily speech about Him? Will you commit to removing any casual or irreverent labels?
?
Do you treat the names and attributes of God as a matter of life and death, or as intellectual play? What will you change today so that your tongue glorifies Him with His own names?

The Eternity of God's Attributes: A Reductio Argument
Baqillani argues using reductio that God's attributes are eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's reductio argument for the eternity of God's attributes and why the passage is incomplete.

Core Idea
If God ever lacked an attribute, He would have its opposite—impossible for the Eternal.

This lesson presents a key argument from Imam al-Baqillani's Tamhid on the divine attributes. The passage responds to an objection about why God must be eternally described as Living, Knowing, etc. Using a reductio, Baqillani shows that if God lacked these attributes pre-eternally, He would have been described by their opposites—which is impossible. However, the manuscript breaks off mid-argument, so we will examine what is available and note the intended conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the objector's challenge: why must God be eternally Living, Knowing, etc., just as He is today?
The objection sets up the need for a proof.
2
Follow the reductio reasoning
Baqillani says: if God were not eternally Living, then He would have been dead—an impossibility for God.
This logical move extends to all attributes.
3
Consider the next step about essence vs. cause
The passage then asks whether God's opposite attributes (e.g., death) would be by essence or by an eternal cause.
If by essence, change is impossible; if by cause, that cause would be eternal.
4
Recognize the incomplete argument
The text cuts off mid-sentence. The intended conclusion is that God cannot acquire attributes later, so they must be eternal.
We will note the need for continuation.
5
Reflect on the lesson's limits
Understand that this is a fragment; the full proof requires the next page.
This highlights the challenge of manuscript transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أنه لم يزل حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً مريداً فإن قال قائل : ولِمَ زعمتم أن الصانع لم يزل حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً مريداً كما أنه اليوم موصوف بذلك؟ قيل له: لأنه لو كان، فيما لم يزل، غير حي ولا عالم ولا قادر ولا سميع بصير ولا متكلم ولا مريد لكان فتعالى لم يزل ميتاً عاجزاً أخرس ساكتاً فتعالى عن ذلك. ولو كان لم يزل موصوفاً بالموت الذي يضاد الحياة والعلم والقدرة لكان إنما يوصف بذلك لنفسه أو لعلة قديمة، ولو كان لنفسه كذلك لاستحال أن يحيا ما دامت
Chapter on that He has always been Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Willing. If an objector says: Why do you claim that the Maker has always been Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Willing, just as He is today described by that? It is said to him: Because if He, in what has not ceased, were not Living, nor Knowing, nor Powerful, nor Hearing, Seeing, nor Speaking, nor Willing, then He would – exalted is He – have not ceased being dead, incapable, mute, silent – exalted is He above that. And if He had not ceased being described by death which is contrary to life, knowledge, and power, then He would only be described by that by Himself or by an eternal cause. And if He were by Himself so, it would be impossible for Him to come to life as long as...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity of God's attributes. It starts with an objection asking why we believe God has always been described by these attributes. The answer uses a reductio: if He were not always Living, then He would have been dead (which is impossible). Similarly for the other attributes. The argument then considers the possibility of being described by death (or its opposite) by essence or by an eternal cause. If by essence, then it would be impossible to ever become alive (because essence is unchangeable). The text cuts off here, but the intended conclusion is that it is impossible for God to ever acquire attributes He previously lacked. So they must be eternal.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Baqillani argues that if God lacked an attribute eternally, He would have its opposite, which is impossible. Therefore, He must have always had them.

B) He created life.
C) The Quran says so.
D) Consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses a reductio argument to show that God must be eternally described by His attributes, because the alternative is impossible for the Divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that God's attributes are essential, not accidental. Is there any area of your faith where you treat God's mercy or justice as if they could be turned on and off? What would it mean to truly believe He is eternally and unchangeably just? Resolve to rely on His unchanging nature.
?
Consider the deepest fear you have. If God were capable of change, could He change His promise to you? How does the eternity of His attributes ground your trust in Him today? Let this truth transform your confidence in His faithfulness.

The Impossibility of Shahwa for God – Refining the Meaning
Baqillani answers an objection about attributing shahwa to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some meanings of 'desire' are impossible for God while a specific meaning (will) is acceptable in concept but not in wording.

Core Idea
Words matter, but meaning can be correct even when the term is inappropriate.

Al-Baqillani tackles a subtle objection: can we say that God has desire (shahwa)? The answer depends entirely on what we mean by 'desire.' This lesson unpacks his careful distinction between acceptable and unacceptable interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection and notice the questioner's phrasing. The objection assumes shahwa might be attributed to God.
2
Understand the two interpretations
Al-Baqillani splits the term 'shahwa' into two possible meanings: will (iraada) and instinctual appetite. Identify each.
3
See the ruling for each
The first meaning is correct in concept but wrong in terminology; the second is absolutely impossible. Note the basis for each ruling.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how al-Baqillani uses semantic clarification to defend divine transcendence without denying that God has will.
This is a common kalam technique: disambiguating terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أنه لا يجوز عليه الشهوة
فإن قال قائل : فهل تجوز عليه الشهوة؟ قيل له : إن أراد السائل بوصفه بالشهوة الإرادة لأفعاله فذلك صحيح في المعنى، غير أنه قد أخطأ وخالف الأمة في وصفه القديم بالشهوة إذ لم يكن ذلك من أوصافه وأسمائه وإن بوصفه بالشهوة توقان النفس وميل الطبع إلى المنافع واللذات فذلك محال ممتنع عليه لما قد مر من قبل.
A question that it is not permissible to attribute desire (shahwa) to Him. If someone says: Is it permissible to attribute desire to Him? It is said to him: If the questioner intends by describing Him with desire the will for His actions, then that is correct in meaning, except that he has erred and opposed the community (umma) in describing the Eternal with desire, since that is not among His descriptions and names. But if he describes Him with desire as the yearning of the soul and the inclination of nature towards benefits and pleasures, then that is impossible and impermissible for Him, due to what has preceded.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the attribution of shahwa (desire) to God. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, presents an objection: can desire be attributed to God? He then distinguishes two possible meanings. If by 'desire' one means the will (iraada) to perform actions, then this is correct in meaning, but it is a mistake in wording because the term 'desire' is not among God's names and attributes as established by revelation. However, if one means the instinctual craving and natural inclination towards pleasures, that is impossible for God because He is transcendent above such bodily appetites, as previously established. Thus, the objection is answered by clarifying the equivocation in the term 'shahwa'.

Key Terms
شهوة (shahwa: desire/appetite)
إرادة (iraada: will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributing desire to God is always blasphemous.
Correction: Al-Baqillani shows that if 'desire' means will for actions, it is acceptable in meaning, though the term is not a proper divine attribute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani, when is it acceptable to describe God with shahwa?
A) When it means instinctual appetite
B) When it means will for actions
C) Never
D) When it means anger

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani resolves the objection by showing that shahwa cannot mean instinctual appetite for God, but if it means will, the concept is acceptable though the term is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

God's Eternal Life: The Argument from Impossibility of Change
Baqillani proves God's eternal life via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that God must be eternally living, because if He were not, He would be eternally dead—an impossibility.

Core Idea
God's eternal life is proven by the contradiction of its opposite.

This passage presents a foundational proof in Islamic theology for God's eternal attributes. Baqillani uses a reductio ad absurdum: if God were not eternally living, He would have the opposite attribute eternally, which cannot change. This establishes His eternal life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure.
The passage begins with an objection to be refuted.
2
Identify the assumption and conclusion
The assumption is that God might not be eternally living. The conclusion is that He must be eternally living.
Follow the logical flow.
3
Understand the two possibilities
Baqillani argues that if God were eternally dead, that death would be either by His essence or by an eternal cause. Both make change impossible.
Note the key terms: 'li-nafsihi' and 'li-'illatin qadimah'.
4
Connect to today's evidence
The proof concludes that because we see evidence of life today, God must have always been alive.
This links the eternal attribute to observable effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في أنه لم يزل حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً مريداً
فإن قال قائل : ولِمَ زعمتم أن الصانع لم يزل حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً مريداً كما أنه اليوم موصوف بذلك؟ قيل له: لأنه لو كان، فيما لم يزل، غير حي ولا عالم ولا قادر ولا سميع بصير ولا متكلم ولا مريد لكان فتعالى لم يزل ميتاً عاجزاً أخرس ساكتاً فتعالى عن ذلك. ولو كان لم يزل موصوفاً بالموت الذي يضاد الحياة والعلم والقدرة لكان إنما يوصف بذلك لنفسه أو لعلة قديمة، ولو كان لنفسه كذلك لاستحال أن يحيا ما دامت نفسه كائنة؛ وكذلك لو كان على ما ذكر السائل لعلة قديمة لاستحال أن يحيا اليوم لاستحالة عدم موته القديم، لأن القديم لا يجوز عدمه وإذا استحال ذلك استحال أن يفعل ويوجد منه ما يدل على أنه اليوم حي وفي صحة ذلك منه ووجوده دليل على أنه لم يزل حياً؛ وكذلك لو كان لم يزل حياً، وهو غير متكلم ولا سميع ولا بصير ولا مريد ولا عالم ولا قادر لوجب أن يوصف بأضداد هذه الصفات في أزله من الخرس والسكوت والصمم والعمى والاستكراه والسهو والجهل والعجز فتعالى عن ذلك أجمع. ولو كان لم يزل موصوفاً بهذه الأوصاف لنفسه أو لمعنى قديم لاستحال خروجه عنها ووصفه بضدها لاستحالة عدم القديم ولوجب أن يكون في وقتنا هذا غير حي ولا عالم ولا قادر ولا سميع ولا بصير خلاف إجماع المسلمين.
Chapter that He is eternally living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking, willing. If someone says: Why do you claim that the Maker is eternally living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking, willing as He is described today? It is said to him: Because if He were, in pre-eternity, not living, not knowing, not powerful, not hearing, not seeing, not speaking, not willing, then He would be—exalted is He above that—eternally dead, incapable, mute, silent—exalted is He above that. And if He were eternally described with death which opposes life, knowledge, and power, then He would be described with that either by Himself or by an eternal cause. If by Himself, then it would be impossible for Him to ever become alive as long as His essence exists. Similarly, if by an eternal cause as the questioner supposed, it would be impossible for Him to become alive today because it would necessitate the non-existence of His eternal death, and the eternal cannot cease to exist. If that is impossible, then it is impossible for Him to act and produce what indicates that He is alive today. And the soundness of that from Him and His existence are proof that He has eternally been alive. Similarly, if He were eternally alive but not speaking, not hearing, not seeing, not willing, not knowing, not powerful, then He would necessarily be described in His eternity with the opposites of these attributes: muteness, silence, deafness, blindness, coercion, heedlessness[?], ignorance, and incapacity—exalted is He above all that. And if He were eternally described with these attributes by Himself or by an eternal meaning, then it would be impossible for Him to depart from them and be described with their opposites, due to the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal. And He would necessarily be, in our time, not living, not knowing, not powerful, not hearing, not seeing, contrary to the consensus of the Muslims.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is eternally described with seven attributes: life, knowledge, power, hearing, seeing, speech, and will. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum. If God were not eternally living, He would have been eternally dead, which is impossible. The same applies to the other attributes. The argument proceeds in two main parts: first, if God lacked an attribute, He would have its opposite eternally. Second, if He were described with that opposite eternally (like death), then whether that was by His essence or by an eternal cause, it would be impossible for Him to ever acquire the attribute (like life) because the eternal opposite cannot cease. Therefore, He must be eternally described with these attributes. The proof concludes that the fact that we see evidence of these attributes today indicates that He has always had them. This is a standard kalam argument based on the impossibility of change in the eternal essence.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim: eternal)
صانع (sani': Maker)
أضداد (addad: opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God could acquire attributes over time.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if God lacked an attribute eternally, He would have its opposite eternally, which cannot change, so He must have always had it.

Checkpoint
What does al-Baqillani argue would follow if God was not eternally living?
A) He would be eternally unable to create
B) He would be eternally dead
C) He would have acquired life later
D) He would be non-existent

Key Takeaway
God must be eternally living because if He were not, He would be eternally dead, which is impossible, and the evidence of life today confirms His eternal life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believed that God has been perfectly alive and aware of you from all eternity, would you dare to live as if He is not watching? What one action today would reflect that belief?
?
The proof shows that God cannot change from dead to alive. How does the unchanging nature of God's life challenge your tendency to take breaks from worship or to assume He might 'look away' from your sins?
?
If God's eternal life is proven by the impossibility of its opposite, what opposite qualities are you allowing to persist in your own soul that contradict the life of faith? Will you confront them today?

Applying the Proof to All Divine Attributes
Baqillani extends the reductio to God's other six attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani extends the same argument to prove that God is eternally knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking, and willing.

Core Idea
The same reasoning used for life applies to all of God's attributes.

Having established the method for life, Baqillani now applies it to the other six attributes. He shows that if God were lacking any of them, He would have their opposites, which is impossible. This unified argument demonstrates that all divine attributes are eternal.

Guided Path
1
Recall the previous argument
Briefly review how Baqillani proved God's eternal life. The same structure applies here.
Look for parallelism in the text.
2
Identify the list of attributes
The passage mentions six additional attributes: speech, hearing, seeing, will, knowledge, and power. Note their opposites.
Opposites: muteness, silence, deafness, blindness, coercion, heedlessness, ignorance, incapacity.
3
Apply the reductio to each
For each attribute, if God lacked it eternally, He would have its opposite eternally. Then the same two possibilities arise: by essence or by eternal cause, both making change impossible.
The text compresses the argument for brevity.
4
Note the final appeal to consensus
Baqillani concludes that if the opposite were true, it would contradict the consensus of Muslims that God is described with these attributes today.
Consensus (ijma') is a strong proof in kalam.

5 min
The Eternity of God's Attributes
Baqillani proves God's eternal attributes via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be eternally described with all attributes of perfection, and why lacking any attribute would imply an impossible defect.

Core Idea
God's attributes are co-eternal with His essence; their absence would mean eternal defects.

Baqillani presents a powerful logical argument to show that God cannot acquire attributes over time or lack any attribute. He uses reductio ad absurdum, proving that if God were not eternally knowing, powerful, etc., then He would have to be described with their opposites (ignorance, weakness) in pre-eternity, which is impossible. This passage establishes the foundation for understanding God's necessary perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure emerge naturally.
Notice the three conditional scenarios: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the three conditional arguments
The passage contains three separate reductio arguments: 1) if attributes were caused, God could not be alive today; 2) if God were living but lacked other attributes, He would have their opposites in pre-eternity; 3) if He were eternally defective, He could never change, contradicting Islamic consensus.
Each argument hinges on the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist.
3
Understand the key logical principle
Baqillani repeatedly uses the axiom 'the eternal cannot cease to exist' (القديم لا يجوز عدمه). This principle is central to proving that God's attributes must be eternal.
Contrast this with created things, which are contingent and can change.
4
Connect to Islamic consensus
The final argument concludes that if God were eternally defective, He would remain defective now, which contradicts the unanimous belief of Muslims that God is perfect.
Consensus (إجماع) is used as a source of theological certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفسه كائنة ; وكذلك لو كان على ما ذكر السائل لعلة قديمة لاستحال أن يحيا اليوم لاستحالة عدم مَوْتِهِ القديم، لأن القديم لا يجوز عَدَمُه وإذا استحال ذلك استحال أن يفعل ويُوجد منه ما يدل على أنه اليوم حي وفي صحة ذلك منه ووجوده دليل على أنه لم يزل حياً؛ وكذلك لو كان لم يزل حياً، وهو غير متكلم ولا سميع ولا بصير ولا مريد ولا عالم ولا قادر لوجب أن يوصف بأضداد هذه الصفات في أزله من الخرس والسكوت والصمم والعمى والاستكراه والسهو والجهل والعجز فتعالى عن ذلك أجمع . ولو كان لم يزل موصوفاً بهذه الأوصاف لنفسه أو لمعنى قديم لاستحال خروجه عنها ووصفه بضدّها لاستحالة عدم القديم ولوجب أن يكون في وقتنا هذا غير حَيّ ولا عالم ولا قادر ولا سميع ولا بصير خلاف إجماع المسلمين .
His self is existing; and likewise if it were as the questioner mentioned for an eternal cause, it would be impossible for him to be alive today due to the impossibility of the non-existence of his eternal death, because the eternal cannot cease to exist. And if that is impossible, it is impossible for him to act and produce from himself what indicates that he is living today, and in the correctness of that from him and his existence is evidence that he has not ceased to be living. And likewise, if he had always been living, while he is not speaking, nor hearing, nor seeing, nor willing, nor knowing, nor powerful, it would be necessary that he be described with the opposites of these attributes in his pre-eternity: dumbness, silence, deafness, blindness, coercion, forgetfulness, ignorance, and incapacity. Exalted is He above all of that. And if he had always been described with these attributes by Himself or by an eternal meaning, it would be impossible for him to depart from them and be described with their opposites, due to the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal, and it would be necessary that he be in our present time not living, nor knowing, nor powerful, nor hearing, nor seeing, contrary to the consensus of the Muslims.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof by reductio ad absurdum. Baqillani argues that if God were not eternally living, knowing, powerful, etc., then He would have to be described with the opposites of those attributes (dead, ignorant, weak, etc.) in pre-eternity, which is impossible. He begins with a conditional: if the questioner's premise (that God's attributes are caused) were true, then God could not be alive today because His eternal death would never cease. Since the eternal cannot cease, He could not act to show He is alive. Then Baqillani argues that if God were eternally living but lacked other attributes (like speech, hearing, etc.), He would have to have their opposites (dumbness, deafness, etc.) in eternity, which is impossible for God. Lastly, if God were eternally described with these defective attributes by Himself or an eternal cause, He could never change to their opposites, so He would remain defective now, contradicting Islamic consensus. Thus God must be eternally described with all perfect attributes.

عدم
صفات
أضداد
خرس
سهو
جهل
عجز
سكوت
صمم
عمى
استكراه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are accidental or acquired over time.
Correction: Baqillani shows that attributes are co-eternal with God's essence; otherwise He would have had eternal defects, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What would necessarily be true if God were not eternally described with all attributes of perfection?
A) He would still be perfect by essence
B) He would have to be described with their opposites in pre-eternity
C) He would acquire attributes later
D) He would be non-existent

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are eternal and intrinsic to His essence; their absence would imply necessary defects, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is eternal and never absent, is there any area of your life where you live as if God is unaware? How would you honestly change your private habits knowing that His knowledge has never ceased and never will?
?
Baqillani argues that lacking an attribute from eternity would force God to have its opposite. Do you ever attribute to God a temporary lapse—like 'God is not concerned with me right now'? How does the eternity of His hearing and sight challenge that assumption?
?
The proof concludes that God cannot be otherwise than perfect. When you complain about your circumstances, do you implicitly accuse God of lacking wisdom or power? What would it take for you to truly submit to His eternal attributes in your daily struggles?
