God's Actions Are Not Driven by Need
Baqillani argues against comparing God's actions to human actions based on need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that God cannot be compared to human agents in terms of motivation for action, and how this distinction defends divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God acts without need; human actions are driven by need.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani tackles a common objection: if God's actions seem purposeless, does that make them arbitrary? He responds by drawing a sharp distinction between human agents, who are needy and obligated, and the Eternal God, who is self-sufficient and beyond command. The lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get a feel for Baqillani's reasoning without analyzing deeply.
2
Identify the human analogy
Notice how Baqillani describes human agents: they act for benefit or harm avoidance. If they claim to act without purpose, they are deemed foolish.
This is key to the contrast.
3
Contrast with God
See how Baqillani states that God is not needy and not commanded, so the analogy fails. Reflect on the implications for divine attributes.
This prevents anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه صفته خطور الأمر بباله كالذي لم يكن عالماً به ولا أن تبعثه الدواعي والبواعث على أفعاله ؛ وذلك انا إذا قلنا لفاعلنا : ما الذي دعاك إلى الفعل وحرّكك على إيقاعه وما الغرض فيه دون غيره وفي فعله دون تركه والانصراف عنه ؟ فإنما نأله ليخبرنا أقصد بذلك اجتلابَ منفعة أو دفع مضرة أم لا ؛ فإن قال : فَعَلَه لا لاجتلاب منفعة ولا لدفع مَضَرَّة مع علمه بوقوعه وجب القضاء على تسفيهه لأنه ممن يحتاج إلى جرّ المنافع ودفع المضار وهو مأمور بذلك وإيقاعه الفعل عارياً من القصد إلى ذلك والتصدي له سَفَه وخلافٌ لما وجب عليه والقديم تعالى ليس بذي حاجة ولا ممن يلزمه الانقياد والطاعة ؛ فلم يجز أن يقاس على فاعلنا .
This is His attribute: the occurrence of the matter in his mind is like one who was not knowing of it, nor do motives and incentives impel him to his actions. That is because when we say to our agent: 'What invited you to the action and moved you to perform it, and what is the purpose in it rather than other than it, and in doing it rather than leaving it and turning away from it?' — we only ask him to inform us whether he intended thereby to attract a benefit or repel a harm, or not. If he says: 'I did it neither to attract a benefit nor to repel a harm, while knowing it would occur', then judgment of his foolishness is necessary, because he is one who needs to draw benefits and repel harms, and he is commanded to do so; and his performing the action devoid of intention for that and striving for it is foolishness and contrary to what was obligatory upon him. But the Eternal, exalted, is not in need, nor is He one to whom submission and obedience are incumbent; so it is not permissible to compare Him to our agent.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an argument demonstrating the difference between God and human agents regarding motivation. The author begins by describing the attribute of the divine agent: the occurrence of an action in His mind is not like a human who is initially unaware and then motivated by incentives. He then explains by analogy with human agents: when we ask a human why he performed an action, he must cite a purpose such as gaining benefit or avoiding harm. If he claims to have acted without any such purpose despite knowing the outcome, we judge him as foolish (safīh), because humans are needy and commanded to seek benefit and avoid harm. Acting without purpose contradicts his obligations. However, God (al-Qadīm, the Eternal) is not needy and is not subject to command or obligation; therefore, He cannot be measured against human standards. The conclusion is that God's actions are not necessarily tied to purposes of benefit or harm, unlike human actions.

دواعي (dawāʿī)
بواعث (bawāʿith)
منفعة (manfaʿa)
مضرة (maḍarra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's actions have no purpose (benefit/harm), they are arbitrary and meaningless.
Correction: Baqillani only denies that God acts to gain benefit or avoid harm, not that His actions lack wisdom. God's actions are wise but not driven by need.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why is it invalid to compare God's actions to human actions?
A) Because God's actions are always for a benefit
B) Because humans are needy and commanded to seek benefit/harm, while God is not
C) Because humans can never act without purpose
D) Because God's actions are beyond human understanding

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency means His actions are not driven by need, so comparing Him to human agents is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you questioned God's wisdom in a trial you faced. Did you assume that His action must be for a specific benefit or harm avoidance, as you would? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to His transcendence without needing to understand the 'why'? What one concrete change can you make today in how you respond to life's hardships?
?
The passage highlights that humans are commanded to seek benefit and avoid harm. In what area of your life are you so focused on maximizing benefit or minimizing discomfort that you forget God is not bound by such calculations? How does that self-focus affect your trust in His plan? Commit to one specific act of reliance on God this week that sets aside your own calculation.
?
If God's actions are not driven by need, then your existence and your trials are not because God 'needs' anything from you. How does that reframe your relationship with Him? Are you trying to 'give' God something He doesn't need? What would it look like to worship Him purely out of gratitude and love, rather than as a transaction?

God Does Not Act for a Cause: The Direct Answer
Baqillani gives the direct answer to the cause question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani denies that God created the world for a cause, based on the nature of causes as bringing benefit or repelling harm.

Core Idea
Causes imply need; God is beyond need.

Baqillani begins by addressing a direct question: Did God create the world for a cause? The answer is a firm no. He explains that causes are only relevant to beings that seek benefit or avoid harm—neither of which applies to Allah. This sets the stage for a deeper rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the structure: an objector asks a question, and the author responds directly.
The objector represents a common theological position that God acts for a purpose.
2
Understand the definition of cause
Baqillani says causes are confined to drawing benefits and repelling harms. Consider why this definition excludes God.
In Islamic theology, God is perfect and self-sufficient; He does not seek benefit or fear harm.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: If God acted for a cause, would that imply a need? How does this preserve divine transcendence?
This connects to the broader principle that God's actions are not motivated by external factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أن القديم لم يفعل العالم لعلة. فإن قال قائل: فهل تقولون إن القديم فعل العالم لعلة أوجبت حدوثه منه؟ قيل له : لا لأن العلل لا تجوز عليه لأنها مقصورة على جرّ المنافع ودفع المضارّ ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه لو كان تعالى فاعلاً للعالم لعلة أوجبت له لم تخل تلك العلة أن تكون قديمة أو مُحدّثة، فإن كانت قديمةً لوجب قدمُ العالمَ لِقِدَم علّته وألا يكون بين العلة القديمة وبين وجود العالم إلا مقدار زمان الإيجاد وذلك يوجب حدوث القديم لأن ما لم يكن قبل المُحْدَث إلا بزمان أو أزمنة محدودة وجب حدوثه لأن فائدة توقيت وجود الشيء هو أنه كان معدوماً قبل تلك الحال، فلما لم يَجُز حدوث القديم لم يَجُز أن يكون العالم مُحْدَثاً لعلة قديمة، وإن كانت تلك العلة محدثة فلا يخلو محدثها أن يكون أحدثها لعلّة أو لا لعلة فإن كانت محدّثة لعلة وعلتها أيضاً مُحْدَثة وجب أن تكون علة العلة مُحْدَثة لعلة أخرى وكذلك أبداً إلى غير غاية وذلك يُحيل وجود العالم جملةً لتعلقه بما يستحيل فعله وخروجه إلى الوجود، وإن كانت العلة والخاطر والداعي والباعث والمحرك محدثةً لا لعلة وكانت بالوجود لما وجدت من فاعلها أولى منها بالعدم لا لعلة . وكان فاعلها حكيماً غير سفيه جاز حدوث سائر الحوادث منه لا لعلة وكان حكيماً غير سفيه ولم يكن خروجه عن السفه بإحداث مُحدث واحد لا لعلة أولى من خروجه عنه بإحداث جميع الحوادث لا لعلة وهذا يبطل ما توهموه إيطالاً ظاهراً .
Issue: That the Eternal did not create the world for a cause. If someone says: 'Do you say that the Eternal created the world for a cause that necessitated its origination from Him?' It is said to him: 'No, because causes are not permissible for Him, since they are confined to drawing benefits and repelling harms.' This is also indicated by the fact that if He, exalted, were an agent of the world for a cause that necessitated it for Him, that cause would not escape being either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the eternity of the world would be necessary due to the eternity of its cause, and there would not be between the eternal cause and the existence of the world except the amount of time of bringing into existence, and that necessitates the origination of the eternal, because whatever did not exist before the originated except with a time or limited times must be originated, since the benefit of timing the existence of a thing is that it was non-existent before that state. So when the origination of the eternal is not permissible, it is not permissible that the world be originated for an eternal cause. And if that cause were originated, then its originator would not escape being either that he originated it for a cause or not for a cause. If it were originated for a cause, and its cause is also originated, then it would be necessary that the cause of the cause be originated for another cause, and so on endlessly, and that renders the existence of the world entirely impossible because it is attached to what is impossible to do and to come into existence. And if the cause, thought, motive, incentive, and mover were originated not for a cause, and they existed from their agent with existence being more appropriate for them than non-existence not for a cause, and their agent was wise (ḥakīm) not foolish (safīh), then the occurrence of all other events from Him without a cause would be permissible, and He would be wise not foolish, and His departure from foolishness by bringing about one single event without a cause would not be more appropriate than His departure from it by bringing about all events without a cause. And this nullifies what they imagined as an apparent refutation.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a detailed answer concerning whether God created the world for a cause ('illa). The objector asks if Baqillani's school holds that the Eternal created the world for a cause that necessitated its creation. The answer is no, because causes (al-‘ilal) are only applicable to beings that seek benefit or avoid harm, which does not apply to God. Then a deeper argument is given: suppose God did act for a cause; that cause must be either eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath). If eternal, then the world would be eternal because of the eternity of its cause, and the temporal gap between cause and effect would imply the origination of the eternal—a contradiction. If the cause is originated, then its own originator must have acted either for a cause or not. If for a cause, there is an infinite regress, making the world's existence impossible. If not for a cause, then it is possible that the originator acted without a cause and is still wise; by the same token, God can create all events without a cause and still be wise. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
علة ('illa)
قديم (qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying a cause means God's actions are arbitrary and without wisdom.
Correction: Baqillani is only denying that God acts out of need, not that His actions lack wisdom. Wisdom and causality (in the sense of need) are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why are causes not permissible for God?
A) Because God does not act at all
B) Because causes are confined to drawing benefits and repelling harms
C) Because causes are only for humans
D) Because causes require time

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not caused by need for benefit or harm; He is completely self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you assume that God's purpose is to harm you or to benefit you in a way that reduces His transcendence? How does knowing that God does not act for causes (benefit/harm) change your reaction to suffering? Will you trust Him without needing to see the benefit?
?
If God does not need your obedience, why do you obey? Is it out of genuine love or to gain something? Let this lesson strip away any transactional view of your relationship with God. Will you worship Him for who He is, not for what you get?
?
Are you living as if God is compelled by your actions—that your good deeds force His mercy or your sins force His punishment? Embrace the freedom of God's absolute sovereignty: He acts without need. Does that make you feel small or liberated? How will you realign your heart to His perfect freedom?

The Impossibility of a Cause for Creation
Baqillani presents a disjunctive argument against creation for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's disjunctive argument showing that positing a cause for the world leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If creation had a cause, it must be impossible.

Baqillani strengthens his case with a logical argument. He considers the hypothetical that God did act for a cause. That cause must be either eternal or originated. Both options lead to contradictions, proving that the original position—no cause—is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two possibilities
Baqillani presents a disjunction: if there were a cause, it must be either eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath). Identify these two branches.
This is a classic kalam argument form called taqsīm (division).
2
Explore the eternal cause scenario
If the cause were eternal, the world would be eternal because an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect. But the world is originated, so this is impossible.
Note the reasoning: no temporal gap between an eternal cause and its effect except the moment of bringing into existence, which implies the effect is also eternal.
3
Explore the originated cause scenario
If the cause is originated, then its own originator either acted for a cause or not. If for a cause, we get an infinite regress (tasalsul), making the world's existence impossible. If not for a cause, then it is possible for the originator to act without a cause and still be wise; by analogy, God can create all things without a cause.
This branch shows that even if an originated cause exists, it leads either to impossibility or to the very position Baqillani defends.
4
See the conclusion
Since both branches are absurd or lead to the same conclusion, the original position—that God created without a cause—is vindicated.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of the objector's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في أن القديم لم يفعل العالم لعلة. فإن قال قائل: فهل تقولون إن القديم فعل العالم لعلة أوجبت حدوثه منه؟ قيل له : لا لأن العلل لا تجوز عليه لأنها مقصورة على جرّ المنافع ودفع المضارّ ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه لو كان تعالى فاعلاً للعالم لعلة أوجبت له لم تخل تلك العلة أن تكون قديمة أو مُحدّثة، فإن كانت قديمةً لوجب قدمُ العالمَ لِقِدَم علّته وألا يكون بين العلة القديمة وبين وجود العالم إلا مقدار زمان الإيجاد وذلك يوجب حدوث القديم لأن ما لم يكن قبل المُحْدَث إلا بزمان أو أزمنة محدودة وجب حدوثه لأن فائدة توقيت وجود الشيء هو أنه كان معدوماً قبل تلك الحال، فلما لم يَجُز حدوث القديم لم يَجُز أن يكون العالم مُحْدَثاً لعلة قديمة، وإن كانت تلك العلة محدثة فلا يخلو محدثها أن يكون أحدثها لعلّة أو لا لعلة فإن كانت محدّثة لعلة وعلتها أيضاً مُحْدَثة وجب أن تكون علة العلة مُحْدَثة لعلة أخرى وكذلك أبداً إلى غير غاية وذلك يُحيل وجود العالم جملةً لتعلقه بما يستحيل فعله وخروجه إلى الوجود، وإن كانت العلة والخاطر والداعي والباعث والمحرك محدثةً لا لعلة وكانت بالوجود لما وجدت من فاعلها أولى منها بالعدم لا لعلة . وكان فاعلها حكيماً غير سفيه جاز حدوث سائر الحوادث منه لا لعلة وكان حكيماً غير سفيه ولم يكن خروجه عن السفه بإحداث مُحدث واحد لا لعلة أولى من خروجه عنه بإحداث جميع الحوادث لا لعلة وهذا يبطل ما توهموه إيطالاً ظاهراً .
Issue: That the Eternal did not create the world for a cause. If someone says: 'Do you say that the Eternal created the world for a cause that necessitated its origination from Him?' It is said to him: 'No, because causes are not permissible for Him, since they are confined to drawing benefits and repelling harms.' This is also indicated by the fact that if He, exalted, were an agent of the world for a cause that necessitated it for Him, that cause would not escape being either eternal or originated. If it were eternal, then the eternity of the world would be necessary due to the eternity of its cause, and there would not be between the eternal cause and the existence of the world except the amount of time of bringing into existence, and that necessitates the origination of the eternal, because whatever did not exist before the originated except with a time or limited times must be originated, since the benefit of timing the existence of a thing is that it was non-existent before that state. So when the origination of the eternal is not permissible, it is not permissible that the world be originated for an eternal cause. And if that cause were originated, then its originator would not escape being either that he originated it for a cause or not for a cause. If it were originated for a cause, and its cause is also originated, then it would be necessary that the cause of the cause be originated for another cause, and so on endlessly, and that renders the existence of the world entirely impossible because it is attached to what is impossible to do and to come into existence. Then a deeper argument is given: suppose God did act for a cause; that cause must be either eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath). If eternal, then the world would be eternal because of the eternity of its cause, and the temporal gap between cause and effect would imply the origination of the eternal—a contradiction. If the cause is originated, then its own originator must have acted either for a cause or not. If for a cause, there is an infinite regress, making the world's existence impossible. If not for a cause, then it is possible that the originator acted without a cause and is still wise; by the same token, God can create all events without a cause and still be wise. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
قديم (qadīm)
محدث (muḥdath)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
حكيم (ḥakīm)
سفيه (safīh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's action has no cause, it is arbitrary and He would be foolish.
Correction: Baqillani shows that an agent can act without a cause and still be wise, as demonstrated by the possibility of an originated cause without a cause from a wise agent.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the consequence if the cause for the world's creation were eternal?
A) The world would be eternal
B) The world would be non-existent
C) The cause would be originated
D) God would be foolish

Key Takeaway
Any attempt to posit a cause for creation leads to an impossible consequence, affirming that God acts without cause in the sense of need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself expecting God to explain His actions to you, as if He must have a cause or reason that you can understand? How does Baqillani's proof challenge that expectation, and what would it mean for you to truly trust that God is wise even when you see no reason?
?
If God's actions are not caused by anything external, then every moment of your existence is a direct, uncaused act of His will. How should this change the way you approach your daily life—your successes, your trials, even your next breath? Are you living as if you are dependent on His free choice every second?
?
The argument contrasts being wise (ḥakīm) with being foolish (safīh). In what areas of your life are you tempted to think that acting without an external cause is foolish? Are you willing to imitate the divine attribute of wisdom by making choices that are not compelled by circumstances but are simply rooted in truth and goodness?

Disanalogy Between Divine and Human Action
Baqillani responds to the objection that a wise agent always acts with a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine action cannot be analogized to human action based on the criterion of benefit and harm.

Core Idea
God's actions are not caused by benefit or harm, unlike human actions.

This passage addresses a common objection: if a wise agent always acts for a reason, then God must also act for a reason. Baqillani responds by distinguishing the basis of human action (benefit/harm) from divine action, which transcends such considerations. Understanding this disanalogy is crucial for a correct theology of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection, then Baqillani's answer, then a second objection, and a final response.
Pay attention to the key terms introduced.
2
Identify the two objections
The objector first asks if a wise agent can act without a cause. Then, when told that humans can under certain conditions, the objector tries to analogize to God. Identify both objections clearly.
3
Understand Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani refutes the analogy by pointing out that humans act for benefit or harm, whereas God is beyond both. This is the core disanalogy.
Reflect on why benefit and harm cannot apply to God.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this principle safeguards divine transcendence. The lesson reinforces that God's actions are not bound by human limitations.
This is a foundational concept in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : فهل وجدتم فاعلاً حكيماً يفعل الفعل لا لعلة مع العلم والذكر؟ قيل له : لا إذا صح أن يوقعه على وجه يصح انتفاعه به أو دفع الضرّ عنه ، فإن قال : فيجب حمل أمر القديم في فعله على حال فاعلنا ؛ قيل له : لا يجب ذلك لافتراقهما في علة جواز النفع والضرر عليهما وقد مرّ ذلك بما يغني عن ردّه .
If someone says: 'Have you found a wise agent who does an act without a cause while being knowing and mindful?' It is said to him: 'No, [not] if it is valid that he performs it in a way that he benefits from it or repels harm from himself.' Then if he says: 'Then the matter of the Eternal must be judged in His act according to the state of our agent.' It is said to him: 'That is not necessary because of the difference between them in the basis for the possibility of benefit and harm upon them, and that has already been discussed sufficiently.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that a wise agent always acts for a reason, so God must also. The answer distinguishes: humans act for benefit or to avoid harm, but God is beyond benefit and harm. Therefore the analogy fails, and the point has already been covered.

Key Terms

علة
قديم
نفع
ضرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God's actions must be analogous to human actions in every respect.
Correction: God is beyond benefit and harm, so human analogies based on these motives do not apply.

Checkpoint

C) Because the Eternal's acts are uncaused.
D) Because the objector is mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Divine action is not analogous to human action because God is beyond benefit and harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Eternal Power and Temporal Creation
Baqillani answers the objection that eternal power entails eternal creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why eternal divine power does not necessitate an eternal world, and how Baqillani distinguishes divine agency from natural causation.

Core Idea
Eternal power does not force eternal creation; God chooses when to act.

After establishing arguments for the temporal creation of the world, Baqillani anticipates a key objection: if God's power is eternal, why isn't the world eternal as well? He responds by clarifying that divine power is not like a natural cause, and that God's power does not require the act to exist simultaneously. In this passage, he presents two decisive distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage along with the translation. Notice how the objection is presented and how Baqillani introduces his two-part answer.
The objection claims that eternal power must produce eternal effects, just as a natural cause does.
2
Identify the first distinction
The first reason states that power does not require the act to exist at the same time. God can create acts in time even though His power is eternal.
Key phrase: 'أنه قادر على أن يستأنف الأفعال'
3
Identify the second distinction
The second reason emphasizes that divine power is not a 'cause' (علة) that necessitates its effect, unlike natural forces. God acts by choice, not by necessity.
Compare with the idea of طبع (nature) that inevitably produces its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : هذا يلزمكم في قولكم إن صانع العالم لم يزل قادرا على إيجاده لأن قدرته على الإيجاد قديمة، قلنا لا يجب ذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أننا نحن لا نزعم أن القديم سبحانه قادر بقدرة في الأزل على أن تكون الأفعال مع القدرة وإنما نقول : إنه قادر على أن يستأنف الأفعال وعلى أن يحدثها في زمان قد كانت قبله معدومة؛ ومحال أن تكون قدرة على ما لم يكن معدوما قبل وجوده؛ فلم يجب قدم الأفعال لقدم القدرة عليها.

والأمر الآخر أننا نحن لا نزعم أن قدرة القديم سبحانه علة للأفعال ولا موجبة لها حسب ما تقولونه أنتم في إيجاب الطبع لحدث عنه وكونه علة له ووجوب كونه عنه، ولا نحيل أن توجد قدرة القديم في الأزل وهو غير
So if they say: This [argument] applies to you in your statement that the maker of the world has never ceased to be powerful over its creation, because His power over creation is eternal. We say: That is not necessary for two reasons: One of them is that we do not claim that the Eternal, may He be exalted, is powerful by a power in pre-eternity such that acts [must] coexist with the power; rather we say: He is powerful to initiate acts and to bring them about in a time in which they were previously non-existent. It is impossible for a power to be over what was non-existent before its existence. So the eternity of acts is not necessitated by the eternity of power over them.

The other matter is that we do not claim that the power of the Eternal, may He be exalted, is a cause for acts nor does it necessitate them, as you claim regarding the necessitation of the nature for what occurs from it and its being a cause for it and the necessity of its occurrence from it. And we do not deem impossible that the power of the Eternal exists in pre-eternity while He is not...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if God's power is eternal, then the same argument used against the nature (an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect) would apply to God as well. Baqillani provides two clear distinctions. First, God's power is not such that the act must exist simultaneously with the power; rather, God can create acts in time. Eternal power does not force eternal creation—the act is dependent on will and occurs at the moment God chooses. Second, God's power is not a natural cause that necessitates its effect tautologically. Nature acts by necessity (like fire burning), but God acts by choice; His power is not a 'cause' in the demanding sense. Therefore, the eternity of God's power does not entail the eternity of the world. The text ends mid-sentence (عير but likely continuing with 'فاعلا' or similar), but the two reasons are complete.

Key Terms
قدرة
علة
إيجاب
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God's power is eternal, He must have created the world eternally, just as a natural cause produces its effect at all times.
Correction: Baqillani shows that God's power does not require the act to coexist with it; God can create in time. Also, divine power is not a natural cause that necessitates, but rather acts by choice.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Baqillani gives to show that eternal divine power does not necessitate an eternal world?
A) God's power is a cause like nature
B) God can create acts in time even though His power is eternal
C) The world's creation is impossible
D) God's power is not eternal

Key Takeaway
Eternal divine power is compatible with temporal creation because God chooses when to act, unlike necessary natural causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani teaches that God's eternal power does not force Him to create without choice. He chose to create you at a specific moment in time. How does knowing that your existence was a deliberate, chosen act of God—not a necessary emanation—change the way you see your purpose and your relationship with Him? What will you do today to live in light of that truth?
?
Opponents argued that if God is all-powerful, He must have always been creating. Baqillani rejects this, saying God's power is not like a natural cause. Have you ever felt that your life was simply a product of forces beyond your control—nature, fate, circumstances? How does Baqillani's distinction free you from that mindset and call you to personal responsibility before a God who acts by will?

Objection: God's Eternal Power and Response
Objection that God's eternal power implies eternal world, and author's two-part answer distinguishing power from natural causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part response to the objection that God's eternal power would require an eternal world, and how the author distinguishes divine power from natural causation.

Core Idea
God's power is not a physical cause; it does not necessitate its effect.

This passage records a powerful objection from the philosophers: if God's power is eternal, then like any eternal cause, the world should be eternal. The author gives two clear reasons why this does not follow. As you read, notice how he distinguishes God's power from the necessitating causality of nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection: 'If they say: This applies to your statement...' Understand the philosophers' claim that an eternal power implies an eternal effect.
2
Identify the first reason
The author says we do not claim that acts coexist with power; rather God can initiate acts in time. So the eternity of power does not require eternal acts.
3
Identify the second reason
The author states that God's power is not a cause (illa) or necessitator (mujib). It is a capacity that does not force an effect into existence.
4
Contrast with natural causation
Notice the contrast: the philosophers' nature necessitates its effect unless prevented, whereas God's power does not necessitate.
5
Summarize the key difference
The core point: divine power is a capacity to act or not act; natural causation is a necessitating force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : هذا يلزمكم في قولكم إن صانع العالم لم يزل قادراً على إيجاده لأن قدرته على الإيجاد قديمة. قلنا لا يجب ذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أننا نحن لا نزعم أن القديم سبحانه قادر بقدرة في الأزل على أن تكون الأفعال مع القدرة وإنما نقول : إنه قادر على أن يستأنف الأفعال وعلى أن يحدثها في زمان قد كانت قبله معدومة؛ ومحال أن تكون قدرة على ما لم يكن معدوماً قبل وجوده؛ فلم يجب قدمُ الأفعال لقدم القدرة عليها. والأمر الآخر أننا نحن لا نزعم أن قدرة القديم سبحانه علة للأفعال ولا موجبة لها حسب ما تقولونه أنتم في إيجاب الطبع لحدث عنه وكونه علةً له ووجوب كونه عنه، ولا نحيل أن توجد قدرة القديم في الأزل وهو غير لمقدوره فاعل بها وإن كانت على صفة ما يصح أن يَفْعَل بها، وكان هو تعالى على صفة من يصح أن يفعل بها، وكان المعدوم المقدورُ مما يصح أن يخرج إلى الوجود ولا مانع يمنع من خروجه لأن قدرته ليست بعلةٍ ولا ولا موجبةٍ له؛ وأنتم تزعمون أن الطبعَ الكائن عنه العالم وكلّ طبع كان منه أمرٌ الأمور موجب لما يحدث عنه ومقتضٍ له، إذا لم يمنع من ذلك مانع، فبان الفرق بين قولنا وقولكم.
If they say: 'This applies to your statement that the Maker of the world has never ceased to be capable of bringing it into existence, because His power over bringing into existence is eternal.' We say: That is not necessary for two reasons. One: We do not claim that the Eternal, exalted is He, is powerful with a power in pre-eternity such that acts occur together with the power; rather we say: He is powerful to initiate actions and to bring them about in a time when they were previously non-existent; and it is impossible for there to be power over what was not non-existent before its existence. Thus, the eternity of actions is not necessitated by the eternity of power over them. The other reason: We do not claim that the power of the Eternal, exalted is He, is a cause of actions nor a necessitator of them, as you claim concerning the nature's necessitation of that which occurs from it and its being a cause of it and the necessity of its occurrence from it. And we do not deem it impossible that the power of the Eternal exist in pre-eternity while He is not acting with it upon what is possible, even though it is in a state that makes it valid to act with it, and He, exalted, is in a state that makes it valid to act with it, and the non-existent possible is something that is valid to come into existence and there is no impediment preventing its emergence, because His power is neither a cause nor a necessitator of it. But you claim that the nature from which the world comes to be, and every nature from which any matter proceeds, necessitates what occurs from it and requires it, if nothing prevents it. Thus the difference between our statement and yours becomes clear.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the philosophers and the author's response. The objection: the same argument (that an eternal cause necessitates an eternal effect) applies to your view that God's power is eternal: if God is eternally powerful, then the world should be eternal. The author gives two answers. First: we do not say that God's power coexists with its acts; rather, God is powerful to initiate acts in time, so power is not simultaneous with the act. Second: God's power is not a cause (illa) or necessitator (mujib); it is not like nature that necessitates its effect. God's power is such that He can act or not act; the possible thing can come into existence without necessitation. The philosophers' nature, by contrast, necessarily produces its effect unless prevented. Hence the objection fails.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra, power)
علة (illa, cause)
موجبة (mujiba, necessitator)
مقتض (muqtada, required)
معدوم (ma'dum, non-existent)
مقدور (maqdur, possible object of power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's power operates like a physical cause, necessitating its effect.
Correction: The author explains that God's power is not a cause (illa) or necessitator (mujib); it is a capacity that allows the possible to come into existence without compulsion.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between God's power and natural causation according to the author?
A) God's power is eternal while nature is temporal.
B) God's power necessitates its effect while nature does not.
C) God's power is not a cause that necessitates, while nature necessitates its effect.
D) God's power is limited while nature is unlimited.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal power does not necessitate an eternal world because His power is a capacity to initiate acts, not a necessitating cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you, deep down, assume that God had to create because of His power, as if He were compelled by His own attribute? Examine your heart: do you see His creation as an act of free generosity or as a necessary outflow? Commit today to thanking Him for His willful act of bringing you into existence, not because He had to, but because He chose to.
?
When you face obstacles or delays in your life, do you become frustrated as if the universe runs on mechanical cause and effect, forgetting that God's power is not bound by necessity? Are you trusting in His wisdom and will, or are you demanding that He act according to your expectations? Repent of any attitude that treats God as a slave to His own power, and surrender to His timing and choice.
?
If God's power does not necessitate His actions, then every moment of your existence is a fresh, voluntary act of His will. Are you living with the awareness that you are sustained not by a natural law, but by the ongoing, unforced choice of your Creator? How does this change your urgency in seeking His pleasure? Make a specific intention today to respond to His personal care with personal devotion.

Answer Regarding God's Eternal Will
Extending the same argument to God's will: eternal will does not necessitate eternal act because will is for delay and not a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's eternal will does not necessitate an eternal creation, according to Imam Baqillani's argument.

Core Idea
Eternal will can be for a delayed act; it is not a cause.

Imam Baqillani extends his earlier argument about knowledge and power to the attribute of will. The objection is that if God's will is eternal, then what He wills must also be eternal. But Baqillani distinguishes: will is for delay and is not a causal determinant. This short passage clarifies a key point in the debate over the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two reasons given: delay and non-causality.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage gives two distinct reasons why eternal will does not force an eternal act. Find each one and understand how they work.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this argument defends the Islamic belief that the world is created in time, even though God's will is eternal.
This connects to the broader debate about the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الجواب إن ألزموا هذا الإلزام في إرادة الله تعالى للأفعال وإن كانت قديمة عندنا لأنها على قولنا إرادة لكون الفعل على التراخي ولأنها ليست علة لوجود المراد.
And similarly the answer if they insist on this objection regarding the will of God, exalted, for actions, even if it is eternal according to us, because it is, according to our statement, a will for the occurrence of the act after delay, and because it is not a cause for the existence of the object of will.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This short passage extends the same reasoning to God's will. The philosophers might object that if God's will is eternal, then the world should be eternal too. The author replies: God's will is a will for the act to occur at a delayed time (not immediately), and it is not a cause that necessitates the existence of what is willed. Therefore, an eternal will does not imply an eternal effect.

تراخي (tarakhi, delay)

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will is for temporal acts, and it is not a cause that forces immediate existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Philosophers' 'Nature' as the Creator by Another Name
Baqillani argues that philosophers who describe nature as volitional essentially affirm the Islamic God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani demonstrates that the philosophers' description of 'nature'—as living, knowing, powerful, and acting by choice—actually aligns with the Islamic concept of the Creator, differing only in terminology.

Core Idea
A self-aware, willing 'nature' is not nature; it is the Creator by another name.

Imam al-Baqillani responds to the philosophers' claim that the world's creator is a 'nature' that is eternal. He shows that if this nature possesses life, knowledge, power, and acts by choice, then it is functionally identical to the God of Islam. The only dispute is over the word 'nature', which Islamic law prohibits using for the Divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic and its literal translation. Note the conditional structure: 'If they say... then they have acknowledged the truth.'
Pay attention to the attributes listed: living, knowing, powerful, acting by choice.
2
Identify the attributes
List the four attributes the philosophers ascribe to nature: life, knowledge, power, and choice-based action. Contrast these with mechanical necessitation.
These are exactly the attributes of the Islamic Creator.
3
Understand the author's conclusion
Notice the author's argument: by admitting these attributes, the philosophers have agreed with the Islamic position. The only difference is the term 'nature', which is forbidden in Islamic law.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument reframes the debate: it's not about the existence of a Creator, but about what you call Him.
This is a powerful rhetorical and theological move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : إن هذا الطبع القديم هو شيء حي عالم قادر ليس بموجب للفعل ولا علةٍ له، بل يفعل بالقدرة والاختيار، أقروا بالحق وصانع العالم الذي نثبته، وإن خالفونا في تسميته طبعاً، وكان هذا عندنا محظوراً بالشرع.
If they say: 'This eternal nature is something living, knowing, powerful, not a necessitator of the act nor a cause of it, but it acts by power and choice' — then they have acknowledged the truth and the Maker of the world that we affirm, even if they disagree with us in calling it 'nature', and this is prohibited by the Sharia according to us.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a partial concession. If the philosophers describe the eternal nature as living, knowing, powerful, and acting by choice rather than by necessitation, then they have actually agreed with the proponents of Islam (that there is a voluntary Creator), even though they misuse the term 'nature'. However, the author notes that using the term 'nature' for such a being is prohibited by Islamic law (Sharia), implying a verbal concession but substantial agreement.

Key Terms
حي (hayy, living)
عالم (alim, knowing)
قادر (qadir, powerful)
اختيار (ikhtiyar, choice)
شرع (shar', law)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that philosophers who believe in a 'nature' as the world's origin are atheists who deny a creator.
Correction: The passage shows that if they attribute life, knowledge, power, and volition to that nature, they are essentially affirming the same Creator as Islam, even if they use a different name.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response if the philosophers admit their nature acts by power and choice?
A) They are atheists.
B) They have acknowledged the truth but use a prohibited term.
C) They have proved their point.
D) They must be rejected.

Key Takeaway
When philosophers describe 'nature' as living, knowing, powerful, and acting by choice, they unwittingly affirm the Islamic Creator—only the name differs, and that name is prohibited.

God's Actions Do Not Change His Essence
Baqillani answers an objection about God's actions being separate from His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God's actions (like justice) do not imply a change in His essence, refuting the analogy with celestial causes.

Core Idea
God's attributes do not change Him; effects are merely acts, not modifications.

In this passage, Baqillani confronts a clever objection: if God's actions are separate from His essence, then why can't celestial bodies produce separate effects? He answers by clarifying that for God, being an 'agent' or 'just' simply means the act exists from Him — His essence remains unchanged. This lesson unpacks that response and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's statement in Arabic. Notice how they link 'action' and 'justice' to the agent's identity, and then extend the logic to celestial bodies.
The objection uses 'فإن قالوا' — a common formula for presenting a hypothetical challenge.
2
Identify Baqillani's reply
Read the response ('قيل لهم'). Pay attention to the key phrase 'ليس للفاعل بكونه فاعلاً وعادلاً حكم أكثر من وجود الفعل والعدل منه'. This is the core of his argument.
Baqillani distinguishes between a property that changes the subject (like knowledge) and a property that is merely the existence of an act.
3
Compare the two cases
Contrast the case of God (whose essence does not change) with a being that acquires knowledge or will (whose state changes). This comparison shows why the analogy with celestial bodies fails.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why Baqillani says 'فسقط ما سألتم عنه'. The objection is invalid because the 'effect' in God's case is not a separate entity that alters His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أفليس الفعل والعدل والتفضل يوجب كون الفاعل فاعلاً والعادل عادلاً، وإن لم يُوجَد ذلك في محله؛ لأن الفعل والعدل من الله تعالى منفصل من ذاته تعالى؟ قيل لهم : ليس للفاعل بكونه فاعلاً وعادلاً حكم أكثر من وجود الفعل والعدل منه وليس يتغير حكم نفسه بوجود الفعل كما يتغير حكم من ليس بعالم ولا مريد بوجود العلم والإرادة؛ فسقط ما سألتم عنه
If they say: 'Does not action, justice, and favor necessitate the agent being an agent and the just being just, even if that is not found in its place? Because action and justice from God Almighty are separate from His Essence?' It is said to them: The agent, by being an agent and just, has no necessary property more than the existence of the action and justice from him. And his own necessary property does not change with the existence of the action, unlike the necessary property of one who is not knowing or willing changes with the existence of knowledge and will. Therefore, what you asked about is refuted.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a short objection-and-answer. The objector raises a point: If a cause must produce its effect within itself, then how do we explain God's actions (like creating or being just)? These actions seem to be separate from God's essence, yet they still make Him an agent or just. So why can't celestial bodies have effects separate from themselves? Baqillani answers that when we say God is an agent or just, the 'necessary property' (ḥukm) is simply the existence of the act or justice from Him. This does not change His essence; He remains the same. The analogy fails because in the celestial case, the claimed effect would be a separate entity, whereas God's attributes do not produce a separate 'effect' that changes Him. Moreover, unlike a being that acquires knowledge or will (which changes its state), God's essence does not change with the existence of actions. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
فعل
عدل
تفضل
فاعل
عادل
حكم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's actions cause a change in His essence, similar to how acquiring knowledge changes a creature.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that God's essence does not change; the 'effect' of being an agent is merely the existence of the act from Him.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani refute the objection that God's actions are separate from His essence, so celestial effects could also be separate?
A) He says God's essence is similar to celestial bodies, so the analogy holds.
B) He argues that for God, the 'effect' of being agent is just the existence of the action, which does not alter His essence.
C) He denies that God's actions are separate from His essence.
D) He says celestial bodies are different because they are not divine.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that God's actions do not modify His essence; they are merely expressions of His attributes, so the analogy with celestial causes fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously thought that your actions could change God—making Him pleased or angry as if He were like us? How does Baqillani's point correct that assumption in your heart? Commit to abandoning one such anthropomorphic thought today.
?
The objector argued from analogy with created beings. Where in your own thinking about God do you draw analogies from human experience that are not fitting? What is one analogy you must consciously reject and replace with what the lesson teaches?
?
Baqillani says the agent's necessary property is only the existence of the act, not a change. Does your worship reflect that God's essence is unchanged by your actions? Or do you act as if you can alter His state? Repent from that mindset and resolve to worship Him as the Self-Sufficient, Unchanging One.

God Cannot Be Devoid of Knowledge in Pre-Eternity
Objection-and-answer on divine knowledge in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam uses analogical reasoning to defend the necessity of divine knowledge in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If God could lack knowledge, He could lack life—but both are impossible.

This lesson examines a powerful objection against the necessity of God's knowledge in pre-eternity. The opponent suggests that God might have been devoid of thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance. The author refutes this by showing that if such deprivation were possible, then deprivation of life and power would also be possible—yet all agree God must have these perfect attributes. The argument hinges on a shared principle of divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's statement and note the four things they claim God could be devoid of: thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance.
Notice that the opponent includes both knowledge and ignorance—they are symmetrical.
2
Understand the author's reply
The author uses an analogy: if devoid of knowledge is possible, then devoid of life and power is possible. But that is false by consensus.
The author appeals to a shared belief between the opponent and himself.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opponent's claim leads to an impossible conclusion, so the claim is false.
The conclusion 'fasada ma qultumūhu' means 'what you have said is invalidated.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قَالُوا: مَا أَنْكَرْتُمْ أَنْ يَكُونَ قَدْ خَلَا فِي الْقِدَمِ مِنَ الْفِكْرِ وَالشَّكِّ وَالْعِلْمِ وَالْجَهْلِ؟ قِيلَ لَهُمْ: لَوْ جَازَ ذَلِكَ عَلَيْهِ مَعَ صِحَّةِ وُجُودِ الْعِلْمِ لَهُ وَجَوَازِهِ عَلَيْهِ لَجَازَ خُلُوُّهُ تَعَالَى فِي الْقِدَمِ مِنَ الْحَيَاةِ وَالْمَوْتِ وَالْقُدْرَةِ وَالْعَجْزِ، وَذَلِكَ بَاطِلٌ مِنْ قَوْلِنَا وَقَوْلِكُمْ، فَفَسَدَ مَا قُلْتُمُوهُ.
Then if they say: What do you deny that He was devoid in pre-eternity of thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance? It is said to them: If that were permissible for Him, with the possibility of the existence of knowledge for Him and its permissibility for Him, then it would be permissible for Him, exalted be He, to be devoid in pre-eternity of life and death, power and impotence, and that is false according to our statement and your statement, so what you have said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents object: what prevents God from being devoid (khala) in pre-eternity (al-qidam) of thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance? The author answers by drawing an analogy: if it were permissible for God to lack knowledge despite it being possible for Him to have it, then similarly it would be permissible for Him to lack life and power. But both sides agree that God cannot be devoid of life and power in pre-eternity — that would be absurd (bāṭil). Therefore, by parity of reasoning, it is also absurd for God to be devoid of knowledge. The argument uses the principle that attributes of perfection (life, knowledge, power) are inseparable from God in pre-eternity; their absence would imply deficiency, which is impossible.

Key Terms
الْخُلُوُّ (being devoid)
الْقِدَمُ (pre-eternity)
الْحَيَاةُ (life)
الْقُدْرَةُ (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that being devoid of something is neutral and does not imply imperfection.
Correction: In kalam, being devoid of a perfection attribute (like knowledge, life, power) in pre-eternity implies imperfection, because God must possess all perfections essentially.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that God could be devoid of knowledge in pre-eternity?
A) By saying knowledge is necessary for creation.
B) By analogy with life and power — if devoid of knowledge were possible, then devoid of life and power would also be possible, which is absurd.
C) By arguing that knowledge is eternal like God.
D) By quoting scripture.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be devoid of knowledge in pre-eternity because that would entail the possibility of being devoid of life and power, which is impossible by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God has never been without knowledge of every detail of your life, would you be willing to change any habit you have been hiding?
?
When you find yourself doubting God's awareness of your struggles, what specific reminder from this lesson can you use to realign your heart?
?
This lesson argues that God's knowledge is as essential as His life. Can you point to an area in your life where you act as if God does not see—and what would it cost you to repent right now?

Refuting the Claim That God Could Be Devoid of Knowledge in Pre-Eternity
Bāqillānī refutes an objection using a parity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Bāqillānī uses a reductio ad absurdum argument to refute the claim that God could be devoid of knowledge in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If God can lack knowledge, He could also lack life — an absurdity both sides reject.

In this passage, Bāqillānī responds to an opponent's suggestion that in pre-eternity, God might have been without thought, doubt, knowledge, or ignorance. The argument is sharp: if it is possible for God to be devoid of knowledge when knowledge is possible for Him, then by the same logic He could be devoid of life and power. Since both parties affirm that God is living and powerful, the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's statement: 'What prevents you from accepting that He might have been devoid in pre-eternity of thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance?' Recognize that the opponent is proposing a neutral state.
The opponent lists both positive and negative attributes (thought, doubt, knowledge, ignorance) to suggest God might lack them all.
2
Follow Bāqillānī's response
Bāqillānī responds by drawing an analogy: if lacking knowledge is permissible, then lacking life and power should also be permissible. But both sides agree God is living and powerful eternally.
This is an argument by parity of reasoning.
3
Understand the conclusion
The opponent's suggestion leads to a conclusion that both parties reject (God lacking life and power), so the premise must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون قد خلا في القدم من الفكر والشك والعلم والجهل قيل لهم لو جاز ذلك عليه مع صحة وجود العلم له وجوازه عليه لجاز خلوه تعالى في القدم من الحياة والموت والقدرة والعجز وذلك باطل من قولنا وقولكم ففسد ما قلتموه
If they say: 'What prevents you from [accepting] that He might have been devoid in pre-eternity of thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance?' It is said to them: 'If that were permissible for Him, while the existence of knowledge for Him is sound and its being permissible for Him, then it would be permissible for Him, exalted, to be devoid in pre-eternity of life and death, power and incapacity. And that is invalid according to our statement and your statement, so what you said is corrupted.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The opponent suggests that in pre-eternity (before creation) God might have been devoid of certain attributes like thought, doubt, knowledge, and ignorance. Bāqillānī responds by drawing an analogy: if it were possible for God to lack knowledge (while knowledge is possible for Him), then by the same logic it should be possible for God to lack life, power, etc. But both parties agree that God must be living and powerful eternally. Thus the opponent's suggestion leads to an absurd consequence and is therefore invalid.

Key Terms
خلا في القدم
الفكر
الشك
العلم
الجهل
الحياة
الموت
القدرة
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be without any attributes in pre-eternity, including knowledge, as a neutral state.
Correction: Bāqillānī shows that if such neutrality were allowed, God could also be without life and power, which is impossible by consensus.

Checkpoint
How does Bāqillānī refute the claim that God could be devoid of knowledge in pre-eternity?
A) By arguing that knowledge is impossible for God.
B) By saying that God cannot be devoid of life and power either.
C) By claiming that thought and doubt are essential to God.
D) By citing scripture.

Key Takeaway
Bāqillānī uses a parity argument to show that denying God knowledge in pre-eternity would entail denying life and power, which is impossible by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The opponent's claim sounds abstract, but it has real consequences. Are there areas of your life where you treat God's attributes as optional or negotiable? What would it mean for you to fully embrace that God's knowledge and power are inseparable from His essence?

Refuting the Origin of Shaytan from Punishment
Baqillani refutes the view that shaytan originated from God's punishment of a disobedient person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical argument that God's wisdom remains consistent whether He creates a disobedient person or directly creates shaytan, and that opponents cannot find a principled distinction.

Core Idea
Creating evil directly is no different from creating a sinner who becomes evil.

This passage presents a powerful dialectical argument against those who claim that shaytan (the devil) originated from God's punishment of a disobedient person. Baqillani challenges them to explain why God would be considered wise if He created a sinner from the beginning, but not if He directly created shaytan. The argument forces a logical stalemate, showing that the opponent's position is inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to follow the flow of the argument.
Notice the question-and-answer format used by Baqillani.
2
Identify the key analogy
Baqillani draws an analogy between God creating a disobedient person (who is evil) and creating shaytan directly. Ask yourself: what is the similarity?
Both involve God bringing evil into existence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Realize that if opponents accept God as wise in creating a sinner, they must accept Him as wise in creating shaytan. The inability to find a distinction undermines their position.
The argument ends by extending to all evils.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك السؤال على أصحاب الفكر والعقاب . ويقال للقائلين بأن الشيطان حدث عن عقوبة من خلق العاصي المستوجب للعقاب؟ فإذا قالوا الله قيل لهم أفليس من كان منه العصيان شراً كالذي كان منه؟ فإذا قالوا: أجل، قيل لهم : أفليس قد خَلَق الله شريراً عصاه ابتداءً، وكان عندكم بذلك حكيماً؟ فلِمَ لا يجوز على هذا أن يبتدىء خلق الشيطان الذي كان منه الشرُّ ويكون بذلك خيراً حكيماً؟ فإن راموا فصلاً لم يجدوه، وإن مروا على ذلك تركوا قولهم، وقيل لهم فما أنكرتم أن يخلق الله سائر الشرور ويكون بذلك حكيماً .
And likewise the question to the adherents of thought and punishment. And it is said to those who say that shaytan originated from the punishment of one who created the disobedient deserving punishment? If they say: 'God,' it is said to them: 'Is not the one from whom disobedience came evil, like that from which it came?' And if they say: 'Yes,' it is said to them: 'Has not God created an evil person who disobeyed Him from the beginning, and in your view He was thereby wise? Why then is it not allowed that He initially create shaytan from whom evil came, and thereby be good and wise? And if they seek a distinction, they will not find it, and if they persist in that, they abandon their statement, and it is said to them: 'What prevents you from [admitting] that God creates all evils and thereby is wise?'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that shaytan originated from God's punishment of a created disobedient person. Baqillani argues that if God creates a sinner and punishes him, He is considered wise; similarly, He could directly create shaytan. The opponent cannot draw a logical distinction between creating a sinner (who later becomes disobedient) and creating shaytan directly. If they try to find a distinction, they fail. Thus, the argument forces them to accept that God can create all evils and still be wise, which contradicts their original denial.

Key Terms
العقاب (al-uquba: punishment)
العاصي (al-asi: the disobedient)
حكيم (hakim: wise)
شيطان (shaytan: devil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Punishment of a sinner is fundamentally different from directly creating shaytan, so God's wisdom applies only to the former.
Correction: Baqillani shows that both cases involve God causing evil to exist. If He is wise in one, He must be wise in the other; there is no principled distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the main point Baqillani makes against those who say shaytan originated from punishment?
A) That punishment cannot produce evil.
B) That if God can create a disobedient person and punish him wisely, He can directly create shaytan.
C) That shaytan is evil, so God cannot create him.
D) That punishment is good, so it cannot produce evil.

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom is not constrained by the source of evil; creating a sinner and creating shaytan are morally equivalent for the Creator, so opponents cannot distinguish them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil or hardship in your life, do you truly believe that God is wise in creating it? Or do you secretly attribute it to chance or secondary causes? Examine your heart: are you willing to submit to God's decree without questioning His wisdom?
?
This passage shows that no distinction can be made between God creating a sinner and creating shaytan. If God directly created the source of evil, then what does that mean for your own sins? Will you stop blaming shaytan or circumstances and take full responsibility for your disobedience?
?
If God's wisdom is unconstrained by how evil arises, then your own trials are directly from Him for a wise purpose. Can you trust that His plan for you, even in pain, is good? Commit today to saying 'I believe in God's wisdom' even when you do not understand.

Four Christian Arguments for God as Substance (Jawhar)
Baqillani enumerates four Christian proofs that God is a jawhar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four different dichotomies Christians used to argue that God must be a substance (jawhar), and why these arguments are presented before being refuted.

Core Idea
Christians used four different binary classifications to argue God is a substance.

Imam al-Baqillani presents four hypothetical arguments that Christians might use to claim that God is a jawhar (substance). Each argument starts from a different division of existence, but all conclude that God must be a self-subsistent substance. These are not Baqillani's own views; he lays them out before refuting them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the repeated phrase 'أو قالوا' (or they said) which marks each new argument.
There are four distinct arguments, each based on a different binary division.
2
Examine the first two arguments
The first argument uses the division into substance/accident, the second into self-subsistent/dependent. Understand how each leads to the conclusion that God is a substance.
These are the simplest arguments.
3
Examine the last two arguments
The third argument uses the division into agent/non-agent, the fourth into noble/base. Note how each appeals to God's attributes (action, nobility).
The fourth argument includes a value judgment (noble vs base).
4
Reflect on the purpose of these arguments
Remember that Baqillani is presenting these as objections to be refuted later. Consider what assumptions these arguments make about 'substance' that might be problematic.
This prepares you for the refutation in the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تخلو من أن تكون جواهر وأعراضاً؛ وقد اتفقنا على أن القديم ليس بعرض فوجب أن يكون جوهراً؛ أو قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أنا وجدنا الأشياء كلها لا تخرج عن قسمين: إما قائم بنفسه أو قائم بغيره؛ والقائم بغيره العرض والقائم بنفسه هو الجوهر؛ فلما فسد من قولنا وقولكم أن يكون هو قائماً بغيره وأن يكون عرضاً، ثبت أنه قائم بنفسه وأنه جوهر من الجواهر. أو قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أنا وجدنا الأشياء كلها على ضربين: فضرب منها يصح منه الأفعال وهو الجوهر وضرب تتعذر وتمتنع منه وهو العرض فلما ثبت أن القديم فاعل وممن تأتي منه الأفعال ثبت أنه جوهر. أو قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أنّا وجدنا الأشياء على ضربين: شريف وهو الجوهر القائم بنفسه المستغني في الوجود عن غيره؛ وخسيس قائم بغيره ومحتاج إليه وهو العرض؛ فلما لم يجز أن يكون القديم من قبيل الخسيس ثبت أنه شريف وأنه قائم بنفسه.
They cannot be free from being substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad). And we have agreed that the Eternal (al-qadim) is not an accident, so He must be a substance. Or they said: The proof for that is that we have found all things not going beyond two divisions: either self-subsistent (qa'im bi nafsihi) or subsistent in another (qa'im bi ghayrihi). That which subsists in another is the accident, and the self-subsistent is the substance. Since it is false from our statement and yours that He is subsistent in another and is an accident, it is established that He is self-subsistent and that He is a substance among substances. Or they said: The proof for that is that we have found all things to be of two types: a type from which actions proceed, and that is the substance, and a type for which actions are impossible and prevented, and that is the accident. Since it is established that the Eternal is an agent (fa'il) and from whom actions come, it is established that He is a substance. Or they said: The proof for that is that we have found things to be of two types: noble (sharif), which is the substance self-subsistent, independent in existence from other than itself, and base (khasis), subsistent in another and in need of it, which is the accident. Since it is not permissible for the Eternal to belong to the base category, it is established that He is noble and that He is self-subsistent.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents four hypothetical arguments that Christians might use to claim that God is a jawhar (substance). The first argument: All existents are either substances or accidents. Since we agree that the Eternal is not an accident, He must be a substance. The second argument: All things are either self-subsistent or subsistent in another. The latter is accident, the former is substance. Since God cannot be accident, He is self-subsistent, hence a substance. The third argument: All things are either capable of actions (substance) or incapable (accident). God is an agent (fa'il), so He must be a substance. The fourth argument: All things are either noble (self-subsistent, independent) or base (dependent on another). God cannot be base, so He is noble and self-subsistent, hence a substance. Baqillani is likely presenting these arguments before refuting them, but within this passage he only states them.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
قديم
قائم بنفسه
قائم بغيره
فاعل
شريف
خسيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these arguments represent Baqillani's own position or Islamic theology.
Correction: Baqillani is presenting hypothetical Christian arguments, which he will later refute. They are not affirmed by him.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the proofs presented for God being a jawhar?
A) Based on the division of existents into substance and accident
B) Based on the division into self-subsistent and dependent
C) Based on the division into living and non-living
D) Based on the division into noble and base

Key Takeaway
The passage presents four Christian arguments that God must be a substance, each based on a different binary division of existence; these are later refuted by Baqillani.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify any assumption in your own thinking about God that you hold simply because it seems logical, without realizing it might be based on a flawed human framework? How willing are you to have that assumption challenged by revelation?
?
The Christians used binary divisions to 'prove' God is a substance. When you try to understand Allah through categories like 'noble vs. base', are you imposing a worldly value system on the Divine? What would it mean to let Allah define Himself, even if it defies your categories?
?
If these arguments for God being a substance are ultimately rejected, what does that tell you about the limits of human reason in knowing Allah? Are you prepared to accept that some of your well-reasoned beliefs about God might be wrong, and to seek truth from the Qur'an and Sunnah instead?

The Eternal Must Be Self-Subsistent
Baqillani presents the first proof: the eternal is a substance because it is not an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that the eternal must be a substance based on the division of all entities into self-subsistent and subsistent by another.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot be an accident, so it must be self-subsistent.

Baqillani begins with a fundamental classification: every existing thing is either self-subsistent (substance) or dependent on another (accident). Since the eternal is agreed not to be an accident, it must be a self-subsistent substance. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation to confirm understanding.
Notice how the argument uses the dichotomy of substance vs. accident.
2
Identify the two categories
The argument divides everything into two: self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi) and subsistent by another (qa'im bi-ghayrihi). Identify which corresponds to substance and which to accident.
The eternal cannot be an accident because accidents are dependent.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow how the argument eliminates accident as a possibility for the eternal, leaving only the self-subsistent category, which is substance.
This is a classic kalam argument by dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تخلو من أن تكون جواهر وأعراضاً؛ وقد اتفقنا على أن القديم ليس بِعَرَض فوجب أن يكون جوهراً؛ أو قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أنا وجدنا الأشياء كلها لا تخرج عن قسمين: إما قائم بنفسه أو قائم بغيره؛ والقائم بغيره العَرَضُ والقائم بنفسه هو الجوهر؛ فلما فَسَدّ من قولنا وقولكم أن يكون هو الأفعال وهو قائماً بغيره وأن يكون عَرَضاً، ثبت أنه قائم بنفسه وأنه جوهر من الجواهر. أو قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أنا وجدنا الأشياء كلها على ضربين: ضرب منها يصح منه الأفعال وهو الجوهر وضرب تتعذر وتمتنع منه وهو العَرَضُ فلما ثبت أن القديم فاعل وممن تأتي منه الأفعال ثبت أنه جوهر. أو قالوا: الدليل على ذلك أنا وجدنا الأشياء على ضربين: شريف وهو الجوهر القائم بنفسه المستغني في الوجود عن غيره؛ وخسيس قائم بغيره ومحتاج إليه وهو العرض؛ فلما لم يجز أن يكون القديم من قبيل الخسيس ثبت أنه شريف وأنه قائم بنفسه.
It is not free from being substances or accidents; and we have agreed that the eternal is not an accident, so it must be a substance. Or they said: the proof of that is that we found all things do not exit from two divisions: either self-subsistent or subsistent by another; and that which subsists by another is the accident, and that which is self-subsistent is the substance. So when it became invalid from our statement and your statement that it is the actions and that it is subsistent by another and that it is an accident, it was established that it is self-subsistent and that it is a substance among substances. Or they said: the proof of that is that we found all things are of two kinds: a kind from which actions are valid and that is the substance, and a kind from which actions are impossible and prevented and that is the accident; so when it was established that the eternal is an agent and actions come from it, it was established that it is a substance. Or they said: the proof of that is that we found things are of two kinds: noble, which is the self-subsistent substance, independent in existence from others; and base, subsistent by another and in need of it, and that is the accident. So when it was not permissible for the eternal to be of the base category, it was established that it is noble and that it is self-subsistent.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct arguments for the conclusion that the eternal (al-qadim) must be a substance (jawhar) and not an accident (arad). The first argument uses a division of all things into either self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi) or subsistent by another (qa'im bi-ghayrihi). Since the eternal is agreed not to be an accident, it must be self-subsistent, i.e., a substance. The second argument classifies things into that from which actions proceed (the substance) and that from which actions are impossible (the accident). Since the eternal is an agent (fa'il), it must be a substance. The third argument divides things into noble (sharif), which is the self-subsistent substance independent in existence, and base (khasis), which is the accident dependent on another. The eternal cannot be base, so it is noble and self-subsistent. Each proof is introduced with 'aw qalu' (or they said), indicating alternative proofs for the same conclusion.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
قديم
قائم بنفسه
قائم بغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the eternal could be an accident if it is not dependent on anything.
Correction: Accidents are by definition dependent on a substance; the eternal, being independent, cannot be an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the fundamental division used to argue that the eternal is a substance?
A) Things are either bodies or accidents.
B) Things are either self-subsistent or subsistent by another.
C) Things are either eternal or created.
D) Things are either material or immaterial.

Key Takeaway
The eternal must be a substance because it cannot be an accident, and everything is either substance or accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any aspect of your life where you are functionally treating something created (a person, status, possession) as if it were self-subsistent — as if your existence depended on it? What would it take for you to reorient that dependence solely toward the Eternal?
?
If the eternal is self-subsistent and you are not, what does that mean for your daily anxieties? Are you trying to make yourself into a 'substance' when you were created to depend on God? How can you consciously embrace your dependence today?
?
You just read that the eternal cannot be an accident — it cannot be 'subsistent by another'. Are there beliefs or actions in your life that treat God as if He were dependent on your obedience or worship? In what specific way can you worship Him as entirely Self-Sufficient this week?

The Eternal as Agent Requires It to Be a Substance
Baqillani presents the second proof: the eternal is an agent, so it must be a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument: because the eternal is an agent capable of actions, it must be a substance rather than an accident.

Core Idea
Only substances can act; accidents cannot. The eternal acts, so it is a substance.

A second argument is that among existing things, only substances can perform actions; accidents are incapable of acting. Since the eternal is an agent (fa'il) from whom actions proceed, it must belong to the category of substance. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note the classification into two kinds based on the ability to act.
The proof hinges on the attribute of 'being an agent' (fa'il).
2
Identify the two kinds
Things are of two kinds: that from which actions are valid (substance) and that from which actions are impossible (accident). Understand why accidents cannot act.
Accidents are dependent and transient, so they cannot originate actions.
3
Apply to the eternal
Since the eternal is an agent and actions come from it, it must be of the first kind, i.e., a substance.
This argument connects God's attribute of being fa'il to His being jawhar.
