The Eternal's Nobility Shows It Is a Substance
Baqillani presents the third proof: the eternal is noble, so it must be a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument: since the eternal is noble and independent, it cannot be a base accident, so it must be a substance.

Core Idea
The eternal is noble and independent; accidents are base and dependent. So the eternal is a substance.

The third argument appeals to the inherent nobility of the eternal. Things are divided into noble (self-subsistent, independent) and base (dependent accident). The eternal, being perfect and independent, cannot be in the base category, so it must be a noble substance. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third proof. Notice the value-laden terms ‘noble’ (sharif) and ‘base’ (khasis).
This argument is more ethical/metaphysical than the previous ones.
2
Understand the two categories
The two kinds are: noble (self-subsistent, independent) and base (subsistent by another, dependent). Accidents are dependent and therefore base.
Nobility here refers to ontological independence, not moral virtue.
3
Apply to the eternal
The eternal is perfect and independent; it cannot be base. Therefore, it must be noble, i.e., a self-subsistent substance.
This argument shows that the eternal's perfection implies it is a substance.

Refuting the Analogy: The Creator Is Not a Substance
Baqillani uses a dialectical argument to refute the analogy of God as a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's dialectical argument that God cannot be a substance because doing so would entail properties that are incompatible with divinity.

Core Idea
If God were a substance, He would be like all other substances — but we know He is not.

Baqillani presents a rigorous argument against those who claim God is a substance. He uses their own method of analogy from the witnessed world to show the absurdity of their position. This passage demonstrates the critical importance of not applying analogy uncritically to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own dialectical flow come through.
The passage is short but dense with logical steps.
2
Identify the key terms
Notice the terms جوهر (substance), عرض (accident), and متحيز (extended/occupying space). These are central to the argument.
3
Follow the dialectical structure
The argument proceeds as a question, an expected answer, an implication, a dilemma, and a conclusion. Track each step.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Consider why the opponents cannot accept that God is extended and receptive to accidents. The author forces them to choose between consistency and religious belief.
5
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that God is an existent unlike any witnessed thing. Grasp why this refutes the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : فهل وجدتم جوهراً في الشاهد إلا مُتَحَيِّزاً قابلاً للأعراض من جنس هذه الجواهر المعقولة؟ فإذا قالوا : بلى، قيل لهم : فيجب عليكم، إذا كان القديم تعالى جوهراً، أن يكون كالجواهر المعقولة ومن جنسها وقابلا للأعراض كقبولها؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم، وإن أبوا، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون القديم سبحانه موجوداً ليس بجوهر ولا عرض ولا كالموجودات في الشاهد، كما أنه ليس كالجواهر؟ ولا فَصْلَ في ذلك أبداً.
Then it is said to them: Did you find any substance in the witnessed world except that it is extended, receptive to accidents, of the same kind as these intelligible substances? When they say: Yes, it is said to them: Then it is incumbent upon you, if the Eternal, exalted is He, is a substance, that He be like the intelligible substances, of the same kind as them, and receptive to accidents like their reception; if they accept that, they abandon their religion, and if they refuse, it is said to them: What prevents you from holding that the Eternal, glorified is He, is an existent that is neither a substance nor an accident, nor like the existents in the witnessed world, just as He is not like the substances? And there is no difference in that ever.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage tackles the claim that God might be a substance (jawhar). The author asks the opponents if they find any substance in the witnessed world that is not extended (mutaḥayyiz) and receptive to accidents. They agree. The author then applies their analogy: if God is a substance, He must be like witnessed substances — extended and receiving accidents. Since they reject that, they must admit that God is unlike any witnessed existent, thereby abandoning the necessity of strict analogy. The key point is that the opponents themselves do not fully apply their own method when it contradicts their beliefs.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
قديم
مُتَحَيِّز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'jawhar' (substance) can be attributed to God without implying anthropomorphism.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if God were a substance, He would necessarily be extended and receptive to accidents, which are properties of created things, contradicting His transcendence.

Checkpoint
What property of witnessed substances does the author highlight to argue that God cannot be a substance?
A) They are created in time
B) They are extended and receive accidents
C) They are divisible
D) They have color

Key Takeaway
The attributes of created substances cannot be attributed to God without contradicting His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God in your prayers or thoughts, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having form or location? How does this argument expose that tendency and call you to purify your conception?
?
Are there any attributes you affirm about God that are actually borrowed from your experience of creation? This lesson forces a dilemma: either accept that God is unlike anything, or unknowingly slip into tashbih. Will you sincerely examine your beliefs and repent from any subtle comparison?
?
The opponent is left with no escape: either abandon religion or abandon the analogy. But what about you? Do you truly believe that God is unlike anything you have ever seen? Can you live with the discomfort of not being able to picture Him, or do you still cling to mental images?

Baqillani's Dilemma: The Opponents' Own Division Forces God to Be a Body
Baqillani argues that the opponents' own division of things forces God to be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani turns the opponents' classification of existents against them to argue that God must be a bearer of accidents (a body).

Core Idea
If the opponents' own division makes God a body, their whole method collapses.

Baqillani now takes the offensive. Instead of merely defending the Ash'ari position, he shows that the opponents' own reasoning forces them into a contradiction. Using their classification of all things into two types, he demonstrates that the Eternal, being effective and self-subsistent, must belong to the category of bodies that bear accidents. This puts them in a dilemma: either accept that God is a body (which they reject) or abandon their entire deductive approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice how Baqillani begins by addressing the opponents directly.
2
Identify the two categories of existents
Find the two types of things Baqillani divides all existents into. Note the characteristics of each.
The first type is described as effective, noble, self-subsistent, and the bearer of accidents.
3
Apply the classification to the Eternal
See how Baqillani applies the opponents' own criteria to the Eternal. Why must He belong to the first category?
The opponents themselves consider the Eternal effective and self-subsistent.
4
Understand the dilemma
Consider both outcomes: if they accept the conclusion, they abandon their religion; if they reject it, they invalidate their own reasoning.
This dilemma is the core of the refutation.
5
Reflect on the argument's effectiveness
Think about why this argument is powerful against the opponents' methodology. It uses their own premises against them.

ثم يقال لهم على سائر أدلتهم التي قدمنا ذكرها : ما أنكرتم أن يكون حاملاً للأعراض بمثل كل دليل ذكرتموه؟ وذلك أنا وجدنا القديم الأشياء كلها على ضربين فضرب فعّال شريف قائم بنفسه ليس بعرض، وهو الحامل للأعراض؛ وضرب آخر ليس قائماً بنفسه ولا فعّالاً ولا شريفاً، وهو العرض؛ فلما ثبت أن القديم فعّال قائم بنفسه شریف ليس بخسيس، ثبت أنه حامل للأعراض ذو حَيز وشغل؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم؛ وإن أبوا أبطلوا استدلالهم إبطالاً ظاهراً.
Then it is said to them concerning all their proofs which we have previously mentioned: What prevents you from holding that He is a bearer of accidents by the like of every proof you have mentioned? That is because we found that all things in the existent world are of two types: a type that is effective, noble, subsistent by itself, not an accident, and it is the bearer of accidents; and another type that is not subsistent by itself, nor effective, nor noble, and it is the accident. So when it is established that the Eternal is effective, subsistent by itself, noble, not base, it is established that He is a bearer of accidents, possessing extension and occupation of space; if they accept that, they abandon their religion, and if they refuse, they invalidate their deduction in a manifest manner.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of all existents into two categories: (1) effective, noble, self-subsistent bearers of accidents (i.e., bodies or substances that can receive accidents), and (2) accidents themselves, which are non-self-subsistent and non-effective. The author argues that since the opponents describe the Eternal as effective and self-subsistent, He must belong to the first category, making Him a bearer of accidents, i.e., a body with spatial attributes. Since the opponents deny this, they contradict their own classification and thus invalidate their entire method of reasoning. The dilemma forces them to either accept an absurd conclusion or abandon their deductive approach.

The Division of Existence and the Bearer of Accidents
Refuting the denial that God bears accidents via existence division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the denial that God is a bearer of accidents by dividing existence into two fundamental categories.

Core Idea
If existence is divided into active-noble and passive-base, then the Eternal must be the former—and thus a bearer of accidents.

Imam Baqillani now presents a powerful argument against those who deny that Allah bears any accidents. He challenges them using their own method: he divides all existing things into two fundamental types. This division leads to a conclusion that the opponents cannot accept without abandoning their religion, but rejecting it would invalidate their own reasoning. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the authoritative tone and the logical structure.
The passage is a single, dense argument.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides existence into two types. Find the descriptions of each type and note what qualifies something as a bearer of accidents.
One type is described as active, noble, self-subsistent; the other as non-self-subsistent, non-active, base.
3
Follow the logical inference
See how the author applies this division to the Eternal (al-Qadim). Since the Eternal is active, noble, self-subsistent, He must belong to the first category and therefore be a bearer of accidents.
The conclusion also includes that the Eternal has spatial location (hayyiz) and occupation (shughl).
4
Consider the dilemma
The passage ends with a dilemma: if opponents accept the conclusion, they abandon their religion; if they reject it, they invalidate their own method of inference. Reflect on why this is a powerful rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم على سائر أدلتهم التي قدمنا ذكرها ما أنكرتم أن يكون حاملا للأعراض بمثل كل دليل ذكرتموه وذلك أنا وجدنا القديم الأشياء كلها على ضربين فضرب فعال شريف قائم بنفسه ليس بعرض وهو الحامل للأعراض وضرب آخر ليس قائما بنفسه ولا فعالا ولا شريفا وهو العرض فلما ثبت أن القديم فعال قائم بنفسه شريف ليس بخسيس ثبت أنه حامل للأعراض ذو حيز وشغل فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا دينهم وإن أبوه أبطلوا استدلالهم إبطالا ظاهرا
Then it is said to them concerning all their proofs which we have previously mentioned: Why do you deny that He is a bearer of accidents, by the like of every proof you have mentioned? That is because we found the Eternal, all things are of two types: a type that is active, noble, self-subsistent, not an accident, and it is the bearer of accidents; and another type that is not self-subsistent, not active, and not noble, and it is the accident. So when it is established that the Eternal is active, self-subsistent, noble, not base, it is established that He is a bearer of accidents, possessing spatial location and occupation. If they pass over that, they abandon their religion, and if they reject it, they invalidate their inference manifestly.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents another argument against those who deny that God is a bearer of accidents (hamil li-l-a'rad). The author challenges them: why do you deny that God bears accidents, using the same kind of proof you yourselves rely on? The argument is based on a division of all things into two categories: (1) active, noble, self-subsistent, not an accident, which is the bearer of accidents; (2) passive, base, non-self-subsistent, which is the accident. Since the Eternal is active, noble, self-subsistent, and not base, it follows that He is a bearer of accidents and therefore has spatial location (hayyiz) and occupation (shughl). If the opponents accept this, they abandon their religion (because it implies temporality); if they reject it, they clearly invalidate their own method of inference.

Why God’s Attributes Are Not Like the Trinity: Baqillani’s Reply
Baqillani distinguishes the Christian view from the Islamic view of divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the Christian analogy between divine attributes and the Trinity by showing the fundamental difference in how Muslims and Christians conceive the relationship between God and His attributes.

Core Idea
Muslims never claim agreement between God and His attributes; Christians do, which leads to contradictions.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani responds to a Christian attempt to draw an analogy between the Islamic view of God's attributes and the Christian Trinity. The Christian argues that if Muslims can say attributes are neither identical to nor different from God, then Christians can say the same about the substance and hypostases. Baqillani exposes the flaw in this analogy by highlighting that Muslims never posit any respect of agreement between God and His attributes, unlike Christians who claim agreement in substantiality. This lesson walks through his logical refutation and its significance for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian objection
Start by reading the opening question posed by the Christian interlocutor. Notice how they try to draw a parallel between the Islamic view of attributes and their own view of substance and hypostases.
2
Examine Baqillani’s first reply
Baqillani immediately points out that Muslims do not say attributes are 'other than' God, whereas Christians say the substance is other than the hypostases. This is a critical difference.
3
Consider the hypothetical argument
Even if Muslims hypothetically said God differs from His attributes in meaning, it would not lead to contradiction because Muslims never claim any respect of agreement. Christians, however, claim agreement in substantiality.
4
Analyze the Christian evasion
When Christians try to deny both agreement and disagreement, Baqillani forces a logical dilemma: either the substance takes the place of the hypostases (agreement) or not (disagreement). There is no middle ground.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Baqillani’s refutation preserves the transcendence of God and avoids the self-contradiction inherent in the Trinity doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال منهم قائل: أفليس قد قلتم أنتم في صفات الباري سبحانه إنها ليست بموافقة له ولا مخالفة له؟ فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون الجوهر غير موافق للأقانيم ولا مخالفا لها؟ قيل لهم: إنما سألناكم عن هذا لأجل قولكم إن الجوهر غير الأقانيم؛ ونحن فلا نقول إن الله سبحانه غير صفاته، فلا يلزم ما قلتم. وعلى أننا لو قلنا: إن الله تعالى مخالف لصفاته في معناها بمعنى أنه يجوز عليه ما يستحيل عليها وأنه لا يسد مسدها ولا ينوب منابها لم يدخل علينا مثل ما لزمكم من كون المتفق بنفسه مختلفاً بنفسه وكون جهة الاتفاق هي جهة الاختلاف لأننا لا نزعم أن الله سبحانه موافق لصفاته في جهة من الجهات وأنتم تزعمون أن الجوهر موافق للأقانيم بالجوهرية فإنه موافق لها بنفسه ومخالف لها أيضاً في القنومية بنفسه فشتان بين قولنا وقولكم.
وإن قالوا: لا نقول إنه موافق لها ولا مخالف لها، قيل لهم: فلا بد أن يسد مسدها فيوافقها أو لا يسد مسدها فيخالفها وهذا المعنى بنفسه هو الذي نعنيه بالاتفاق والاختلاف فلا معنى للمراوغة وإن قالوا: أليس لا يقال: إن يد الإنسان من الإنسان مخالفة له ولا موافقة له، وكذلك الواحد من
If a speaker among them says: Did you not yourselves say concerning the attributes of the Creator, exalted is He, that they are neither in agreement with Him nor in disagreement with Him? So why do you also deny that the substance is neither in agreement with the hypostases nor in disagreement with them? It is said to them: We only asked you about this because of your statement that the substance is other than the hypostases. As for us, we do not say that Allah, exalted is He, is other than His attributes, so what you said does not follow. Moreover, even if we said that Allah, most high, is in disagreement with His attributes in their meaning, in the sense that what is permissible for Him is impossible for them, and that He does not stand in their place nor take their position, there would not enter upon us what entered upon you of the thing being by itself both agreeing and differing, and of the respect of agreement being the respect of difference, because we do not claim that Allah, exalted is He, is in agreement with His attributes in any respect, whereas you claim that the substance is in agreement with the hypostases in substantiality, so it is in agreement with them by itself and also in disagreement with them in hypostaticity by itself. So how great is the difference between our statement and yours. And if they say: We do not say that it is in agreement with them nor in disagreement with them, it is said to them: Then it must either take their place and thus agree with them, or not take their place and thus disagree with them. This very meaning is what we intend by agreement and disagreement, so there is no point in evasion. And if they say: Is it not said that a person's hand is neither in disagreement with the person nor in agreement with him, and likewise the one from...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage addresses a Christian objection: since Muslims say God's attributes are neither identical to nor different from Him, why can't Christians say the same about the substance and hypostases? Baqillani replies that Muslims do not claim the attributes are other than God (the way Christians claim the substance is other than the hypostases). Even if Muslims hypothetically said God is different from His attributes (in meaning and rulings), that would not imply the same contradiction because Muslims never claim agreement in any respect, whereas Christians claim agreement in substantiality. Then he counters an attempted evasion: if they say the substance is neither agreeing nor differing, he argues that logically it must either function as the hypostases (agree) or not (differ), and this is what is meant by agreement and difference. The final incomplete statement suggests an analogy with a hand and a person.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the Christian analogy by showing that Muslims never claim any respect of agreement between God and His attributes, thus avoiding the logical contradictions inherent in the Trinity.

The Christian analogy falters because it claims both agreement and disagreement in the same respect. How can you cultivate a deeper conviction that they are not other than Him, and let that reshape your worship and reliance on Allah alone?

How will you ensure your heart submits to the truth, not just your mind?

Refuting the Christian Objection on Attributes and Hypostases
Baqillani refutes the Christian analogy between divine attributes and Trinitarian hypostases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the Christian analogy between divine attributes and Trinitarian hypostases is logically flawed and does not apply to the Muslim position.

Core Idea
The Christian Trinity involves a logical contradiction: substance is both identical and distinct in the same respect.

In this passage, Baqillani responds to a Christian interlocutor who tries to equate the Muslim position on divine attributes (neither identical nor distinct) to the Trinitarian doctrine of substance and hypostases. Baqillani shows that the Christian view entails a self-contradiction that the Muslim position avoids. This lesson examines his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This is a dialectical exchange — an objection followed by a response.
2
Identify the Christian objection
The objector says: 'You say attributes are neither identical nor distinct from God. So why can't we say the substance is neither identical nor distinct from the hypostases?' Note what the Christian is trying to do: create a parallel.
3
Understand Baqillani's first response
Baqillani first clarifies that Muslims do not say God is 'other than' His attributes. Therefore the Christian analogy does not apply from the start.
This is a quick refutation, but Baqillani goes further.
4
Grasp Baqillani's second, stronger argument
Even if Muslims had said God is distinct from His attributes (in meaning), it would not lead to the contradiction that the Christian position entails. Why? Because Muslims do not claim identity in any respect, while Christians claim the substance is both identical (in substance-hood) and distinct (in hypostasis-hood) in the same respect.
This is the core of the refutation.
5
Recognize the key difference
The Christian position has the absurdity of the same thing being identical and different in the same respect. The Muslim position on attributes avoids this because it does not posit identity-in-any-respect.
Baqillani ends: 'How great is the difference between our statement and yours.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال منهم قائل : أفليس قد قلتم أنتم في صفات الباري سبحانه إنها ليست بموافقة له ولا مخالفة له؟ فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون الجوهر غير موافق للأقانيم ولا مخالفا لها؟ قيل لهم : إنما سألناكم عن هذا لأجل قولكم إن الجوهر غير الأقانيم ؛ ونحن فلا نقول إن الله سبحانه غير صفاته، فلا يلزم ما قلتم . وعلى أننا لو قلنا : إن الله تعالى مخالف لصفاته في معناها بمعنى أنه يجوز عليه ما يستحيل عليها وأنه لا يسد مسدها ولا ينوب منابها لم يدخل علينا مثل ما لزمكم من كون المتفق بنفسه مختلفاً بنفسه وكون جهة الاتفاق هي جهة الاختلاف لأننا لا نزعم أن الله سبحانه موافق لصفاته في جهة من الجهات وأنتم تزعمون أن الجوهر موافق للأقانيم بالجوهرية فإنه موافق لها بنفسه ومخالف لها أيضاً في القنومية بنفسه فشتان بين قولنا وقولكم.
If an objector among them says: 'Did you not say concerning the attributes of the Exalted Creator that they are neither identical to Him nor distinct from Him? So why do you also deny that the substance is neither identical to the hypostases nor distinct from them?' It is said to them: 'We only asked you about this because of your claim that the substance is other than the hypostases; we do not say that God is other than His attributes, so what you said does not apply. Moreover, if we did say that God is distinct from His attributes in their meaning, in the sense that what is possible for Him is impossible for them, and that He does not substitute for them nor act in their stead, that would not entail for us what you have been required to accept: that something is identical in itself and distinct in itself, and that the aspect of identity is the aspect of distinction, because we do not claim that God is identical to His attributes in any respect, while you claim that the substance is identical to the hypostases in its substance-hood; it is identical to them in itself and also distinct from them in its hypostasis-hood in itself. How great is the difference between our statement and yours.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation in the form of an objection-and-answer. The Christian objector tries to apply the Muslim position on divine attributes (that they are neither identical nor distinct) to the Trinity, arguing that the divine substance is likewise neither identical nor distinct from the three hypostases. Baqillani responds by first clarifying that the Muslim position does not assert a 'distinction' between God and His attributes in the way Christians assert between substance and hypostases. He then argues that even if Muslims had held such a distinction, it would not lead to the absurdity of the same thing being both identical and distinct in the same respect, because Muslims do not affirm identity in any respect, whereas Christians claim the substance is identical to the hypostases in its substance-hood and distinct in its hypostasis-hood. This self-contradiction is unique to the Christian doctrine.

Key Takeaway

?

?
The Christian objection tries to compare Muslim attributes to Trinity. But Baqillani exposes the contradiction in their claim. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold two beliefs that secretly contradict each other? Would you be willing to have them tested by the same rigorous logic?
?
Baqillani's argument forces the Christian to face the absurdity of saying 'identical and distinct in the same respect.' When you reflect on your own understanding of God, is there any respect in which you unintentionally imply that God is both identical to and distinct from something else (like His creation, His will, or His attributes)? What would it mean for you to abandon that contradiction?

Rewarding without action and the distinction from lying
Baqillani answers the objection that God cannot thank the undeserving by distinguishing favor from lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God's bestowal of favor without prior action is not lying, because lying is impossible for God while beneficence is permissible.

Core Idea
God cannot lie, but He can give favors freely.

In this passage, Baqillani responds to an objection that would make God's favoritism seem absurd. The objectors argue that if God can reward without merit, He could thank the undeserving more than the righteous, which they think is impossible. Baqillani clarifies the distinction between lying (which God cannot do) and beneficence (which He can do freely), thereby refuting the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objectors' claim that God cannot thank the undeserving because it would be absurd. Notice the assumption they are making.
2
Spot the first distinction
Baqillani points out that thanking someone for what they didn't do is lying, which is impossible for God because truthfulness is His attribute.
3
Spot the second distinction
Then he argues that giving favor (tafaḍḍul) to someone without merit is not lying; it is beneficence, which is permissible.
4
Understand the refutation
The objection fails because it confuses lying with beneficence. Therefore, God's favoritism is not absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لو حَسُنَ من الله ما قلتم لحَسُنَ من الله أن يُشكر ويُثنى على مَنْ لم يعمل شيئاً أو مَنْ قَلَّ فِعْلُ البِرِّ منه بأكثر مما يَشْكُرُ ويُثْنِي على العامل الزاهد المجتهد، قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون الفرق بينهما أن الشكر والثناء على المرء بما لم يكن منه كَذِبٌ ؛ والكَذِب مستحيل على الله تعالى إذ كان الصدق من صفات نفسه، ولغير ذلك كما يستحيل عليه الجهل والعجز؟ والتفضُلُ على من لم يعمل أو على من عمل أقل من عمل غيره بأكثر من التفضل على العامل إنعام وإحسان وليس ذلك بقبيح ولا من الكذب بسبيل فَبطل ما قلتم.
If they say: 'If what you said were acceptable from God, then it would be acceptable from God that He thanks and praises one who did nothing, or one whose deed of righteousness is little, more than He thanks and praises the diligent, ascetic worker.' It is said to them: 'Why did you say that?' Then it is said to them: 'What prevents you from recognizing that the difference between the two is that thanking and praising a person for what he did not do is lying; and lying is impossible for God, since truthfulness is an attribute of His Self, and for other reasons just as ignorance and inability are impossible for Him? And favoring one who did nothing or who did less than another's deed, with more favor than on the worker, is bestowal and beneficence, and that is not ugly, nor is it in the category of lying. Therefore what you said is false.'
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objectors argue that if God's favoritism is acceptable, then it would also be acceptable for God to thank someone who did no good deeds more than a diligent worshiper, which they consider absurd. Baqillani answers by distinguishing between two types of actions: thanking and praising someone for what they did not do is lying, which is impossible for God because truthfulness is His attribute. However, bestowing favor (tafaḍḍul) on someone without merit is not lying; it is simply generosity. Therefore, the objection fails because it confuses lying (which is impossible for God) with beneficence (which is permissible).

Key Terms
شكر
ثناء
كذب
صدق
تفضل
إنعام
إحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot give special favors to the undeserving.
Correction: Baqillani says God cannot lie, but He can bestow favors freely.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say that God cannot thank or praise someone for what they did not do?
A) Because it would be unfair.
B) Because it would be lying, and lying is impossible for God.
C) Because God only thanks those who have done good deeds.
D) Because such an action would be foolish.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by distinguishing between lying (impossible for God) and beneficence (permissible), showing that God's free favor is not absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do good deeds, do you ever feel entitled to God's special reward? How does Baqillani's point that God can favor without reason challenge your assumption that your actions obligate Him? Will you continue to worship even if you feel 'unrewarded'?
?
Are there times when you have exaggerated your own good deeds to gain praise from others? How does the fact that God cannot lie about you (nor can you truly lie to Him) make you reconsider your speech about yourself? Ask yourself: 'Do I speak truthfully even when it diminishes me in people's eyes?'
?
Consider someone you know who seems to receive blessings despite little apparent piety. Do you feel jealousy or resentment? How does knowing that God's favor is a free gift (not a wage) help you accept His distribution with contentment? What step will you take today to combat envy in your heart?

Refutation of the Objection of Unjust Differences in Creation
Baqillani refutes the claim that God's varied creation is unjust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that God's creation of differences among people is unjust by appealing to the concept of benefit (maslaha) and consistency with prophetic missions.

Core Idea
God's differences in creation are not unjust—they are part of a beneficial plan.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a common objection: if God is just, why does He create some people with knowledge and health and others with ignorance and disabilities? The opponents argue this is unjust preference. Baqillani turns their reasoning against them, showing that if differences can be for a greater benefit, then sending prophets and appointing leaders is equally justified. This lesson unpacks his sophisticated argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: objection, hypothetical response, and counter.
The passage is a continuous logical argument.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and defense
Find where the opponents state that giving different abilities to some and not others is a benefit. Then see Baqillani's response.
The opponents concede that differences could be for benefit.
3
Understand Baqillani's counter-argument
Baqillani applies the same logic of benefit to the sending of messengers and shepherds. If the opponents accept benefit in one case, they must accept it in the other.
This shows consistency and refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم أيضاً ما أنكرتم من أنه لا يجوز في عدل القديم سبحانه وحكمته على موضوع دليلكم، أن يخلق في بعض عباده الجهل وفي بعضهم العلم وفي بعضهم العمى والبكم والخرس والزمانة وفي بعضهم القوة والتمكين وصحة الآلة وكمال العقل والنحيزة الطبيعية لأن ذلك تفضيل بعض الجنس على بعض. فإن قالوا: عطيته العلم والحياة وكمال العقل والحواس لبعضهم ومنعه لغيرهم مصلحة للمعطى والممنوع وسبيل لهم إلى نفع عظيم هو سبحانه أعلم به، قيل لهم: فما أنكرتم أن يكون إرساله بعض الخلق وجعله راعيا وجعل باقيهم رعية مصلحة للراعي والرعية وللرسول والمرسل إليه ولطفا لهم في النظر في حجج العقول التي أمرهم بالرجوع إليها والعمل على موجبها.
And it is also said to them: What objection do you have that it is not permissible in the justice of the Eternal and His wisdom, based on the subject of your proof, that He creates in some of His servants ignorance and in some knowledge, and in some blindness, muteness, deafness, and chronic illness, and in some strength, capability, soundness of tools, completeness of intellect, and natural disposition? Because that is preference of some of the kind over others. If they say: His giving of knowledge, life, completeness of intellect and senses to some and withholding from others is a benefit for the giver and the withheld, and a means for them to a great benefit which He knows best, it is said to them: What objection do you have that His sending some of creation and making them shepherds, and making the rest a flock, is a benefit for the shepherd and the flock, and for the messenger and the one to whom he is sent, and a grace for them in considering the rational proofs which He commanded them to return to and act upon their necessary consequence?
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a second objection and its refutation. The author argues that if the opponents consider it unjust for God to create differences in abilities—such as knowledge, blindness, etc.—then they must also object to any variation among humans. The opponents might respond that such variations are beneficial (maslaha) and a means to a great benefit. The author counters that sending messengers and appointing some as shepherds over others is likewise a benefit. Thus, the objection is inconsistent: if they accept variation for benefit, they must also accept sending messengers for the same reason.

Key Terms
عدل
حكمة
خلق
جهل
علم
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's creation of inequalities (e.g., some healthy, some disabled) is unjust and incompatible with divine wisdom.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such differences are not unjust but serve a greater benefit (maslaha) known to God, and accepting this logic also justifies sending prophets and leaders.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Baqillani refute the objection that God's creation of differences in abilities is unjust?
A) He denies that God creates differences
B) He says it is for the benefit of all, so sending messengers is similarly beneficial
C) He says it is arbitrary
D) He says it is impossible

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that God's diverse creation is not unjust but serves greater benefits, including the sending of messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt resentment toward God because of the unequal circumstances of your life compared to others? How does this lesson challenge you to see those differences as part of a wise plan? Will you now thank Him for His hidden benefits?
?
When you encounter someone with a severe disability or chronic illness, do you silently question God's justice? Commit to viewing such differences as possible means of grace—for them and for you. What one change will you make in your thoughts about suffering today?
?
If you are a leader, teacher, or parent, do you ever resent the responsibility placed on you while others have less? The lesson shows that being a 'shepherd' is a benefit designed for you and your flock. Will you accept this role as a gift from God and fulfill it with gratitude?

Refuting Anthropomorphic Inferences about God
Baqillani refutes the claim that God cannot be described as a thing or agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the claim that describing God as a 'thing' or 'agent' necessarily implies He is like created bodies, substances, or accidents.

Core Idea
Describing God as a 'thing' does not make Him like created things.

This passage presents a classic kalam argument. Baqillani's opponent claims that if God is called a 'thing' or 'agent', then He must be like created things—bodies, substances, or accidents. Baqillani shows that this inference is flawed; the opponent's own logic leads to a contradiction. We'll read the original Arabic and unpack the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Baqillani begins with the opponent's claim.
The Arabic uses condensed style; translation helps clarify.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
The opponent argues that if God is a thing (shay'), agent (fā'il), knowing, etc., then He must belong to the genus of intelligible things, which in the witnessed world are only bodies, substances, or accidents. Find this in the text.
The opponent relies on empirical observation (al-shāhid).
3
See Baqillani's refutation move
Baqillani does not directly deny the opponent's premise but uses it to show that if the opponent's reasoning were necessary, it would force them to accept that God is a body, which they deny. The phrase 'if all this is not necessary' is key.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Understand the logical structure
The opponent's claim (that describing God as a thing forces anthropomorphism) leads to a false conclusion. Therefore, the opponent's premise is not necessary and the argument collapses.
The refutation does not require Baqillani to define what 'thing' means for God; it only shows the opponent's inference fails.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
This lesson shows a key principle in kalam: God is not comparable to creation. The terms used for God (thing, agent, etc.) do not imply similarity in essence.
This protects tawhid without denying that God has attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوعكم ألا يكون القديم سبحانه شيئاً ولا فاعلا ولا عالماً حياً قادراً لأن ذلك يوجب أن يكون من جنس الأشياء المعقولة لأن الشيء في الشاهد والوجود لا ينفك من أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً والحي العالم القادر لا يكون إلا جسماً وجواهر مجتمعة والفاعل منا لا يفعل إلا في نفسه أو في غيره بسبب يُحْدِثُهُ في نفسه فإن لم يجب هذا أجمع، سقط ما تعلقتم به .
Your claim is that the Eternal (al-qadīm), glorified be He, is not a thing (shay'), nor an agent (fā'il), nor knowing ('ālim), living (ḥayy), powerful (qādir), because that would require Him to be of the genus of intelligible things, because a thing in the witnessed world and existence is not free from being a body (jism), a substance (jawhar), or an accident ('araḍ), and the living, knowing, powerful is only a body and combined substances, and the agent among us only acts in himself or in others by a cause (sabab) he produces in himself. If all this is not necessary, your attachment falls.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim. The opponent argued that if we describe the Eternal (God) as a thing, agent, knowing, etc., then He would be like created things, because in the empirical world, any thing is either a body, substance, or accident, and any living, knowing, powerful being is a body or composition of substances. Baqillani turns the argument by showing that the opponent's reasoning leads to a false conclusion: if the opponent's premise were true, then God would indeed be like created things, but since God is not like that, the premise must be rejected. The phrase 'إن لم يجب هذا أجمع' (if all this is not necessary) indicates that the opponent's own logic forces them to either accept that God is a body or abandon their claim. The argument is a classic kalam refutation using the taghāyur (difference) between God and creation.

Key Terms
قديم
شيء
فاعل
عالم
حي
قادر
جسم
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Describing God as a 'thing' or 'agent' necessarily implies He is a created body, substance, or accident.
Correction: The term 'thing' in kalam applies to any existing entity, including God, without implying composition or similarity to creation. The opponent's inference is not logically necessary.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the consequence if the opponent's claim that God cannot be described as a thing or agent is accepted?
A) God would be composed of parts.
B) God would not exist.
C) God would become unknowable.
D) The opponent's attachment to that claim falls.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes anthropomorphic inferences by showing that the opponent's reasoning is not necessary and thus their argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you unconsciously project human limitations onto Him? What specific assumption from your own life are you most tempted to apply to God? Write down one such assumption and commit to questioning it this week.
?
Are you willing to admit that your intellect cannot fully categorise God? How does it feel to realise that your reasoning might be based on false necessities? Pray that God grants you humility in acknowledging His transcendence.
?
If you are honest, do you sometimes reject descriptions of God because they seem 'too human'? How can you worship a God whose attributes are real yet unlike anything you experience? Ask God to increase your reliance on revelation over your own analogies.

Divine Speech Is Unlike Human Speech
Baqillani begins rebuttal: the unique nature of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the unique nature of divine speech—its complete dissimilarity to created speech—is the first proof that a prophet knows the speech is from God.

Core Idea
Divine speech is unlike any created speech—eternal, not created.

Baqillani begins his rebuttal of the skeptics by stating that their entire objection collapses. He then presents the foundational insight: a prophet like Moses recognizes divine speech not by any resemblance to human talk, but precisely because it is utterly unlike any created speech. This lesson examines that first aspect and its implications for the divine attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: Baqillani first declares the skeptics' argument falls, then gives the reason through Moses, then lists four aspects, and elaborates only the first.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key claim about divine speech
Focus on the phrase 'ليس من جنس كلام الآدميين ولا مشبه لكلام المخلوقين'. This is the core of the first aspect. Note that Baqillani also states that divine speech is eternal (قديم) and an attribute (صفة), not created.
This directly counters the Mu'tazilite view that the Quran is created.
3
Understand the context of the argument
Ask yourself: Why does Baqillani insist divine speech is unlike any created speech? Because if it were like human speech, it would be created and contingent, but the established proof in kalam is that God's speech is eternal. That uniqueness makes it identifiable.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari doctrine of divine attributes.
4
Reflect on the promise for further clarification
At the end, Baqillani says he will clarify this further in the chapter on attributes. This shows the argument is part of a larger systematic discussion.
The passage is thus both a rebuttal and a preview.
5
Take the checkpoint quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding. It directly asks about the first aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم : ما أنكرتم من سقوط ما تعلقتم به؟ وذلك أن موسى عليه السلام وكل من تولى الله خطابه بلا واسطة ولا ترجمان يعلم أن خالق العالم هو المتولي لخطابه من أربعة أوجه : أحدها أن كلام الله سبحانه الذي يخاطب به من يشاء من خلقه ليس من جنس كلام الآدميين ولا مشبه لكلام المخلوقين بل هو لسائر الأجناس والأصوات وأبنية اللغات، وإن كان مسموعاً بحاسة السمع لما قام عندنا من الدليل على قدمه واستحالة خلقه وأنه صفة المتكلم به وسنوضح ذلك بما يوضح الحق في باب القول في الصفات
It is said to them: What do you deny of the fall of what you clung to? That is because Moses, peace be upon him, and everyone whom God undertakes to address without intermediary or interpreter knows that the Creator of the world is the one undertaking his address, from four aspects: One of them is that the speech of God (kalām Allāh), glorified be He, with which He addresses whom He wills of His creation, is not of the genus of human speech nor similar to the speech of creatures, but rather it is unlike all kinds, voices, and structures of languages, even if it is heard by the sense of hearing, because of the proof that has been established with us concerning its eternity (qidam) and the impossibility of its being created (khalq), and that it is an attribute (ṣifa) of the one who speaks it. And we will clarify that with what clarifies the truth in the chapter on attributes.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of Baqillani's refutation of the skeptics' objection. He asserts that the skeptics' argument collapses (squṭ). Then he states that Moses and any direct recipient of divine speech know that it is from God through four aspects. He only provides the first here: the divine speech is unlike any created speech. It is not of the genus of human speech, not similar to the speech of creatures, but rather it transcends all kinds, voices, and linguistic structures. Although it is heard by the ear, it is eternal (qadīm) and not created, and it is an attribute of God. This unique quality makes it identifiable. Baqillani promises to clarify this further in the chapter on attributes. The argument relies on the established proof of the eternity of divine speech, a key Ash'ari doctrine.

Key Terms
كلام الله
قدم
خلق
صفة
متكلم
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine speech is essentially like human speech, just louder or more powerful, or that it is a created sound.
Correction: Baqillani emphasizes that divine speech is not of the genus of human speech at all—it is unlike all created speech in its essence, though it may be heard. It is eternal, not created, and an attribute of God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first aspect by which a prophet knows that the speech he hears is from God?
A) The speech is loud and clear.
B) The speech is unlike any human or created speech.
C) The speech comes from a visible angel.
D) The speech includes a miracle.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is uniquely unlike any created speech, proving it is from God and not a mere human phenomenon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly sense that you are engaging with speech that is unlike any human speech, eternal and from the Creator? If not, what holds you back from that awareness?
?
If you were to internalize that God's speech is His eternal attribute, not a created thing, how would that change the way you approach verses that challenge your desires? Name one specific verse where you will apply this understanding today.

First Refutation: The Unique Nature of Divine Speech
Baqillani introduces the first aspect: God's speech is fundamentally different from created speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that prophets recognize God's speech through its unique, pre-eternal nature.

Core Idea
God's speech is unlike any created speech, revealing its divine origin.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani begins his refutation by explaining that when God directly addresses a prophet, the prophet can recognize the speaker as God through four aspects. The first aspect is the distinctive nature of divine speech itself. This lesson explores that foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument. Notice the structure: a refutation of those who deny that prophets can recognize divine speech.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first aspect
The passage mentions four aspects but elaborates only the first. Find where it says 'one of them is that...' and understand that this is the primary reason.
The first aspect is the nature of God's speech itself.
3
Understand the nature of divine speech
God's speech is not like human speech; it is pre-eternal, not created, and an attribute of God. Although it is heard, it is fundamentally different from any created speech.
This distinction is crucial for recognizing the speaker.
4
Connect to prophecy
Because the speech is unique, the person addressed (like Moses) knows the speaker is the Pre-eternal God, to the exclusion of all others.
This validates the prophet's direct experience with God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم: ما أنكرتم من سقوط ما تعلقتم به؟ وذلك أن موسى عليه السلام وكل من تولى الله خطابه بلا واسطة ولا ترجمان يعلم أن خالق العالم هو المتولي لخطابه من أربعة أوجه: أحدها أن كلام الله سبحانه الذي يخاطب به من يشاء من خلقه ليس من جنس كلام الآدميين ولا مشبه لكلام المخلوقين بل هو لسائر الأجناس والأصوات وأبنية اللغات، وإن كان مسموعاً بحاسة السمع لما قام عندنا من الدليل على قدمه واستحالة خلقه وأنه صفة المتكلم به وسنوضح ذلك بما يوضح الحق في باب القول في الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى. وإذا كان ذلك كذلك، علم من تولى الله خطابه أن المتكلم له بما سمعه هو القديم الذي ليس كمثله شيء وأنه الذي ينبغي أن يكون ما سمعه كلاماً له دون سائر الخلق.
So it is said to them: What do you deny of the collapse of what you have clung to? That is because Moses, peace be upon him, and everyone whom God undertakes to address without an intermediary or interpreter knows that the Creator of the world is the one undertaking His address from four aspects: One of them is that the speech of God, Exalted, with which He addresses whomever He wills of His creation, is not of the kind of human speech, nor similar to the speech of created beings; rather it is of a different kind of types, sounds, and structures of languages, even though it is heard by the sense of hearing, because of the proof established with us of its pre-eternity and the impossibility of its being created, and that it is an attribute of the speaker, and we will clarify that with what clarifies the truth in the chapter on attributes, God willing. And if that is the case, then the one whom God undertakes to address knows that the speaker to him with what he heard is the Pre-eternal, the One like whom there is nothing, and that He is the one whose speech what he heard should be, to the exclusion of the rest of creation.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the refutation. It introduces a principle: anyone whom God addresses directly (like Moses) can know the speaker is God from four aspects. The first aspect is the nature of God's speech itself. God's speech is not like human speech; it is pre-eternal, not created, and is an attribute of God. Although heard, it is fundamentally different from any created speech. Therefore, the person addressed recognizes that the speaker is the Pre-eternal God, whose speech uniquely befits Him. The author promises to clarify this further in the discussion on attributes.

Key Terms
كلام الله
قديم
صفة
مشبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's speech is like human speech.
Correction: This passage emphasizes it is fundamentally different: pre-eternal, not created, and an attribute of God.

Checkpoint
According to this refutation, how does a prophet know the speech he hears is from God?
A) The speech is delivered by an angel.
B) The speech is in a human language.
C) The speech is unlike any created speech.
D) The speech is accompanied by a miracle.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is pre-eternal and unlike creation, enabling prophets to recognize divine address.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Moses recognized God's speech instantly because it was unlike any human voice. When you recite or hear the Qur'an, do you treat it as ordinary words, or do you pause to sense its otherness? What would need to change in your heart for you to approach the Qur'an as the unique, pre-eternal speech of God?
?
If you truly believed that God could speak to you directly, with speech that is not of this world, how would your life today be different? Would you listen with more urgency? Would you obey more readily? What prevents you from living with that awareness now?

Baqillani's Proof on Divine Permission to Inflict Pain and Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Objection
Baqillani argues God can inflict pain without compensation, using consensus and logical necessity, and refutes the death objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God may initiate pain in animals without compensation, based on consensus and the distinction between grace and obligation, and how he refutes the alternative of causing death.

Core Idea
God's justice is defined by His ownership, not by human standards of fairness.

Baqillani tackles a classic Mu'tazilite objection: how can a just God cause pain to animals without reason? In this passage, he grounds his answer in two pillars: the consensus that God is gracious (not obligated) and the logical necessity that withholding pleasure from a living being entails pain. He then addresses the alternative suggestion that God could simply cause death instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening objection
Read the first sentence where the Mu'tazilites ask for proof that God can inflict pain without compensation. This frames the entire discussion.
Notice the terms 'without compensation' (ghayri 'iwad) and 'no preceding sin' (la jurm taqaddama).
2
Understand the consensus argument
Baqillani cites consensus that God is gracious to animals. Understand how he uses this to establish that grace is voluntary, not obligatory.
The key distinction is between tafaddul (grace) and mustahaqq wajib (obligatory entitlement).
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: God can withhold pleasure → animal cannot be free of opposites → withholding pleasure causes pain → that pain is just from God but unjust from us.
Pay attention to the phrase 'justice and rightness in wisdom' (adlan wa sawaban fi al-hikma).
4
Examine the death objection and refutation
The Mu'tazilites suggest God could cause death instead of pain. Baqillani responds by distinguishing pleasure with life from pleasure without life.
This refutation hinges on the fact that grace is tied to life, not death.
5
Conclude with the implication
The final sentence establishes God's absolute ownership: He may permit destruction and pain to animals without any creature's objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: فما الدليل على أن الله سبحانه ابتدأ الحيوان بالآلام من غير عوض ولا جرم تقدم؟ قيل لهم: الدليل على ذلك اتفاقنا وسائر أهل التوحيد وأهل الملل على أن الله تعالى متفضل على الحيوان بالنعم واللذات التي يبتدئهم بها وأنه مستوجب للحمد والشكر على ذلك وإذا كان هذا هكذا وكان للمتفضل فعل التفضل وله تركه على وجه ما كان له فعله، وأن هذا هو الفرق بين التفضل وبين المستحق الواجب الذي يجب الظلم بتركه ثبت أن الله سبحانه أن يترك فعل اللذة في الحيوان على وجه ما كان له فعلها فإذا ثبت ذلك وكان الدليل قد قام على أن الحيوان المحتمل للذات والآلام المتضادة لا يجوز أن ينفك منها بأسرها كما لا يجوز أن تنفك الأجسام من سائر المتضادات ثبت أن الله سبحانه إذا ترك فعل اللذة في الحيوان حسن منه ذلك وكان عدلاً وصواباً في الحكمة، ولن يترك الله اللذة إلا بما يضادها من الألم، وذلك يوجب أن يكون فعل الألم بغير جرم ولا لعوض عدلاً الله سبحانه، وإن كان مثله ظلماً وجوراً منا إذا كلفنا تركه وأمرنا من هو أملك بالحيوان منا بترك إيلامه. فإن قالوا أو قال إخوانهم من المعتزلة ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله سبحانه ترك التفضل من اللذة بفعل الموت النافي للألم واللذات وليس له ذلك بفعل الألم؟ وقيل لهم: أنكرنا ذلك لأجل ما اتفقنا عليه من متفضل بفعل اللذة في الجسم مع وجود الحياة لا مع عدمها فيجب أن يكون له ترك فعل اللذة على الوجه الذي كان له فعلها وله فعلها مع الحياة فيجب أن يكون له تركها مع الحياة ولن يترك اللذة مع وجود الحياة إلا بفعل الألم وإذا كان ذلك كذلك سقط ما سألتم عنه وبطل ما تعلقتم به وثبت أن لمالك الأعيان أن يبيح خلقه ما شاء منها من إتلاف بعض الحيوان وإيلامه وأنه لا اعتراض لمخلوق في حكمه.
Then they say: What is the proof that God Most High initiated animals with pains without compensation or preceding sin? It is said to them: The proof of that is our agreement and the rest of the people of tawhid and the people of religions that God Most High is gracious to animals with the blessings and pleasures with which He initiates them, and that He is deserving of praise and thanks for that. And since this is the case, and the gracious one has the act of grace and its abandonment in the manner in which He had the power to do it, and that this is the difference between grace and obligatory entitlement whose abandonment would be injustice, it is established that God Most High may abandon the act of pleasure in animals in the manner in which He had the power to do it. So when this is established, and the proof has been established that an animal subject to opposing pleasures and pains cannot be free of them all just as bodies cannot be free of all opposites, it is established that if God Most High abandons the act of pleasure in the animal, that is good from Him and is justice and rightness in wisdom. And God will not abandon pleasure except by what opposes it of pain. And that requires that the act of pain without prior sin or compensation is justice from God Most High, even if the like of that would be injustice and tyranny from us if we are tasked with abandoning it and commanded by one who is more owning of the animal than us to abandon inflicting pain on it. Then if they say—or their brothers from the Mu'tazila say: What prevents you from admitting that God Most High abandoned the grace of pleasure by causing death, which negates both pain and pleasures, and He does not have that by causing pain? It is said to them: We deny that because of what we have agreed upon, that the gracious one has the act of pleasure in the body with the existence of life, not with its absence. So it must be that He has the abandonment of the act of pleasure in the same manner in which He had the power to do it—and He has the power to do it with life—so He must have the abandonment of it with life. And He will not abandon pleasure with the existence of life except by causing pain. And when that is the case, what you asked about falls away, and what you clung to is invalidated, and it is established that the Owner of entities may permit His creation whatever He wishes from it of destroying some animals and inflicting pain on them, and there is no objection for any creature against His judgment.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage contains a proof and an objection-answer. It is an argument that God may initiate pain in animals without compensation. Baqillani begins with an objection: what proof do you have that God initiates animals with pain without compensation or preceding sin? His answer: consensus (of Muslims and other religions) that God is gracious to animals and deserves praise. Since grace is voluntary, God may choose to give or withhold it. The difference between grace and obligation is that withholding obligation would be injustice, but withholding grace is not. Therefore, God may withhold pleasure from animals. Since an animal cannot be free of both pleasure and pain (like bodies cannot be free of opposites), if God withholds pleasure, pain necessarily follows. That pain, without prior sin or compensation, is just from God, though similar from us would be unjust. Then a second objection from the Mu'tazila: God could avoid pain by causing death instead, which removes both pain and pleasure. Baqillani answers: grace consists of pleasure with life, not with death. Since God has the power to give pleasure with life, He also has the power to withhold it with life. Withholding pleasure with life necessarily results in pain. Therefore, the objection fails, and it is established that God may permit the destruction and pain of animals without any creature objecting.

Key Terms
تفضل
مستحق واجب
عدل
ظلم
متضادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani denies God's justice by allowing Him to inflict pain without cause.
Correction: Baqillani redefines justice as what the owner does with His property. Since God is the absolute owner, His actions are just by definition, not subject to human standards.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that the Mu'tazilite objection—that God could cause death instead of pain—fails?
A) Death is a greater harm than pain
B) Grace consists of pleasure with life, not with death
C) Death would also be unjust
D) Animals cannot experience death

Key Takeaway
God's justice is determined by His absolute ownership; He may inflict pain on animals without compensation because He is the ultimate owner, and His actions cannot be questioned by creatures.

What would it take for you to truly submit to God's absolute ownership over your comfort?
?
Baqillani says that what would be injustice from us (inflicting pain without cause) is justice from God because He is the Owner. Are there areas in your life where you judge God's actions by human standards? Can you name one specific trial where you felt God was 'unfair'? How does this lesson challenge that feeling?

Baqillani's Refutation of Badā' (Change of Mind) in God
Baqillani refutes badā' by a dilemma and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational argument against the doctrine of badā' and why it implies a contradiction in God's knowledge and commands.

Core Idea
Badā' implies a change in God's knowledge, which is impossible.

Baqillani confronts a controversial doctrine held by some sects: that God can change His mind (badā'). To refute it, he presents a logical dilemma. If badā' is denied, the opponents abandon their creed; if affirmed, it leads to contradictions about God's knowledge and commands. This passage shows the strength of rational argument in defending divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Focus on the structure of the argument.
The opening phrase is incomplete; it likely continues from the previous page.
2
Identify the two options in the dilemma
Baqillani presents a question: is badā' a revision of knowledge or not? Then he gives two possible answers and their consequences.
Note the list of actions that would constitute badā'.
3
Analyze the refutation of the second option
When opponents claim badā' is justified by changing benefit, Baqillani applies the same logic to divine commands, showing inconsistency.
This is the crux of the argument.
4
Reflect on the argument's effectiveness
Consider why the analogy with commands leaves no answer. How does this preserve God's perfection?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس أحواله وأوصافه فيقال لهم: البداء من الله تعالى استدراك علم غير ما فعله، ونقض ما ألفه، وفرق ما جمعه، وحرك ما سكنه، وأحيا ما أماته، أم لا بداء؟ فإن قالوا: لا بداء، تركوا دينهم واعتلالهم، وإن قالوا: البداء ولكن لأن النقض في وقت مصلحة للمكلفين، والتأليف مصلحة في غيره، وكذلك التحريك والتسكين، قيل لهم: مثله في الأمر بالشيء وقت والنهي عن مثله في غيره، ولا جواب لهم عن شيء من ذلك.
Not his states and attributes, so it is said to them: Is 'badā'' from God Most High a revision of knowledge of other than what He did, and an annulment of what He established, and a separation of what He united, and a movement of what He stilled, and a revival of what He caused to die, or is there no badā'? If they say: No badā', they abandon their religion and their belief. But if they say: Badā' exists, but because annulment at one time is a benefit for those charged (al-mukallafīn), and establishment is a benefit at another time, and likewise movement and stilling, it is said to them: The same applies to commanding a thing at one time and forbidding its like at another time. And they have no answer to any of that.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation of the doctrine of badā' (change of mind) attributed to God by some sects. Baqillani begins by presenting the dilemma: either badā' is a revision of God's knowledge, implying He learns something new, or it is not. He lists actions that would constitute badā' (annulling, separating, moving, reviving) and asks whether these are badā' or not. If the opponents deny badā', they contradict their own creed. If they affirm it but claim it is based on changing benefit (maṣlaḥa) for humans, then Baqillani retorts that the same logic applies to divine commands and prohibitions: God can command something at one time and forbid it later for the same reason. Thus their position leads to an inconsistency they cannot resolve.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think badā' is a sign of God's wisdom in adapting to circumstances, reflecting His perfect governance.
Correction: Badā' actually implies ignorance and change in God, which contradicts His perfection. Baqillani shows it leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
What is the main outcome of Baqillani's argument against badā'?
A) It proves that badā' is necessary for divine wisdom.
B) It shows that affirming badā' leads to contradictions about God's knowledge and commands.
C) It confirms that badā' occurs only for prophets.
D) It denies that God can change His actions at all.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dilemma shows that the doctrine of badā' is logically incoherent because it either denies a core belief or leads to contradictions about God's knowledge and commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would Baqillani's analogy about commanding and forbidding at different times challenge that assumption? What will you do to check your heart against this subtle form of badā'?
?
Baqillani says the opponents 'have no answer' to his argument. Do you truly believe that God's knowledge is complete and unchangeable? If you do, how does that affect the way you pray, plan, and trust in God's promises? What is one area of your life where you need to surrender the illusion of God 'changing His mind'?

Why 'Thing' Applies but 'Body' Does Not
Baqillani refutes an objection about calling God a body with qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical linguistic distinction between 'thing' (shay') and 'body' (jism) in Islamic theology, and why the latter cannot be applied to God even with qualification.

Core Idea
‘Body’ in Arabic means composed; God is not composed, so the term cannot apply.

A common objection to the Islamic doctrine of divine transcendence is: if you can call God a 'thing' without making Him like other things, why not call Him a 'body' without making Him like other bodies? In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani provides a sharp linguistic and theological answer. He shows that the two terms operate on completely different levels, and his reasoning reveals how precise theological language must be.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the structure: the objection is stated first, then the answer follows.
Notice that the objector uses an analogy: 'thing not like things' → 'body not like bodies'.
2
Understand the key distinction
Baqillani explains that 'thing' is a general term not tied to any genus or composition, so it can be applied to God without contradiction. But 'body' is defined in language as 'composed', so applying it to God would violate its meaning.
Ask yourself: why does the term 'thing' have a wider application than 'body'?
3
Follow the analogy with 'human' and 'originated'
Baqillani strengthens his argument by analogy: just as you cannot call God 'human' or 'originated' even with qualifications, you cannot call Him 'body' either. The analogy shows the principle: terms with specific meanings cannot be stretched.
Relate this to the earlier lessons on divine attributes and the importance of linguistic precision.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence sums up: applying 'body' to God would nullify the meaning of language itself. This highlights the gravity of the issue—theological statements must respect the Arabic language as it is.
This lesson shows how kalam theologians used language analysis to defend Tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : ولم أنكرتم أن يكون الباري سبحانه جسماً لا كالأجسام كما أنه عندكم شيء لا كالأشياء؟ قيل له : لأن قولنا «شيء» لم يبن لجنس دون جنس ولا لإفادة التأليف فجاز وجود شيء ليس بجنس من أجناس الحوادث وليس بمؤلف ولم يكن ذلك نقضاً لمعنى تسميته بأنه شيء وقولنا «جسم» موضوع في اللغة للمؤلف دون ما ليس بمؤلف كما أن قولنا «إنسان» و «محدث» اسم لما وجد عن عدم ولما له هذه الصورة دون غيرها فكما لم يجز أن تثبت القديم سبحانه محدثاً لا كالمحدثات وإنساناً لا كالناس قياساً على أنه شيء لا كالأشياء لم يجز أن نثبته جسماً لا كالأجسام لأنه نقض لمعنى الكلام وإخراج له عن موضوعه وفائدته .
If they say: 'Why do you deny that the Creator, exalted be He, may be a body not like other bodies, just as He is in your view a thing not like other things?' It is said to him: Because our saying 'thing' (shay') was not established for a particular genus nor for conveying composition, so it is possible for something to exist that is not from any genus of originated things and is not composed; and that is not a violation of the meaning of calling Him a thing. But our saying 'body' (jism) is established in language for the composed, not for what is not composed. Just as our saying 'human' (insān) and 'originated' (muḥdath) is a name for what comes into existence after non-existence and has this form, not for other things. So just as it is not permissible for you to affirm that the Eternal, exalted, is originated not like originated things, or human not like humans, by analogy to His being a thing not like things, so too it is not permissible to affirm Him as a body not like bodies, because that would nullify the meaning of language and remove it from its proper usage and benefit.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and answer about describing God as a body. The objector argues: just as you call God a 'thing' without likening Him to other things, why not call Him a 'body' without likening Him to other bodies? Baqillani's answer distinguishes the two terms. 'Thing' (shay') is a general term that does not specify genus or composition; therefore, applying it to God does not imply any impermissible attribute. However, 'body' (jism) in Arabic is specifically defined as something composed (mu'allaf). Since God is not composed (as proven earlier), applying 'body' to Him would contradict the very meaning of the word. He supports this by analogy: one cannot call God 'originated' or 'human' even with qualifications, because those terms are tied to specific meanings of origination and human form. Thus, the analogy fails.

Key Terms
شيء
جسم
مؤلف
موضوع في اللغة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'thing' and 'body' are similar in generality and thus the same reasoning applies.
Correction: 'Thing' is a generic term with no specific implication of composition, while 'body' is a specific term that necessarily implies composition. Therefore, calling God a 'body' contradicts the term's linguistic definition.

Checkpoint

B) Because the Qur'an uses 'thing' but not 'body'
C) Because reason forbids calling God a body
D) Because 'body' is more specific than 'thing' in meaning

Key Takeaway
Terms with specific linguistic definitions cannot be applied to God even with qualification, because doing so would violate the meaning of language itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Calling God a Body Without Literal Meaning Is Impermissible
Baqillani denies permissibility of calling God a body even if not meant literally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rules that calling God a body is impermissible even if not meant literally, based on the principles of reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Neither reason nor revelation permits calling God a body — it is rationally negated and scripturally unsupported.

This passage presents a classic objection against the Ash'ari position on divine names. The opponent suggests that one could use the term 'body' for God figuratively, even if not literal. Baqillani refutes this by invoking two authorities: reason and revelation. Let's see how he builds his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's statement: they concede that 'body' literally means composition but argue for figurative use. Notice the logical concession.
The opponent is trying to make a loophole for using a problematic term.
2
Grasp the response
Baqillani denies it because reason negates it (God is not composed) and revelation provides no support. Identify the two parts of his argument.
The argument uses both rational and scriptural evidence.
3
Connect to the principle
Divine names must be either rationally necessary or revealed. Here, neither condition holds, so the name is rejected. Reflect on why this principle matters.
This safeguards divine transcendence from inappropriate attributions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم من جواز تسميته جسماً وإن لم يكن بحقيقة ما وضع له هذا الاسم في اللغة ؟ قيل لهم : أنكرنا ذلك لأن هذه التسمية لو لم تثبت له إلا شرعاً لأن العقل لا يقتضيها بل ينفيها إن لم يكن القديم سبحانه مؤلفاً وليس في شيء من دلائل السمع من الكتاب والسنة وإجماع الأمة وما يستخرج من ذلك ما يدل على وجوب التسمية ولا على جوازها أيضاً فبطل ما قلتموه .
If they say: 'What prevents you from permitting calling Him a body even if it is not according to the literal meaning for which this name is established in the language?' It is said to them: We deny that because this appellation, if it is not established for Him except by revelation? [the text says: 'if it were not established for Him except by revelation'] because reason does not necessitate it but rather negates it if the Eternal, exalted, is not composed; and there is nothing in the transmitted proofs from the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the community, nor what is derived from them, that indicates the obligation of this appellation nor even its permissibility. So what you said is invalid.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
Here, the objector concedes that 'body' literally implies composition but argues that one could still use the term figuratively. Baqillani responds by noting that reason itself does not require such a name—indeed, it negates it because God is not composed. Moreover, revelation (the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus) provides no basis for either obligating or permitting this name. Since neither reason nor revelation supports it, the claim is baseless. The underlying principle is that divine names are either revealed or rationally necessary; here, neither condition holds, so the name is rejected.

Key Terms

شرع
عقل
دلائل السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may invent respectful names for God as long as the intended meaning is correct and not literal.
Correction: Baqilliani argues that for a name to be used for God, it must either be rationally necessary or supported by revelation. Here, reason negates the name (since God is not a body) and revelation does not support it, so it is impermissible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Calling God a body is impermissible because reason negates it and revelation provides no basis for it.
