Revelation Prohibits Names That Reason Allows
Baqillani explains that reason does not forbid but revelation forbids certain names for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason does not forbid certain names for God but divine law can, and how consensus (ijma) plays a role in determining permissible names.

Core Idea
Reason permits what revelation may forbid in naming God.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against those who forbid calling God a 'body' or similar terms. The objection questions why something that is not rationally impossible is still prohibited. Baqillani responds by distinguishing the authority of reason from the authority of divine law, using the example of the terms 'intelligent' and 'clever' to illustrate the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage carefully. Note the objection: 'Why do you forbid its permissibility even if you do not obligate it?' Then follow Baqillani's answer, starting with 'As for reason...'
The answer distinguishes between what reason does and what revelation does.
2
Distinguish reason and revelation
Identify the three actions reason does NOT do regarding the name: it does not prevent, forbid, or deem impossible. Then note that the prohibition comes solely from divine law (sharʿ).
Reason's permissibility does not imply legal permissibility.
3
Examine the example
Consider the example of calling God 'intelligent' (ʿāqil) or 'clever' (faṭin). Even though the meaning is appropriate (God is knowing), these names are forbidden by consensus (ijma). Ask: Why would the community forbid a name that is logically permissible?
The example shows that the source of prohibition is revelation, not reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : ولم منعتم من جواز ذلك وإن لم توجبوه؟ قيل لهم : أما العقل فلا يمنع ولا يحرم ولا يحيل إيقاع هذه التسمية عليه تعالى وإن أحال معناها في اللسان وإنما تحرم تسميته بهذا الاسم وبغيره مما ليس بأسمائه لأجل حظر السمع لذلك لأن الأمة مجمعة على حظر تسميته عاقلاً وفطناً وإن كان بمعنى من يستحق هذه التسمية؛ لأنه عالم وليس العقل
If they say: 'Why do you forbid its permissibility even if you do not obligate it?' It is said to them: As for reason, it does not prevent, nor forbid, nor deem impossible the application of this appellation to Him, exalted, even if it deems its meaning impossible in language; rather, calling Him by this name and by other than His names is forbidden only because of the prohibition of the Divine Law for that, since the community is in consensus on prohibiting calling Him 'intelligent' (ʿāqil) or 'clever' (faṭin), even if the meaning is one who deserves this appellation, because He is knowing (ʿālim); and reason does not...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This final objection asks: if you do not consider it rationally necessary (to call God a body), why do you forbid it? Baqillani answers by distinguishing between reason and revelation. Reason itself does not forbid applying such a name—it may be linguistically impossible, but reason does not deem it unlawful. The prohibition comes from Divine Law (sharʿ). He gives an example: the community (umma) is agreed that one cannot call God 'intelligent' (ʿāqil) or 'clever' (faṭin), even though these terms imply someone who deserves the name, because God is knowing (ʿālim). The reason is that such names are not revealed or sanctioned by scripture. Similarly, calling God 'body' is prohibited by revelation, not because reason makes it impossible. The passage is cut off, but the argument is that the source of prohibition is the Law, not reason.

Key Terms

عقل
إجماع الأمة
تسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That whatever is rationally possible is permissible in religion.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that divine law can restrict naming even if reason allows it, as shown by the consensus against calling God 'intelligent'.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why is calling God 'intelligent' (ʿāqil) forbidden?
A) Because reason proves God is not intelligent
B) Because the community is in consensus to forbid it
C) Because it implies composition
D) Because the term is not Arabic

Key Takeaway
Revelation, not reason, is the ultimate authority for determining permissible names for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something in your worship or belief about God is acceptable simply because it seemed reasonable to you? How does Baqillani's distinction between reason and revelation confront that assumption? What will you do differently starting today?
?
The community (umma) is in consensus on prohibiting certain names for God. Are you willing to submit to the collective guidance of the scholars and the community even when your own intellect sees no harm? Where in your life is this hardest for you?
?
When you call upon Allah or describe Him, do you ever use words or attributes that are not explicitly revealed, trusting your own judgment over scripture? What would it cost you to let go of that habit and rely solely on what He has taught you?

The Impermissibility of Naming God a Body
Baqillani answers an objection about naming God a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's two-part argument against the permissibility of calling God a body, based on reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Reason negates the meaning of 'body' for God, and revelation does not permit its use.

Baqillani addresses an objection that asks why we cannot call God a body in a non-literal sense, even if it is not the original meaning. He responds by showing that reason itself negates the meaning (since God is not composed), and revelation provides no evidence for its permissibility or obligation. This passage illustrates how reason and revelation work together in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. The objection is presented as a hypothetical challenge: 'What do you deny about the permissibility of calling Him a body even if not with the reality for which this name was placed in the language?'
Notice that the objector assumes that non-literal use might be acceptable.
2
Identify the two parts of Baqillani's answer
Baqillani's answer has two parts: first, reason negates the naming because God is not composed. Second, revelation provides no evidence for its obligation or even its permissibility.
The phrase 'even if it were established for Him only from revelation' is key to understanding the argument's structure.
3
Reflect on the interplay of reason and revelation
Consider why Baqillani begins with reason. He shows that even before consulting revelation, reason already rules out the meaning. This makes the objection fail on two fronts.
This passage demonstrates that both reason and revelation are necessary in matters of divine names and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم من جواز تسميته جسماً وإن لم يكن بحقيقة ما وُضِعَ له هذا الاسم في اللغة ؟ قيل لهم : أنكرنا ذلك لأن هذه التسمية لو لم تثبت له إلا شرعاً لأن العقل لا يقتضيها بل ينفيها إن لم يكن القديم سبحانه مُؤلَّفاً وليس في شيء من دلائل السمع من الكتاب والسنة وإجماع الأمة وما يُستخرج من ذلك ما يدل على وجوب التسمية ولا على جوازها أيضاً فبَطَلَ ما قلتموه
If they say: 'What do you deny about the permissibility of calling Him a body even if not with the reality for which this name was placed in the language?' It is said to them: We deny that because this naming, even if it were established for Him only from revelation, reason does not require it but rather negates it if the Eternal, exalted, is not composed, and there is nothing in the evidences of revelation from the Book, Sunna, consensus of the community, and what is derived from them that indicates an obligation of the naming nor even its permissibility. So what you said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector asks why it is not permissible to call God a body even if not in its literal sense. The answer states that even if such naming could only come from revelation, reason negates it because God is not composed. Moreover, revelation provides no evidence for its obligation or even its permissibility, so the objection fails.

Key Terms

جواز
شرع
عقل
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to call God a body as long as we do not mean its literal sense.
Correction: Baqillani shows that reason negates the meaning (since God is not composed) and revelation does not permit it, so it is impermissible.

Checkpoint

B) It is forbidden by consensus
C) It would imply anthropomorphism
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Calling God a body is impermissible because reason negates its meaning and revelation does not permit it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Claim that God is a Body: Naming vs. Meaning
Baqillani responds to the claim that God is a body meaning self-subsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects calling God a 'body' even when the term is used in a metaphorical sense, and how he distinguishes between the true meaning of 'body' as composite and other attributes like self-subsistence.

Core Idea
Calling God a 'body' confuses a term's meaning with its attributes.

An objector suggests that God could be called a body if we mean by that He is self-subsistent, a thing, or a bearer of attributes. Baqillani responds by clarifying that he does not deny these descriptions of God, but he denies the application of the term 'body' because its proper meaning is 'composite'. This lesson walks through his careful distinction between naming and meaning, and examines the definitions of key terms like 'thing', 'substance', and 'accident'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection where the interlocutor proposes four possible meanings of 'body' applied to God.
Notice that the objector is using 'body' in a loose sense.
2
Identify Baqillani's response
Baqillani agrees that God has the attributes mentioned, but rejects the label 'body'. Why?
Focus on his phrase: 'this is about naming, not about meaning'.
3
Examine the definitions
Baqillani defines 'body', 'thing', 'substance', 'accident', and 'self-subsistent' to show they are distinct.
Note that 'body' = composite; 'self-subsistent' = not needing something else.
4
Understand the conclusion
Baqillani concludes that the objector's argument fails because these meanings are unrelated to 'body' as composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: ما أنكرتم أن يكون جسماً على معنى أنه قائم بنفسه أو بمعنى أنه شيء أو بمعنى أنه حامل للصفات أو بمعنى أنه غير محتاج في الوجود إلى شيء يقوم به؟ قيل له: لا ننكر أن يكون الباري سبحانه حاصلاً على جميع هذه الأحكام والأوصاف وإنما ننكر تسميتكم لمن حصلت له بأنه جسم وإن لم يكن مؤلفاً فهذا عندنا في التسمية دون المعنى لأن معنى الجسم أنه المؤلف على ما بيناه ومعنى الشيء أنه الثابت الموجود وقد يكون جسماً إذا كان مؤلفاً ويكون جوهراً إذا كان جزءاً منفرداً ويكون عرضاً إذا كان مما يقوم بالجوهر ومعنى القائم بنفسه هو أنه غير محتاج في الوجود إلى شيء يوجد به ومعنى ذلك أنه مما له الوجود وإن لم يفعل صانعه شيئاً غيره إذا كان محدثاً ويصح وجوده وإن لم يوجد قائم بنفسه سواه إذا كان قديماً وليس هذا من معنى قولنا جسم ومؤلف بسبب فبطل ما قلتم
If it is said: 'What prevents you from saying that He is a body in the sense that He is self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi), or in the sense that He is a thing (shay'), or in the sense that He is a bearer of attributes, or in the sense that He is not in need of anything in existence to subsist by?' It is said to him: We do not deny that the Exalted Creator obtains all these judgments and descriptions, but we deny your naming the one who has these descriptions as 'body' even if he is not composed. So this with us is about naming, not about meaning, because the meaning of body is that it is composed, as we have explained. And the meaning of thing is that it is established and existent. And something may be a body if it is composed, and a substance (jawhar) if it is an individual part, and an accident ('arad) if it is something subsisting in a substance. And the meaning of self-subsistent is that it is not in need of something to exist by. And the meaning of that is that it has existence even if its maker makes nothing other than it if it is originated, and its existence is valid even if nothing self-subsistent other than it exists if it is eternal. And this is not related to our saying 'body' and 'composed.' So what you said is invalid.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector presents four possible meanings for calling God a body: self-subsistent, a thing, bearer of attributes, or independent in existence. Baqillani responds that he does not deny these attributes of God, but he denies the application of the term 'body' (jism) to God because the proper meaning of 'body' is 'composite' (mu'allaf). He then defines other key terms: 'thing' (shay') means 'established existent' (thabit mawjud); a body is composite; a substance (jawhar) is an indivisible part; an accident ('arad) subsists in a substance; and 'self-subsistent' (qa'im bi-nafsihi) means not needing something else to exist by. For originated beings, it means they exist without their maker creating another thing, and for God, it means He exists without needing anything else. These meanings are distinct from the meaning of 'body' as composite. Therefore, the objection fails because it confuses naming with meaning.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
قائم بنفسه (qa'im bi-nafsihi)
شيء (shay')
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض ('arad)
مؤلف (mu'allaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that 'self-subsistent' is synonymous with 'body'.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'self-subsistent' means not needing something else, while 'body' means composite; they are distinct concepts.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the meaning of 'body' (jism)?
A) Self-subsistent
B) Composite
C) Existent
D) Bearer of attributes

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the notion that God can be called a body by showing that the true meaning of 'body' is composition, which does not apply to God, while the attributes used by the objector are correct but not synonymous with 'body'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God's attributes, do you ever use words carelessly, without knowing their precise theological meaning? How will you ensure that your words about God reflect His true nature, not human concepts of composition or limitation?
?
If someone asked you why God cannot be called a body, would you be able to give them the clear distinction between naming and meaning that Baqillani provides? What does your inability (or ability) to do so reveal about the depth of your understanding of tawhid?
?
You believe God is self-subsistent and independent—but have you ever thought of Him as needing 'parts' or 'components' to exist? Search your heart: Are there any hidden mental images of God that still resemble created things? What are you willing to do to purify your conception of Him?

Proof of Divine Attributes from Human Attributes
Proof of divine attributes by logical analogy with human attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical relationship between a description and its attribute in humans is used to prove that God possesses real attributes.

Core Idea
If a description is true, its corresponding attribute must exist — for humans and for God.

This lesson addresses a fundamental question: Why do theologians insist that God has seven specific attributes? Imam al-Baqillani presents a rational proof based on the necessary connection between a description and its cause. By examining how we use words like 'living' and 'knowing' for humans, he shows that these descriptions are meaningless without real underlying attributes. The same logic applies to God.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
The passage begins with an imagined question — why claim God has attributes? Identify that the proof is a defense against those who deny attributes.
2
Understand the key analogy
The author draws on human experience: a human is called 'living' only because he has life. Note that the attribute is the 'cause' (illa) of the description.
The Arabic term 'علة' means cause or reason.
3
Apply the logic to God
Since we describe God as living, knowing, powerful, etc., He must possess those attributes. See the conclusion that denying attributes would contradict the descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الصفات فإن قال قائل : ولم قلتم إن للقديم تعالى حياةً وعلماً وقدرة وسمعاً وبصراً وكلاماً وإرادة؟ قيل له : من قبل أن الحي العالم القادر منا إنما كان حياً عالماً قادراً متكلماً مريداً من أجل أن له حياة وعلماً وقدرة وكلاماً وسمعاً وبصراً وإرادة. وأن هذا فائدة وصفه بأنه حي عالم قادر مريد يدل على ذلك أن الحي منا لا يجوز أن يكون حياً عالماً قادراً مريداً مع عدم الحياة والعلم والقدرة ولا توجد به هذه الصفات إلا وجب بوجودها به أن يكون حياً عالماً قادراً فوجب أنها علة في كونه كذلك كما وجب أن تكون علة كون الفاعل فاعلاً والمُريد مريداً وجود فعله وإرادته التي يجب كونه فاعلاً مريداً لوجودها وغير فاعل بعدمها فوجب أن يكون الباري سبحانه ذا حياة وعلم وقدرة وإرادة وكلامٍ وسمع وبصر وأنه لو لم يكن له شيء من هذه الصفات لم يكن حياً ولا عالماً ولا قادراً ولا مريداً ولا متكلماً ولا سميعاً ولا بصيراً يتعالى عن ذلك.
Chapter on Speech about the Attributes. If someone says: Why do you say that the Eternal, exalted, has life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, and will? It is said to him: Because the living, knowing, powerful one among us is only living, knowing, powerful, speaking, willing on account of having life, knowledge, power, speech, hearing, sight, and will. And that this is the benefit of describing him as living, knowing, powerful, willing indicates that the living among us cannot be living, knowing, powerful, willing in the absence of life and knowledge and power, and these attributes are not found in him except that by their existence in him it becomes necessary that he is living, knowing, powerful. So it is necessary that they are the cause of his being such, just as it is necessary that the existence of his act and his will are the cause of the agent being an agent and the willing being willing, which necessitate his being agent and willing by their existence and not agent by their absence. So it is necessary that the Creator, exalted, possess life, knowledge, power, will, speech, hearing, and sight, and that if he had none of these attributes, he would not be living, nor knowing, nor powerful, nor willing, nor speaking, nor hearing, nor seeing—exalted is He above that.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a proof of divine attributes. It begins with an objection: why do you assert that God has attributes? The answer proceeds by examining human attributes: a human is called living, knowing, powerful only because he has life, knowledge, power, etc. The attribute is the cause (illa) of the description. Similarly, since God is described as living, knowing, powerful, etc., He must have these attributes; otherwise the description would be meaningless. The analogy is not a crude comparison but a logical necessity: if God lacked these attributes, He would not be truly living, knowing, etc. The proof concludes that God possesses all seven attributes (life, knowledge, power, will, speech, hearing, sight) and that to deny them would be to deny God's perfection.

حياة
علم
قدرة
إرادة
كلام
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are metaphorical or not real.
Correction: The passage argues that descriptions like 'living' and 'knowing' require real attributes; otherwise the descriptions would be meaningless.

Checkpoint

A) Revelation alone.
B) The fact that humans have attributes.
C) The necessity that the description 'living' implies the attribute of life.
D) Consensus of theologians.

Key Takeaway
The descriptions of God (living, knowing, powerful, etc.) logically require that He possess those attributes as real qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Attributes as Causes for Divine Descriptions
Baqillani argues from human analogy that attributes are necessary causes for divine descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that God's attributes must be real and causal for His descriptions, based on human analogy.

Core Idea
Attributes are the necessary causes for divine descriptions.

This lesson presents Baqillani's foundational argument for affirming real attributes of God. He uses a human analogy to show that descriptions like 'living' depend on real attributes (life) that cause the description. Without this causal link, divine descriptions would be meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the objection at the beginning and the response that follows.
2
Identify the key analogy
Pay attention to how Baqillani draws a parallel between human attributes and divine attributes. He argues that just as our being alive is caused by the attribute of life, so too God's being alive must be caused by His attribute of life.
This analogy is the core of the proof.
3
Understand the causal principle
Notice the claim that the attribute is the cause (علة) for the description. The description necessarily follows when the attribute exists, and is absent when the attribute is absent.
This is a rational judgment (حكم عقلي).
4
Apply the principle to God
Consider the conclusion: if God lacked any of these attributes, He would not be truly described as living, knowing, etc. This shows that the attributes are real and distinct from His essence.
Baqillani also mentions that this is the position of the scholars (عندنا وعندهم).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الصفات
فإن قال قائل: ولم قلتم إن للقديم تعالى حياةً وعلماً وقدرة وسمعاً وبصراً وكلاماً وإرادة؟ قيل له: من قبل أن الحي العالم القادر منا إنما كان حياً عالماً قادراً متكلماً مريداً من أجل أن له حياة وعلماً وقدرة وكلاماً وسمعاً وبصراً وإرادة. وأن هذا فائدة وصفه بأنه حي عالم قادر مريد يدل على ذلك أن الحي منا لا يجوز أن يكون حياً عالماً قادراً مريداً مع عدم الحياة والعلم والقدرة ولا توجد به هذه الصفات إلا وجب بوجودها به أن يكون حياً عالماً قادراً فوجب أنها علة في كونه كذلك كما وجب أن تكون علة كون الفاعل فاعلاً والمريد مريداً وجود فعله وإرادته التي يجب كونه فاعلاً مريداً لوجودها وغير فاعل بعدمها فوجب أن يكون الباري سبحانه ذا حياة وعلم وقدرة وإرادة وكلام وسمع وبصر وأنه لو لم يكن له شيء من هذه الصفات لم يكن حياً ولا عالماً ولا قادراً ولا مريداً ولا متكلماً ولا سميعاً ولا بصيراً يتعالى عن ذلك كما أنه لو لم يكن له إرادة وفعل لم يكن عندنا وعندهم فاعلاً مريداً لأن الحكم العقلي الواجب عن علة لا يجوز حصوله لبعض من له مع عدم العلة الموجبة له ولا لأجل شيء يخالفها لأن ذلك يخرجها عن أن تكون علة الحكم
Chapter on the discourse on attributes. If a questioner says: Why do you say that the Eternal, exalted, has life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, and will? It is said to him: Because the living, knowing, powerful one among us is only living, knowing, powerful, speaking, willing on account of having life, knowledge, power, speech, hearing, sight, and will. And that this is the benefit of describing him as living, knowing, powerful, willing. That indicates that the living one among us cannot be living, knowing, powerful, willing without life, knowledge, and power; and these attributes are not found in him unless it is necessary by their existence in him that he be living, knowing, powerful. So it is necessary that they are the cause for his being so, just as it is necessary that the existence of his action and his will, by which he must be an agent and willing by their existence and not an agent by their absence, is the cause for the agent being an agent and the willing one being willing. So it is necessary that the Creator, glorified, has life, knowledge, power, will, speech, hearing, and sight, and that if he did not have any of these attributes, he would not be living, nor knowing, nor powerful, nor willing, nor speaking, nor hearing, nor seeing — exalted above that. Just as if he did not have will and action, he would not be, according to us and according to them, an agent and willing, because the necessary rational judgment based on a cause cannot occur for someone who has it without the cause necessitating it, nor due to something contrary to it, because that would remove it from being the cause of the judgment.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (qiyas) from human attributes to divine attributes. Baqillani begins by posing an objection: why affirm attributes for God? The answer is that in our experience, being alive, knowing, powerful, etc., depends on having real attributes (life, knowledge, power). These attributes are the cause (illa) for the description. If a human cannot be alive without life, then God, who is described as alive, must also have life as a real attribute. The same applies to knowledge, power, will, speech, hearing, and sight. Baqillani further argues that the relationship is necessary: whenever the attribute exists, the description applies; if the attribute were absent, the description would not apply. He concludes that God must possess these attributes, for without them He would not be truly described as alive, etc. The passage also stresses the rational principle that a necessary judgment contingent on a cause cannot occur without that cause.

Key Terms
صفات
حياة
علم
قدرة
إرادة
علة
حكم عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine descriptions are mere metaphors or that attributes are identical to the essence.
Correction: Baqillani argues that attributes are real and distinct causes for the descriptions; without them, the descriptions would not apply.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the relationship between the attribute of life and being described as 'living'?
A) The description is independent of any attribute.
B) The attribute of life is the cause of being described as living.
C) Being described as living is the same as the essence.
D) The attribute is merely a figurative expression.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that divine attributes are real causes for God's descriptions, based on necessary rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Alive,' do you believe that He actually has a real attribute of life that necessitates His being alive, or is that just a phrase? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly believed His attributes are real causes?
?
If God's knowledge is a real cause for His knowing all things, does that make you more aware that He knows your hidden sins and thoughts? How does that awareness affect your actions today?
?
Baqillani uses human analogy to prove divine attributes. If you accept that, then consider: your own life is caused by a real attribute of life. Are you living in a way that acknowledges that your life is a gift from God, the source of all life? Or do you act as if your life is self-sufficient?

Second Proof: Acts Indicate Divine Attributes Beyond Essence
Baqillani gives a second proof from observed acts indicating God's knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani argues that observed divine acts necessitate distinct attributes of knowledge and power, separate from God's essence.

Core Idea
Divine acts point to attributes, not just essence.

In this lesson, Baqillani presents a second proof for the reality of divine attributes. He builds on the observation of God's acts—like creation—to show that they not only indicate an agent, but specifically an agent with knowledge and power. Moreover, he argues that the signification of these acts cannot be the agent's essence itself, because 'knowing' means something more than 'existing'. This establishes that God's attributes are distinct and real.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting key terms like فعل, دلالة, مدلول. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to logical connectors like 'وقد ثبت أن'.
2
Identify the main claim
Baqillani states that acts indicate the agent is knowing and powerful. Ask yourself: How does he support this claim?
3
Understand the logical step about signification
He argues that the act's signification cannot be the agent's essence or existence, because descriptions like 'knowing' are additional to existence. Reflect on why this matters for proving real attributes.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Consider how this proof differs from the first proof (if you recall). This one relies on observed acts (af'al) rather than theoretical reasoning.
This shows Baqillani's method of using creation to infer attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل آخر
ومما يدل أيضاً على إثبات علم الله تعالى وقدرته ما ظهر من أفعاله الدالة على كونه عالماً قادراً وأنه مفارق للجاهل العاجز وقد ثبت أن الفعل الدال على كون الفاعل عالماً قادراً لا بد له من تعلق بمدلول وأن مدلوله لا يجوز أن يكون نفس الفاعل ووجوده ولا صفة ترجع إلى نفسه من حيث ثبت أن معنى وصفه بأنه عالم قادر زائد على وصفه بأنه شيء موجود وأن الوصف له بأنه عالم قادر قد ينتفي عنه مع وجود نفسه وكونه شيئاً موجوداً فوجب اختلاف معنى هذه الأوصاف
Another proof. Also, what indicates the establishment of God's knowledge and power is what appears of His acts indicating that He is knowing and powerful and that He is distinct from the ignorant and powerless. And it has been established that the act indicating that the agent is knowing and powerful must have a connection to a signification, and that its signification cannot be the agent himself and his existence, nor an attribute referring back to himself, because it has been established that the meaning of describing him as knowing and powerful is additional to describing him as a thing that exists, and that the description of him as knowing and powerful may be negated from him while his self exists and he is a thing existing. Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for divine attributes based on the observed acts (af'al) of God. Baqillani argues that the acts of God—such as creation—clearly indicate that their agent is knowing ('alim) and powerful (qadir), distinct from one who is ignorant or powerless. He then makes a logical point: an act that points to the agent's knowledge and power must have a signification (madlul) beyond the agent's essence or existence. Why? Because the description 'knowing' or 'powerful' adds something beyond the mere existence of the agent. Moreover, it is possible for an existing being to not be described as knowing or powerful (e.g., a human can exist but be ignorant). Therefore, the signification of the act cannot be the agent's essence itself but must be distinct attributes of knowledge and power. Thus, the meanings of 'existing' and 'knowing' are different, affirming real attributes.

First Reason: Act Cannot Indicate Self-Referential Attribute
Baqillani gives the first of two reasons why acts cannot refer back to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's first argument against identifying divine attributes with the essence, based on the logical impossibility of an essence ever existing without its inseparable attribute.

Core Idea
If an attribute were identical to the essence, the essence could never exist without it.

Baqillani lays out his first argument against the claim that an act's indication points to an attribute that is merely the essence itself. He uses a conditional argument: if the attribute were self-referential, then the essence would be bound to that attribute eternally. In this lesson, we examine the logic and the illustrative analogy of blackness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by an analogy.
The passage ends with the analogy, but the argument is complete for the first reason.
2
Identify the hypothesis being refuted
Baqillani is critiquing the idea that the act's indication points to an attribute that is identical with the essence (ṣifa tarji'u ila nafsihi). Understand what this view claims.
This view would make the attribute inherent in the essence itself.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If the hypothesis were true, then the essence would never exist without that attribute. Baqillani says this would be necessary.
Think about what 'necessary' means here: it would be impossible for the essence to be without the attribute.
4
Analyze the analogy of blackness
Baqillani compares this to blackness (sawad), which is black by its own nature. Blackness cannot exist without being black, just as a self-referential attribute would never leave the essence.
This analogy shows the absurdity: if divine attributes were like that, then God would always be knowing and powerful essentially, which would make acts unnecessary for proving them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لا يجوز أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أن الفاعل عالم قادر دلالةً على صفة ترجع إلى نفسه لأمرين: أحدهما أن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب ألا توجد نفس العالم القادر إلا عالمة قادرة وألا ينتفي عنه هذان الوصفان إلا بانتفاء نفسه وبطلانها كما أن السواد الذي هو سواد لنفسه يجب ألا تعلم نفسه وتوجد إلا وهو سواد
And likewise, it is not permissible that the indication of the act that the agent is knowing and powerful be an indication of an attribute that refers back to himself, for two reasons: The first is that if that were the case, it would be necessary that the self of the knowing and powerful not exist except as knowing and powerful, and that these two descriptions not be negated from him except by the negation of his self and its annihilation, just as blackness, which is black by itself, must not have its self known and exist except as black.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage gives the first of two arguments against the idea that the act's indication points to an attribute that is identical with the essence (ṣifa tarji'u ila nafsihi). Baqillani says: if the attribute were self-referential (i.e., inherent in the essence such that the essence is the attribute itself), then it would be impossible for the essence to exist without being qualified by that attribute. For example, if knowledge were identical with God's essence, then God would always be knowing—He could never be not-knowing, just as blackness cannot exist without being black. But we know that created beings (and even God in different states?) can have attributes that are not inseparable from their essence; e.g., a human can exist without knowledge. Therefore, divine knowledge and power cannot be self-referential attributes; they must be distinct, real attributes that are not identical with the essence. The analogy of blackness (sawad) illustrates a property that is inseparable from its subject: blackness is black by its own nature, so it never exists as non-black. If divine attributes were like that, God would be necessarily knowing and powerful without possibility of negation, but that is not the case for created agents, and it does not apply to God's optional descriptions.

Key Terms
دلالة
صفة ترجع إلى نفسه
نفس
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that because God is always knowing, knowledge must be identical to His essence.
Correction: Baqillani shows that this would make it impossible for a contingent agent (like a human) to ever lack knowledge, which is false. Hence divine attributes are distinct real attributes, not identical to the essence.

Checkpoint

A) The agent would be knowing and powerful only sometimes.
B) The agent's essence could exist without being knowing or powerful.
C) The agent's essence could only exist as knowing and powerful, never otherwise.
D) The agent would not need acts to prove attributes.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's first reason demonstrates that if an attribute were identical with the essence, the essence could never exist without that attribute, which is not the case for contingent beings and thus refutes the self-referential attribute view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God knows everything,' do you imagine His knowledge as something He possesses or as something He is? Baqillani argues that if knowledge were identical to essence, God could never cease to know—but He is eternal. How does this distinction affect your awe of His knowledge? Will you consciously remind yourself that His knowledge is not like our knowledge?
?
Consider your own attributes: is there any quality you consider so essential that losing it would feel like losing yourself? Baqillani uses blackness as an example of an inseparable attribute. How does this help you understand the difference between Creator and creature? Are you willing to let go of any anthropomorphic notions of God?
?
This lesson refutes the idea that God's actions directly indicate His essence. Does your daily worship reflect a God who is known through His actions (creation) rather than through direct access to His essence? How can you deepen your reliance on the signs of His actions rather than assuming you know His essence?

Proof for Divine Will and Act from Necessity of Cause
Baqillani argues that without will and act, God would not be an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Baqillani argues that divine will and act are necessary for God to be considered an agent and willing.

Core Idea
Without will and act, God cannot be an agent.

This short passage from Baqillani's Tamhid presents a key argument for the necessity of divine attributes of will and act. He uses the principle of causality in rational judgments to show that if God lacked these, He would not qualify as an agent. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if He had no will and act, He would not be an agent and willing.'
The passage connects two attributes: will (إرادة) and act (فعل).
2
Identify the causal principle
Baqillani states that the rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) of being an agent is necessitated by a cause (ʿilla). Understand that this cause must be present for the judgment to hold.
The cause here is will and act themselves.
3
Apply the argument to God
If the cause (will and act) is absent, the judgment (agent and willing) cannot apply. Thus, God must have will and act to be truly an agent.
This establishes that will and act are essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أنه لو لم يكن له إرادة وفعل لم يكن عندنا وعندهم فاعلاً هو مريداً لأن الحكم العقلي الواجب عن علة لا يجوز حصوله لبعض من له مع عدم العلة الموجبة له ولا لأجل شيء يخالفها لأن ذلك يخرجها عن أن تكون علة الحكم
Also, if He had no will and act, He would not, according to us and them, be an agent and willing, because the rational judgment necessitated by a cause cannot occur for some who have it without the cause that necessitates it, nor for something contrary to it, because that would remove it from being a cause of the judgment.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine will and act. Baqillani argues that if God lacked will and act, He would not be considered an agent (fāʿil) or willing (murīd). The reason is that the rational judgment (ḥukm ʿaqlī) of being an agent is necessitated by a specific cause (ʿilla). This judgment cannot occur unless its proper cause is present, nor can it occur due to something contrary to that cause. Therefore, will and act are necessary causes for the judgment that God is an agent and willing. The argument establishes that these attributes are essential for divine agency.

Key Terms
إرادة
فعل
فاعل
مريد
حكم عقلي
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's agency does not require will and act.
Correction: Baqillani argues that without will and act, God would not be considered an agent at all, because the rational judgment of agency is necessitated by those attributes as its cause.

Checkpoint

A) He would still be an agent by essence.
B) He would not be considered an agent or willing.
C) He would be an agent but not willing.
D) He would be willing but not an agent.

Key Takeaway
Divine will and act are essential for God to be an agent; without them, the rational judgment of agency fails.

Proof of Distinct Attributes from Actions
Baqillani proves God's knowledge and power through action indication with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that actions indicate God's knowledge and power as distinct attributes, not as His essence.

Core Idea
Actions point to distinct attributes of knowledge and power, not to God's essence.

This passage presents a crucial argument in Islamic theology: how do we know that God's attributes like knowledge and power are distinct from His essence? Imam Baqillani uses the evidence of actions to prove that these attributes are real and additional to the essence. He carefully rules out the possibility that actions indicate the essence itself, using two logical reasons. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a dense, logical style.
2
Identify the main claim and structure
The passage begins by stating that actions indicate God is knowing and powerful. Then it establishes that this indication must have an indicated thing (madlul). It rules out that the indicated thing could be the essence or an attribute of essence.
3
Understand the first reason against indication on essence
Baqillani argues: if actions indicated the essence, then the essence would always be knowing and powerful as long as it exists. But we observe that descriptions like 'knowing' can be negated while the essence remains, so they cannot be essential.
This is analogous to blackness being always black by its essence.
4
Examine the second reason
The second reason: if indication were on the essence, the essence itself would have to be knowledge, just as a black thing's essence is blackness. Since it's impossible for God's essence to be knowledge, the indication must be on the attributes.
This shows the absurdity of identifying essence with attribute.
5
Conclude the argument
Having ruled out essence and attributes of essence, the only remaining possibility is that the indicated thing is knowledge and power themselves. Thus, actions prove that God has distinct attributes of knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل أيضاً على إثبات علم الله تعالى وقدرته ما ظهر من أفعاله الدالة على كونه عالماً قادراً وأنه مفارق للجاهل العاجز وقد ثبت أن الفعل الدال على كون الفاعل عالماً قادراً لا بد له من تعلق بمدلول وأن مدلوله لا يجوز أن يكون نفس الفاعل ووجوده ولا صفة ترجع إلى نفسه من حيث ثبت أن معنى وصفه بأنه عالم قادر زائد على وصفه بأنه شيء موجود وأن الوصف له بأنه عالم قادر قد ينتفي عنه مع وجود نفسه وكونه شيئاً موجوداً فوجب اختلاف معنى هذه الأوصاف . وكذلك لا يجوز أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أن الفاعل عالم قادر دلالةً على صفة ترجع إلى نفسه لأمرين : أحدهما أن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب ألا توجد نفس العالم القادر إلا عالمة قادرة وألا ينتفي عنه هذان الوصفان إلا بانتفاء نفسه وبطلانها كما أن السواد الذي هو سواد لنفسه يجب ألا تعلم نفسه وتوجد إلا سواد وألا ينتفي عنه الوصف بأنه سواد إلا بانتفاء نفسه فلم يجز لذلك أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أن الفاعل عالم قادر دلالة على صفة ترجع إلى نفسه . والأمر الآخر أن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب أن تكون نفس العالم علماً كما أن الأسود إذا كان أسود لنفسه وجب أن تكون نفسه سواداً ولما استحال أن تكون نفس العالم القادر القديم والمحدث علماً استحال أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أنه عالم دلالة على نفسه ووجوده أو على صفة ترجع إلى نفسه وإذا ثبت ذلك وجب أن يكون مدلول الفعل ومتعله هو العلم والقدرة .
And among what also indicates the affirmation of God Most High's knowledge and power is what has appeared from His actions that indicate He is knowing and powerful and that He is beyond the ignorant and powerless. It has been established that the action that indicates the agent is knowing and powerful must have a relation to an indicated thing (madlul), and that its indicated thing cannot be the agent himself and his existence, nor an attribute belonging to himself, because it is established that the meaning of describing him as knowing and powerful is additional to describing him as a thing existent, and the description of him as knowing and powerful may be negated from him while his self exists and he is a thing existent, so it is necessary that the meanings of these descriptions differ. Likewise, the action's indication that the agent is knowing and powerful cannot be an indication of an attribute belonging to himself for two reasons: First, if that were so, it would necessitate that the self of the knowing and powerful agent would not exist except as knowing and powerful, and that these two descriptions would not be negated from him except by the negation and annulment of his self, just as blackness which is black by itself, its self must not be known and exist except as black, and the description of being black is not negated from it except by the negation of its self; therefore it is not permissible that the action's indication that the agent is knowing and powerful be an indication of an attribute belonging to himself. The second reason is that if that were so, it would necessitate that the self of the knowing agent be knowledge, just as if black is black by itself, its self must be blackness; and since it is impossible that the self of the knowing and powerful agent, whether eternal or originated, be knowledge, it is impossible that the action's indication that He is knowing be an indication of His self and existence or of an attribute belonging to His self. And when that is established, it is necessary that the indicated thing of the action and its relation be knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's knowledge and power through the indication (dalāla) of His actions. Baqillani first establishes that actions indicating a knowing and powerful agent must have a relation to an indicated thing (madlūl). This indicated thing cannot be the agent's essence (nafs) or existence, nor an attribute returning to the essence, because the meaning of 'knowing' and 'powerful' is additional to the meaning of 'existent'. Moreover, the descriptions of knowing and powerful can be negated while the essence remains, showing they are distinct. He then presents two specific arguments against the indication being on the essence: (1) If indication were on the essence, then the essence would always be knowing and powerful as long as it exists, similar to blackness being always black. But we observe that knowing and powerful can be negated while the essence persists, so indication cannot be on the essence. (2) If indication were on the essence, the essence itself would have to be knowledge, just as a black thing's essence is blackness. Since it is impossible for the essence of the knowing and powerful agent to be knowledge, the indication must be on the attributes of knowledge and power. The conclusion is that the indicated thing is knowledge and power themselves.

Key Terms
علم
قدرة
دلالة
مدلول
جوهر
صفة
جاهل
عاجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the actions indicate God's essence itself, so the attributes are not distinct.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if actions indicated the essence, the essence would have to be always knowing and powerful, and would have to be identical to knowledge. Both are impossible, so actions indicate distinct attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Baqillani gives to show that the action's indication cannot be on the essence itself?
A) If it were on the essence, then the essence would have to be knowledge.
B) If it were on the essence, then the essence would always be knowing and powerful as long as it exists, but we see that knowing can be negated while essence remains.
C) If it were on the essence, then the action would have no indication.
D) If it were on the essence, then the agent would be necessarily knowing, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Actions serve as evidence for God's knowledge and power as real attributes distinct from His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just studied a logical proof that God's actions indicate His knowledge and power. Now think: are you living as if every event in your day is a deliberate act of a knowing and powerful Lord? When something unexpected or difficult happens, does your first thought turn to His wisdom and control, or do you feel abandoned? Commit to one specific reminder tomorrow that will help you see His actions in your circumstances.
?
The argument shows that God's knowledge and power are real attributes distinct from His essence. But do you treat God's attributes as comfortable doctrines or as living realities that demand a response? Have you ever been moved to gratitude by realizing that every breath you take is an act of His power? If not, what would it take for you to feel that weight? Resolve to pause three times today to silently acknowledge a specific action of God in your life.
?
Baqillani uses logic to prevent confusion between God's essence and His attributes. Yet many of us struggle to believe in attributes we cannot see. What doubts have you harbored about God's knowledge or power? How does your heart react when logic alone feels insufficient? Instead of dismissing the proof, ask yourself: what personal experience have I had of God's knowledge or power that confirms this truth? Write it down and thank Him for it today.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Action Points to Knowledge and Power as Real Attributes
Baqillani argues that action indicates knowledge and power as real attributes via an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani uses an analogy to show that action indicates the attributes of knowledge and power as real, distinct qualities of the agent.

Core Idea
Action reveals the hidden attributes of knowledge and power, just as blackness reveals the color black.

Imam al-Baqillani continues his proof that action indicates the divine attributes. Here he clarifies a key logical point: when we say an agent is 'knowing' or 'powerful', we mean they possess knowledge and power as real attributes. Therefore, when an action shows the agent to be knowing and powerful, it directly proves the existence of those attributes. He strengthens this with a simple analogy from everyday observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the first part of the passage where Baqillani states that being knowing and powerful means nothing more than possessing knowledge and power. This is the foundational premise.
Notice the phrase 'ليس معنى أن العالم عالم والقادر قادر أكثر من أنه ذو علم وقدرة'.
2
Trace the logical conclusion
From that premise, Baqillani concludes that action's indication of knowing/powerful must be an indication of the attributes themselves. See how he uses 'وجب أن تكون' to signal necessity.
3
Analyze the analogy
Read the analogy about a black agent. Just as 'black and acting' simply means blackness exists and action occurs, so 'knowing and powerful' simply means knowledge and power exist. The indication works the same way.
The analogy is key to understanding the logical structure: attributes are not separate states but inherent qualities.
4
Connect to the divine attributes
Reflect on how this argument supports the doctrine that Allah's attributes of knowledge and power are real and distinct from His essence, yet not separate. Action proves them.
This counters those who say attributes are mere names or identical to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه إذا صح وثبت أنه ليس معنى أن العالم عالم والقادر قادر أكثر من أنه ذو علم وقدرة ومن وجود هاتين الصفتين به وأنه ليس له بكونه عالماً قادراً صفتان وحالتان منفصلتان عن العلم والقدرة أو في حكم المنفصل عن ذلك وجب أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أن العالم القادر عالم قادر دلالة على علمه وقدرته كما أنه إذا ثبت أنه ليس معنى الأسود الفاعل أكثر من وجود السواد به ووقوع الفعل منه وجب أن تكون الدلالة على أنه أسودُ فاعل دلالةً على وجود السواد به فقط ووقوع الفعل منه .
And that also indicates that if it is correct and established that the meaning of the knowing being knowing and the powerful being powerful is nothing more than that he possesses knowledge and power and that these two attributes exist in him, and that he does not, by being knowing and powerful, have two separate states or attributes apart from knowledge and power, or in the ruling of being separate from that, then it is necessary that the action's indication that the knowing and powerful agent is knowing and powerful be an indication of his knowledge and his power, just as if it is established that the meaning of the black agent is nothing more than the existence of blackness in him and the occurrence of action from him, then it is necessary that the indication that he is black and agent be an indication of the existence of blackness in him only and the occurrence of action from him.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is another proof that action indicates the attributes of knowledge and power. Baqillani first establishes that the meaning of 'knowing' (ʿālim) and 'powerful' (qādir) is simply possessing knowledge and power, not something separate or additional states. Therefore, when an action indicates that the agent is knowing and powerful, it directly indicates the existence of knowledge and power in him. He uses an analogy: when we say something is 'black and acting' (aswad fāʿil), the meaning is only that blackness exists in it and action occurs from it. Thus the indication of being black and acting points only to the blackness and the action. Similarly, the indication of being knowing and powerful points only to knowledge and power. This reinforces the conclusion that actions prove the real attributes of knowledge and power as distinct from the essence.

Correction: Baqillani argues that 'knowing' and 'powerful' directly mean possessing knowledge and power as attributes. Action that shows the agent is knowing and powerful therefore shows those attributes exist.

?

Divine Attributes

First Proof: Action Indicates Attributes, Not Essence
First proof by analogy: essence cannot be knowledge, so act indicates attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why action cannot indicate the essence itself, but must indicate distinct divine attributes of knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Action proves attributes, not essence — or essence would be knowledge, which is impossible.

Imam Baqillani begins his proof by showing that if action indicated the essence itself, then the essence would have to be identical with knowledge—like blackness being identical with a black object. But this is impossible for God and creatures, so action must point to distinct attributes. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice how the author uses a conditional clause (لو كان) and an analogy (كما أن).
The passage is dense; focus on the logical structure.
2
Understand the analogy with blackness
The author compares a knowing agent to a black object. If something is black by itself, its essence would be blackness. Similarly, if action indicated essence, the essence would be knowledge. But this is absurd for a knower.
This is the key analogy that drives the first proof.
3
Identify the conclusion
After showing the impossibility, the author concludes that the act must indicate knowledge and power as attributes, not the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمر الآخر أن ذلك لو كان كذلك لوجب أن تكون نفس العالم علماً [؟] كما أن الأسود إذا كان أسود لنفسه وجب أن تكون نفسه سواداً ولما استحال أن تكون نفس العالم القادر القديم والمُحدث علماً استحال أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أنه عالم دلالة على نفسه ووجوده أو على صفة ترجع إلى نفسه وإذا ثبت ذلك وجب أن يكون مدلول الفعل ومتعلقه هو العلم والقدرة . دليل آخر ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أنه إذا صح وثبت أنه ليس معنى أن العالم عالم والقادر قادر أكثر من أنه ذو علم وقدرة ومن وجود هاتين الصفتين به وأنه ليس له بكونه عالماً قادراً صفتان وحالتان منفصلتان عن العلم والقدرة أو في حكم المنفصل عن ذلك وجب أن تكون دلالة الفعل على أن العالم القادر عالم قادر دلالة على علمه وقدرته كما أنه إذا ثبت أنه ليس معنى الأسود الفاعل أكثر من وجود السواد به ووقوع الفعل منه وجب أن تكون الدلالة على أنه أسودُ فاعل دِلالةً على وجود السواد به فقط ووقوع الفعل منه .
And the other matter is that if that were the case, then the essence of the knower would have to be knowledge [؟], just as the black thing, if it is black by itself, its essence would have to be blackness. And when it is impossible for the essence of the knowing, powerful, eternal, and temporal [agent] to be knowledge, it is impossible for the indication of the act that he is knowing to be an indication of his essence and existence, or of an attribute referring to his essence. And when that is established, the indicated object and attachment of the act must be knowledge and power. Another proof: and this also indicates that if it is sound and established that the meaning of 'the knower is knowing' and 'the powerful is powerful' is no more than that he possesses knowledge and power and from the existence of these two attributes in him, and that he does not, by being knowing and powerful, have two attributes and two states separate from knowledge and power or in the ruling of being separate from that, then the indication of the act that the knowing, powerful [agent] is knowing and powerful must be an indication of his knowledge and power, just as when it is established that the meaning of 'the black agent' is no more than the existence of blackness in him and the occurrence of the act from him, then the indication that he is black and agent must be an indication of the existence of blackness in him only and the occurrence of the act from him.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a proof (دليل) consisting of two arguments establishing that actions indicate distinct attributes (knowledge and power) in the agent, not the essence itself or a derivative state. The first argument uses analogy: if a thing were black by itself, its blackness would be its essence; but a knower cannot be knowledge itself (that would be absurd for both eternal and temporal beings), so the act's indication must refer to an attribute, not the essence. The second argument refines this: the meaning of 'knower' and 'powerful' is simply having knowledge and power, not separate states. Therefore, the act indicates these attributes directly, just as 'black' indicates the quality of blackness, not the essence. The author follows the argument's own order: first a conditional, then an impossibility, then a conclusion.

Key Terms

العلم
القدرة
الذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when we say 'God knows', we are referring directly to His essence.
Correction: Baqillani shows this leads to an absurdity: the essence would be identical with knowledge, which is impossible. So the act must indicate a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's first proof, why can't the act's indication be of the agent's essence itself?
A) Because the essence is unknowable.
B) Because if the essence were knowledge, every essence would be knowledge, which is impossible.
C) Because the act is separate from the essence.
D) Because the essence cannot have attributes.

Key Takeaway
Action indicates knowledge and power as real attributes, distinct from the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How would your worship change if you realized that His knowledge is a perfect, real attribute that He possesses eternally?
?

?
The proof shows that action points to attributes, not essence. When you see Allah's actions in your life—provision, mercy, justice—do you stop to recognize the specific attribute behind each act? How can you turn this into a daily habit of deepening your knowledge of Allah?

Second Proof: Action Directly Shows Attributes
Second proof: 'knower' means having knowledge, so act directly indicates the attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'knower' simply means having knowledge, so action directly indicates that attribute without intermediary.

Core Idea
Being a knower is having knowledge; action thus proves the attribute directly.

Baqillani offers a second proof that reinforces the first. He argues that the terms 'knower' and 'powerful' mean nothing more than possessing knowledge and power. Therefore, when an act indicates that the agent is knowing and powerful, it directly indicates those attributes—just as calling something 'black' directly indicates the quality of blackness, not the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second proof. Notice it begins with 'دليل آخر' and uses a structure of conditional and conclusion.
The argument is parallel to the first but refines the point.
2
Understand the meaning of 'knower'
The author asserts that 'knower' and 'powerful' simply mean possessing knowledge and power, not having some separate attribute or state.
This counters the idea that attributes could be non-distinct.
3
Apply the same logic to action
Since the act shows the agent is knowing, it directly shows that he has knowledge—just as calling something 'black' directly shows it has blackness.

Conclusion: The State Must Be an Existent Attribute
Baqillani concludes that a known state must be existent, affirming attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that any known attribute must be existent, and how this supports the Ash'ari doctrine of real divine attributes.

Core Idea
If a state is known, it must be existent, affirming divine attributes as real.

In this concluding passage, Baqillani takes the prior conclusion that the state is known and presses it to its logical conclusion. He argues that a known state cannot be non-existent, because a non-existent thing cannot produce specific rulings or relations. Therefore, it must be existent, and thus it is a real attribute. This validates the Ash'ari insistence that God's attributes are real and distinct from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure and the dichotomy.
The final sentence is incomplete; focus on the complete portion.
2
Follow the dichotomy
Baqillani presents two possibilities: the state is either existent or non-existent. Trace the consequences of each. Why is non-existent impossible?
Consider the specific examples: relation to Zayd vs. Amr, eternal vs. originated.
3
Reflect on the verbal dispute
Baqillani says the disagreement with Abu Hashim is only in terminology. Ask yourself: does this mean the Ash'ari position is ultimately correct?
The passage ends by hinting at a regress argument against the opponent's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كانت هذه الحال معلومة وجب أن تكون إما موجودة أو معدومة فإن كانت معدومة استحال أن توجب حكماً وأن تتعلق بزيد دون عمرو وبالقديم دون المحدث وإن كانت موجودة وجب أن تكون شيئاً وصفة متعلقة بالعالم، وهذا قولنا الذي نذهب إليه وإنما يحصل الخلاف في العبارة وفي تسمية هذا الشيء علماً أو حالاً وليس هذا بخلاف في المعنى فوجب صحة ما نذهب إليه في إثبات الصفات . وعلى أن هذه الحال على أصل القائل بها تقتضي إثبات أحوال لا
If this state is known, then it must be either existent or non-existent. If it is non-existent, it is impossible for it to necessitate a ruling and to relate to Zayd rather than ʿAmr and to the eternal rather than the originated. If it is existent, then it must be a thing and an attribute belonging to the world. This is our position to which we adhere; the disagreement is only in expression and in naming this thing 'knowledge' or 'state', and this is not a disagreement in meaning. So the correctness of what we hold in affirming the attributes is necessary. And that this state, according to the principle of its proponent, entails the affirmation of states not...
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument. Baqillani builds on the previous proof that the state is known. If it is known, it must be either existent or non-existent. If non-existent, it cannot impose any ruling (ḥukm) or be specifically related to one being rather than another, nor to the eternal (God) rather than the originated. Therefore, it must be existent. If existent, then it is a thing (shayʾ) and an attribute (ṣifa) belonging to the subject. This is the Ashʿarī position: the attributes are real and existent. Baqillani notes that the disagreement with Abu Hashim is merely verbal—whether to call it 'knowledge' or 'state'—and not a real difference in meaning. Thus, the Ashʿarī doctrine of affirming attributes (al-ṣifāt) is validated. The final sentence begins to argue that the state claimed by the opponent itself entails the affirmation of multiple states, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
موجود (existent)
معدوم (non-existent)
صفة (attribute)
حكم (ruling)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Baqillani is arguing for the existence of a separate entity called a 'state' (ḥāl) distinct from the essence and attributes.
Correction: Baqillani is actually arguing that any known attribute must be existent, and the dispute with the opponents is merely verbal — whether to call it 'state' or 'knowledge'. The real point is that attributes are real.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani conclude about the state if it is known?
A) It must be a mental construct with no external reality.
B) It must be either existent or non-existent; if existent, it is an attribute.
C) It is identical to the self.
D) It is unknown by definition.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that any known attribute must be existent, thereby affirming the reality of divine attributes against the theory of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat Allah’s attributes – like His knowledge, power, and mercy – as real and effective in your daily life, or do you consider them mere mental concepts? What would change if you truly believed they are existent and active?
?
Baqillani argues that a non-existent state cannot relate specifically to God rather than to creatures. When you think of Allah’s attributes, do you have a clear distinction between His attributes and human qualities? How might you deepen that distinction in your worship?
?
The dispute here is only verbal, but it carries real consequences for faith. In your own understanding, are there areas where you argue about words while missing the deeper truth about Allah? How can you move beyond labels to focus on the reality of His attributes?

Divine Attributes

The State (ḥāl) Is Known and Must Be Existent
Baqillani argues that the state (ḥāl) is knowable and must be an existent attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the state (ḥāl) is a knowable existent attribute, not a mere mental construct.

Core Idea
Knowing a thing's state is necessary; that state must be real and existing.

Baqillani tackles a subtle question: can we know the 'state' (ḥāl) of something without knowing the thing itself? He argues that knowledge of the self implies knowledge of its state, and that this state must be an existent attribute. This passage is central to the Ashʿarī defense of divine attributes as real, not merely conceptual.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English. Notice the logical flow: Baqillani first eliminates impossible scenarios, then establishes the state as known, then argues for its existence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three knowledge scenarios
Baqillani considers three options for what knowledge of the self and state could be. Find them and see why only the third is coherent.
This is the core of the first part.
3
Understand the argument from existence
After establishing the state is known, Baqillani asks whether it exists. Follow the reasoning: if non‑existent, it can't differentiate; if existent, it's an attribute.
This leads to affirming attributes.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument underpins the Ashʿarī view that God's attributes are real and distinct from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالنفس ولا بالحال فذلك محال من قولنا جميعاً وإن كان علماً بالنفس دون الحال فذلك محال وموجب لأن يكون العلم بالنفس أنها نفس علماً بالحال وأن يكون علم كل من علم ذات من له الحال ووجوده علم اختصاصه بتلك الحال وذلك محال وإن كان العلم بأن النفس على الحال علماً بالحال فقط فقد ثبت أن الحال معلومة وإن كان العلم بذلك علماً بالنفس والحال فقد وجب أن يكونا معلومين جميعاً وأن تكون الحال معلومة كما أن النفس معلومة وأن تكون النفس والحال في حكم معلومين لأنه قد يصح العلم بالنفس مع عدم العلم بالحال وعدم العلم بأن النفس على تلك الحال يصح العلم بزيد دون عمرو وهذا يبطل قولهم إن الحال غير معلومة. فإن كانت هذه الحال معلومة وجب أن تكون إما موجودة أو معدومة فإن كانت معدومة استحال أن توجب حكماً وأن تتعلق بزيد دون عمرو وبالقديم دون المحدث وإن كانت موجودة وجب أن تكون شيئاً وصفة متعلقة بالعالم وهذا قولنا الذي نذهب إليه وإنما يحصل الخلاف في العبارة وفي تسمية هذا الشيء علماً أو حالاً وليس هذا بخلاف في المعنى فوجب صحة ما نذهب إليه في إثبات الصفات.
If [knowledge is] of the self alone and not of the state, that is impossible from our statement altogether. And if knowledge is of the self without the state, that is impossible and necessitates that knowledge of the self as a self be knowledge of the state, and that the knowledge of everyone who knows the essence of the one who has the state and its existence be knowledge of its specific state, and that is impossible. And if knowledge that the self is upon the state is knowledge of the state only, then it is established that the state is known. And if knowledge of that is knowledge of the self and the state, then it must be that both are known together, and that the state is known as the self is known, and that the self and the state are in the category of two known things, because knowledge of the self may be valid without knowledge of the state, and lack of knowledge that the self is upon that state is like knowledge of Zayd without 'Amr. And this invalidates their statement that the state is not known. If this state is known, it must be either existent or non-existent. If it is non-existent, it is impossible that it necessitate a ruling and that it be specific to Zayd rather than 'Amr, and to the eternal rather than the originated. And if it is existent, it must be a thing and an attribute pertaining to the described. This is our position which we hold, and the disagreement is only in wording and in naming this thing 'knowledge' or 'state', and this is not a disagreement in meaning. Thus, the correctness of what we hold in affirming attributes is necessary.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the knowability and existence of the state (al-ḥāl). Baqillani begins by arguing that if knowledge of the self does not include knowledge of its state, then that is impossible because knowing the self as a self would require knowing its state. He presents three scenarios: (1) knowledge of self only, (2) knowledge of self and its state as separate, (3) knowledge that the self is upon a state. He shows that only the third scenario makes sense, establishing that the state is known. He then argues that if the state is known, it must be either existent or non-existent. If non-existent, it cannot cause a distinction (ḥukm) or be specific to one thing rather than another, so it is invalid. If existent, it must be a thing and an attribute (ṣifa) belonging to the described. This is the Ashʿarī position affirming attributes, and any difference is merely terminological.

Key Terms
نفس
حال
علم
وجود
عدم
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The state (ḥāl) is merely a mental concept with no external reality.
Correction: Baqillani argues that if the state were non-existent, it could not account for specific properties or differences between entities; therefore, it must be an existent attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, if the state (ḥāl) were non-existent, what problem would arise?
A) It would be indistinguishable from nothing.
B) It could not necessitate a ruling or be specific to a particular thing.
C) It would still be known as a mental concept.
D) It would require an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The state (ḥāl) is a real, existent attribute that is known along with the essence, otherwise it could not cause distinctions or rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you know your own inner state—your real intentions, fears, and attachments—as more than just passing thoughts? Or do you treat them as unreal, refusing to accept that they exist and shape your actions? What would change if you truly acknowledged them as real attributes of your soul?
?
Baqillani says the disagreement about the state is only in wording, not in meaning. Are there beliefs you hold tightly to words or labels, while missing the reality they point to? Where in your faith are you arguing about terms while neglecting the truth they signify?
?
If Allah's attributes are real and existent, then knowing Him requires knowing His attributes. Do you truly know Allah through His names and attributes, or do you settle for a vague, attribute-less concept of God? What step will you take today to learn one divine attribute deeply?
