Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing one essential attribute implies similarity between two entities.
Correction: Sharing one attribute does not entail similarity; blackness and whiteness share the attribute of being contradictory but are not similar.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what example does he use to challenge the idea that sharing one attribute implies similarity?
A) The similarity of fire and heat.
B) The similarity of blackness and whiteness.
C) The similarity of God and His knowledge.
D) The similarity of man and his hand.

Key Takeaway
Sharing one attribute does not force two things to be similar; Baqillani uses blackness and whiteness to illustrate this point and refute the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God knows everything. But have you ever, even slightly, imagined His knowledge as being like yours—only bigger? How does Baqillani's refutation challenge that tendency? What will you do today to train your mind to stop comparing God to creation?
?
If sharing one attribute does not make two things similar, why do you still sometimes equate God's attributes with human traits? Repent now and commit to learning the correct way of affirming attributes without tashbīh.
?
Baqillani uses a simple logical example to protect tawheed. After this lesson, are you willing to actively guard your thoughts about God's attributes? Name one specific anthropomorphic thought you will reject today and replace with the correct belief.

Further Refutations: Alternative Interpretations of Eternity and the Analogy with Created Things
Baqillani presents alternative interpretations and a reductio argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's multiple strategies to refute the objection, including alternative ways to describe God's knowledge as eternal and a reductio ad absurdum using the example of man and his knowledge.

Core Idea
Eternity does not have to be 'by itself' for everything; there are multiple valid interpretations.

Having challenged the similarity assumption, Baqillani now offers several alternative ways to understand how God's knowledge is eternal. He also turns the opponent's logic against them by using the example of man's originated knowledge, leading to a reductio ad absurdum that exposes the inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Examine the alternative interpretations
Read the section where Baqillani presents several possibilities for how God and His knowledge could be eternal. List the alternatives he offers.
Alternatives include: both eternal by one shared eternity, Creator eternal by an eternity and knowledge eternal by itself, or knowledge neither eternal nor originated.
2
Analyze the reductio with man and his knowledge
Focus on Baqillani's argument that if the opponent's logic holds, then man and his knowledge would be similar because both are originated. What does the opponent say in response?
The opponent denies that originated things are originated by themselves.
3
Trace the final reductio
Follow Baqillani's final line of reasoning: if every eternal is eternal by itself, then any two eternals would be similar. Then he counters the opponent's defense by applying the same logic to blackness and whiteness.
The opponent is forced into a contradiction: if they insist on 'by itself' for eternity, then blackness and whiteness would also have to be similar by the same reasoning.

Another doubt of theirs.
For if they say: If the Creator (al-Bāri), exalted is He, possesses knowledge by which He has always been knowing, then it would be necessary that He be eternal by Himself (qadīm li-nafsihī) just as what is known by Him is eternal by itself. And if the two were eternal by themselves, it would be necessary that they be two similar (mithlayn) and resembling (mushtabihayn) things, and that knowledge be a god, living, powerful, knowing, subsisting by itself, and that the knower (al-ʿālim) be an attribute, not living, not knowing, not powerful, not subsisting by itself, because it resembles what has this description.

Then it is said to them: Do you deny, if your statement were correct, that blackness and whiteness would be resembling because they are contradictory and different (ghayrayn) by themselves and their description in that regard is equal? They will find no escape from that.
Then it is said to them: And why have you also said that the Creator, exalted is He, if He is eternal, is eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge? And why do you deny that the two are eternal by an eternity that is an eternity for them? And why do you deny that the Creator is eternal by an eternity and knowledge is eternal by itself? And why do you also deny that knowledge is neither eternal nor originated (muḥdath), according to the statement of those among our companions who said that? They will find no escape from that.
Then it is said to them: If what you have said is necessary, then why do you deny that man is similar to his knowledge, since the two are originated (muḥdathayn) by themselves? If they say: According to us, the originated is not originated by itself, but rather it is originated neither by itself nor by a cause; so what you asked does not follow. Thus the similarity of the two eternals does not follow. If they say: It is necessary that the eternal be eternal by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. It is said to them: Say, for this very reason, that blackness and whiteness are two things, other (ghayrān), different (khilāfān), colors, accidents (ʿaraḍān) by themselves, because they are not known except thus; and also say that each one of them is one (wāḥid) by itself because its essence is not known except as one. And there is no escape for them from that, even if they refuse (abū).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection (shubha) raised by opponents regarding God's eternal knowledge. The objection states that if God has eternal knowledge, then both God and His knowledge would be eternal by themselves (qadīm li-nafsihi), leading to two eternal beings, which would make them similar and thus require that knowledge be considered a separate divine entity possessing divine attributes. The author, Imam Baqillani, responds by first pointing out that sharing one attribute does not necessarily imply similarity; he gives the example of blackness and whiteness, which share the attribute of being mutually exclusive (khalīfayn) yet are not considered similar. He then challenges the opponent's insistence on 'eternal by itself', offering alternative interpretations: both could be eternal by one shared eternity, or the Creator could be eternal by an eternity while knowledge is eternal by itself, or knowledge could be neither eternal nor originated as some theologians hold. Furthermore, he turns the argument around by asking about man and his knowledge: if the opponent's logic holds, then man and his knowledge would be similar because both are originated by themselves — but the opponent might deny that the originated is originated by itself. Baqillani then generalizes: if all that is eternal is described as eternal by itself, then any two eternals would be similar, which is absurd. The opponent may then claim that eternity by itself is necessary because the essence of the eternal is known only as eternal. Baqillani counters that by the same reasoning, blackness and whiteness would have to be similar because they are known only as colors, accidents, etc., and thereby forces a reductio ad absurdum: if the opponent concedes that, they must accept the similarity of blackness and whiteness, which is contrary to common sense. The argument systematically dismantles the opponent's position by exposing logical inconsistencies.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only way for something to be eternal is to be eternal by itself (qadīm li-nafsihi).
Correction: Baqillani shows that there are multiple valid interpretations; God's knowledge could be eternal by an eternal attribute, or even neither eternal nor originated.

Checkpoint
What is one of the alternative interpretations Baqillani offers for how God's knowledge could be eternal?
A) Knowledge is eternal by itself like God.
B) Knowledge is originated (muḥdath).
C) God and knowledge are both eternal by one shared eternity.
D) Knowledge is neither eternal nor originated.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by offering multiple interpretations of eternity and using a reductio ad absurdum to expose the inconsistency in the opponent's reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your understanding of God's attributes is the only possible one, dismissing alternatives without consideration? This lesson shows multiple valid interpretations of eternity. In what area of your belief are you most rigid, and are you willing to consider that you might be limiting Allah's transcendence? What would it take for you to genuinely examine an alternative view?
?
Baqillani uses reductio ad absurdum to expose the inconsistency of the opponent. When you argue about religious matters, do you seek truth or victory? Are you willing to have your own position dismantled if it leads to a deeper truth? Do you approach theological discussions with humility, or do you cling to your views out of pride? This lesson calls you to intellectual honesty. What hidden assumptions do you hold that you might be defending instead of seeking truth?
?
The opponent's reasoning led to absurdities like equating blackness and whiteness. How many of your own beliefs about God are based on faulty analogies with creation? Are you willing to examine them critically, even if it feels uncomfortable? This lesson challenges you to refine your theology. What is one belief you hold about Allah that you have never questioned? Will you question it today?

Divine Attributes

The Opening Challenge: Alternatives to 'Eternal by Self'
Baqillani presents analogies and alternatives to challenge the necessity of 'eternal by itself'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani opens the refutation by presenting alternative possibilities for God's eternity and His knowledge, challenging the opponent's assumption of necessity.

Core Idea
The opponent's claim of 'eternal by itself' is not the only possibility; other logical options exist.

In this passage, Baqillani begins his dialectical assault on the claim that if God is eternal, He must be eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge. He first draws an analogy with black and white to show that the opponent's reasoning leads to problematic conclusions. Then he presents a series of 'what prevents you from saying' alternatives, forcing the opponent to consider that eternity could be by an attribute, or that knowledge might be neither eternal nor originated. This sets the stage for the deeper refutation to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial analogy
Notice how Baqillani starts with a question about black and white being alike in being different by themselves. This is a strategic move to force consistency.
The opponent likely concedes this point, creating a starting premise.
2
Follow the hypothetical alternatives
Baqillani then asks three 'what prevents you from saying' alternatives: that both God and His knowledge are eternal by a single eternity; that the Creator is eternal by an eternity and knowledge eternal by itself; and that knowledge is neither eternal nor originated. Note the opponent cannot escape these.
These alternatives show that the opponent's claim is not the only logical option.
3
Reflect on the strategic purpose
Consider why Baqillani presents these alternatives. He is not committing to them but using them to undermine the necessity of the opponent's position.
This is a classic dialectical method: presenting multiple possible explanations to show the opponent's is not forced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك صحيحاً، أن يكون السواد والبياض مشتبهين من حيث كانا خلافين غيرين لأنفسهما وكان وصفهما بذلك متساوياً؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً . ثم يقال لهم : ولم قلتم أيضاً إن الباري سبحانه إذا كان قديماً كان قديماً لنفسه وكذلك علمه ؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكونا قديمين بقدم هو قدم لهما؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكون الباري قديماً بقدم والعلم قديماً بنفسه؟ . وما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون العلم ليس بقديم ولا بمحدث على قول من قال ذلك من أصحابنا؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً . ثم يقال لهم : إن كان ما قلتموه واجباً فما أنكرتم أن يكون الإنسان مثلاً لعلمه إذ كانا محدثين لأنفسهما؟ فإن قالوا: ليس المحدث عندنا محدثاً لنفسه ، بل هو محدث لا لنفسه ولا لعلة؛ فلم يجب ما سألتم قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون كل قديم وصف بالقدم من صفة وموصوف فإنه قديم لا لنفسه ولا لعلة ؛ فلا يجب بذلك تماثل القديمين؟ فإن قالوا : إنما وجب أن يكون القديم قديماً لنفسه لأن نفسه لا تعلم إلا قديمةً، قيل لهم : فقولوا لأجل هذا بعينه إن السواد والبياض شيئان غيران خلافان لونان عرضان لأنفسهما ؛ لأنهما لا يعلمان إلا كذلك ؛ وقولوا أيضاً إن كل واحد منهما واحد لنفسه؛ لأن نفسه لا تعلم إلا واحدة ؛ فإن مروا على ذلك، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من وجوب تماثلهما، إذا كانا مشتركين في هذه الأوصاف لأنفسهما؟ ولا محيص لهم من ذلك ، وإن أبوه وقالوا : إن هذه الأوصاف جارية على السواد والبياض لا لأنفسهما ولا لعلة وإن لم تعلم أنفسهما إلا عليهما ، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون القديم وعلمه قديمين لا لأنفسها ولا لعلة وإن لم تعلم أنفسهما إلا قديمتين ؟ ولا فصل لهم في ذلك ؛ وفيه سقوط ما عولوا عليه .
That is correct: that black and white are alike in that they are two different, other [things] in themselves, and their description with that is equal? They will find no escape from that. Then it is said to them: Why did you also say that the Creator, exalted, if He is eternal, then He is eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge? And what prevents you from saying that both are eternal by an eternity that is an eternity for them both? And what prevents you from saying that the Creator is eternal by an eternity and knowledge is eternal by itself? And what prevents you also from saying that knowledge is neither eternal nor originated, according to the one who said that among our fellows? They will find no escape from that. Then it is said to them: If what you said is necessary, then what prevents you from saying that a human, for example, is [eternal]? for his knowledge, since both are originated by themselves? If they say: The originated, according to us, is not originated by itself, but rather it is originated neither by itself nor by a cause; so what you asked does not follow. It is said to them: So what prevents you also from saying that every eternal described with eternity, whether attribute or subject, is eternal neither by itself nor by a cause; so by that, the likeness of the two eternals is not necessitated? If they say: It is only necessary that the eternal is eternal by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. It is said to them: So say, for this very reason, that black and white are two things, other, different, two colors, two accidents by themselves, because they are not known except as such; and say also that each one of them is one by itself, because its essence is not known except as one. If they pass over that, it is said to them: So what prevents you from necessitating their likeness, since they share these descriptions by themselves? They have no escape from that, even if they refuse and say: These descriptions apply to black and white neither by themselves nor by a cause, even though their essences are not known except upon them. It is said to them: So what prevents you also from saying that the eternal and His knowledge are two eternals neither by themselves nor by a cause, even though their essences are not known except as two eternals? They have no distinction in that, and in it falls what they relied upon.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of an opponent's claim that if God is eternal (qadim), He must be eternal by Himself (li-nafsihi), and likewise His knowledge. The author (Baqillani) begins by drawing an analogy with black and white, arguing that they are distinct by themselves and yet share the description of being different—so if the opponent insists on 'by itself' for God, they would have to accept that black and white are also 'by themselves' in being different, which leads to problems. He then presents a series of hypothetical objections and answers: first, if the opponent says the Creator is eternal by Himself, then why not say His knowledge is also eternal by itself? Or why not say both are eternal by a single eternity? The author points out that there is no escape from these questions. Next, he challenges the opponent's necessity: if they claim that the eternal must be eternal by itself, then why not say a human is eternal? by his knowledge, since both are originated? The opponent replies that the originated is not originated by itself. The author then turns the table: if the opponent admits that the eternal is eternal by itself because it cannot be conceived otherwise, then the same reasoning would make black and white 'by themselves' in being different, rendering them similar (mutamathilan). The opponent tries to avoid this by saying the attributes apply neither by themselves nor by a cause, but the author counters that the same can be said of God and His knowledge: they are eternal neither by themselves nor by a cause, yet known as eternal. Thus the opponent's position collapses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that 'eternal by itself' is the only logical way for something to be eternal.
Correction: Baqillani shows that multiple logical alternatives exist (e.g., by an attribute, or neither eternal nor originated), so the opponent's claim is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative Baqillani presents regarding the eternity of God and His knowledge?
A) Both are eternal by a single eternity
B) God is eternal by Himself, knowledge by an attribute
C) Knowledge is originated
D) Both are eternal by themselves

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's opening move shows that the opponent's claim of 'eternal by itself' is not necessary; other logical possibilities exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a theological belief, do you ever consider that there might be other logical possibilities you haven't considered? How does that realization affect your certainty? Will you actively seek out alternative explanations before settling on a view?
?
The opponent in this passage assumed 'eternal by itself' was necessary. In your own life, have you ever held a belief about God or religion as the only possibility, only to later discover other valid perspectives? How did that change your faith? What step will you take this week to examine an assumption you hold about Allah?
?
Baqillani challenges the necessity of a claim. Are you willing to have your cherished beliefs challenged in the same way? What would it take for you to genuinely entertain alternative explanations? Will you invite someone to challenge your view on a theological matter you think is settled?

The Reductio: How 'By Itself' Leads to Absurdity
Baqillani completes the refutation with a reductio and final closure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani forces the opponent into a logical collapse by showing that the 'by itself' reasoning leads to unintended similarities between black and white and between God and His knowledge.

Core Idea
If 'by itself' is necessary, then black and white become similar, which is absurd.

In this continuation, Baqillani turns the opponent's own reasoning against them. If they insist that the eternal must be eternal by itself because it cannot be known otherwise, then the same logic would make black and white 'by themselves' in being different, and therefore similar. When the opponent tries to escape by denying that descriptions apply by themselves or by a cause, Baqillani applies the same escape to God and His knowledge, leading to the complete collapse of their position.

Guided Path
1
Trace the reductio argument
Baqillani starts with 'If what you said is necessary...' and then asks why a human isn't eternal? The opponent answers that the originated is not by itself. This sets up the next move.
2
Analyze the opponent's second argument
The opponent says the eternal must be by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. Baqillani turns this around: then black and white must be different by themselves, making them similar.
This is the key logical thrust.
3
Examine the opponent's escape attempt
The opponent then says the descriptions apply neither by themselves nor by a cause. Baqillani applies the same to God and knowledge, showing the opponent's position collapses.
Baqillani concludes: 'They have no distinction in that, and in it falls what they relied upon.'

That is correct: that black and white are alike in that they are two different, other [things] in themselves, and their description with that is equal? They will find no escape from that. Then it is said to them: Why did you also say that the Creator, exalted, if He is eternal, then He is eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge? It is said to them: So what prevents you also from saying that every eternal described with eternity, whether attribute or subject, is eternal neither by itself nor by a cause; so by that, the likeness of the two eternals is not necessitated? If they say: It is only necessary that the eternal is eternal by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. They have no distinction in that, and in it falls what they relied upon.

Refuting the Doubt about God's Knowledge
Baqillani refutes the opponent's doubt about God's knowledge being like human knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the opponent's claim that God cannot have knowledge because it would resemble human knowledge.

Core Idea
Similarity of knowledges is not proven; the opponent's assumption is unsupported.

The opponents raise a doubt: if God had knowledge, it would relate to objects like human knowledge, making it similar and thus impossible. Baqillani counters by questioning the basis of their claim about similarity. He forces them to justify their premise, exposing its weakness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read English translation. Notice the structure: opponent's argument, then Baqillani's response.
The passage is dialogical—like a debate.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
Find the core analogy: if God had knowledge, it would relate like ours, hence similar. Since God cannot be like us, He has no knowledge.
3
Follow Baqillani's dialectical move
Baqillani asks: do you know this by necessity or reasoning? He then handles both possibilities. Notice how he shifts the burden of proof.
This is a classic kalam tactic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة لهم أخرى وإن هم قالوا : الدليل على أن الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلم أنه لو كان له علم لوجب أن يتعلق بالمعلومات على وجه تعلق علومنا بها ولو كان كذلك لوجب أن يكون علمه من جنس علومنا لأن العلمين إنما يجب تماثلهما لتعلقهما بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد فلما لم يجز أن يكون علمه من جنس علومنا ثبت أنه لا علم له يقال لهم : لم قلتم إن طريق العلم بتماثل العلمين المحدثين هو أن يكون متعلقهما واحداً على وجه واحد؟ أباضطرار علمتم هذا أم بنظر واستدلال؟ . فإن قالوا : باضطرار أمسك عنهم أو قلب الكلام عليهم في منع تماثل ما هذه سبيله وادع فيه علم الاضطرار وإن قالوا : بنظر، قيل لهم : وما هو؟ فإن قالوا : هو علمنا بتماثل كل علمين من علومنا إذا كان متعلقهما واحداً على وجه واحد، قيل لهم وما في هذا من الدليل؟ وما أنكرتم : أنهما لم يتماثلا لهذه العلة ولكن لأنفسهما فقط ومن حيث علم أنه لا صفة جازت على أحدهما إلا وهي جائزة على الآخر ولا صفة
Another doubt of theirs: And if they say: The evidence that God, exalted, is not permissible to be knowing by a knowledge is that if He had knowledge, it would necessarily relate to objects known in the manner of the relation of our knowledge to them, and if that were the case, His knowledge would necessarily be of the same genus as our knowledge, because two knowledges are only necessarily similar (tamathul) due to their relation to one known object in one manner. So when it is not permissible that His knowledge be of the genus of our knowledge, it is established that He has no knowledge. It is said to them: Why did you say that the way of knowing the similarity of two originated knowledges is that their object of relation is one in one manner? Did you know this by necessity or by reasoning and inference? If they say: By necessity, then one refrains from them or turns the argument against them concerning the denial of similarity of what is of this way, and claim there necessary knowledge. And if they say: By reasoning, it is said to them: And what is it? If they say: It is our knowledge of the similarity of every two knowledges from our knowledges when their object of relation is one in one manner, it is said to them: And what is the evidence in this? And what prevents you from [saying] that they are not similar for this cause but only by themselves? And from where it is known that no attribute is permitted of one of them except that it is also permitted of the other, and no attribute...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage introduces a new doubt (shubhah) from the opponents. They argue: If God were knowing by a knowledge, that knowledge would have to relate to objects of knowledge in the same way as human knowledge. If that were the case, then His knowledge would be of the same genus as human knowledge, and since two knowledges that relate to the same object in the same manner are similar, and it is impossible for God's knowledge to be like human knowledge (since God is incomparable), it follows that God does not have knowledge. Baqillani responds by questioning the basis of their claim about similarity of knowledges. He asks: Do you know this by necessity (idṭirār) or by reasoning (naẓar)? If they claim it is by necessity, the author suggests either to stop or to turn the argument against them by denying the similarity in this case and claiming necessary knowledge to the contrary. If they claim it is by reasoning, he asks for their proof. Their reasoning is that we know from our own sciences that any two knowledges are similar when they relate to the same object in the same manner. Baqillani then challenges: What proof is there in that? And what prevents the knowledges from being similar not for that reason but only by themselves? The passage is cut off, but the response continues the dialectic.

Key Terms
علم
تعلق
تماثل
علومنا
اضطرار
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge, if it existed, would necessarily be similar to human knowledge because both relate to the same objects.
Correction: The similarity of two knowledges is not proven simply by their sharing the same relation to objects; it could be due to their own essences or other factors.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the opponents' doubt about God having knowledge?
A) God's knowledge would have to be eternal by itself
B) God's knowledge would have to relate to objects like human knowledge, making it similar and thus impossible
C) Knowledge is an accident and cannot be attributed to God
D) God's knowledge would require a multiplicity of eternal entities

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that God cannot have knowledge rests on an unproven premise about similarity of knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's knowledge should align with your own understanding—that He should see things the way you do? What did that assumption cost you in your trust in Him? Today, will you let go of that assumption and accept that His knowledge is entirely unlike yours, yet perfect?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or incomprehensible, do you instinctively judge God's wisdom based on your limited perspective? What would it look like to sincerely affirm that His knowledge is not of the same kind as yours, and to submit to that?
?
The opponent in this lesson insisted on a principle they could not prove. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that you have assumed to be obvious, but have never truly examined? Will you courageously question them today, trusting that true faith welcomes honest inquiry?

Refuting an Objection on Attributes
Baqillani refutes an objection about attributes applying without cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the opponents' claim that attributes apply to accidents without cause, by turning the argument back on them regarding God and His attributes.

Core Idea
If attributes apply without cause to accidents, the same applies to God and His knowledge.

In this short but powerful passage, Baqillani engages with an objection from opponents who argue that attributes such as blackness and whiteness inhere in accidents without any cause. He turns their own reasoning against them, showing that if this is allowed for black and white, it must also be allowed for God and His knowledge. The opponents cannot provide a valid distinction, so their argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening line where the opponents present their claim: attributes apply to black and white not because of themselves nor because of a cause, yet their selves are only known through those attributes.
Notice the structure: they assert both a negative (no cause) and a positive (known through attributes).
2
Analyze Baqillani's counter-argument
Baqillani responds by applying the same logic to God and His knowledge: why not say they are eternal not because of themselves nor a cause? The opponents have no distinction.
This is a classic refutation by analogy or reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the collapse of their argument
The final sentence declares that the foundation of their argument collapses. Reflect on why this is a decisive refutation.
Without a distinct principle, their objection loses force.
4
Test your understanding
Attempt the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the logic of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا : إن هذه الأوصاف جارية على السواد والبياض لا لأنفسهما ولا لعلة وإن لم تُعلم أنفسهما إلا عليهما ، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون القديم وعلمه قديمين لا لأنفسها ولا لعلة وإن لم تُعلم أنفسهما إلا قديمتين ؟ ولا فصل لهم في ذلك ؛ وفيه سقوط ما عولوا عليه .
And they said: These descriptions apply to black and white not because of themselves nor because of a cause, and yet their selves are not known except through them. It was said to them: So why do you not also deny that the Eternal and His knowledge are two eternals not because of themselves nor because of a cause, even though their selves are not known except as two eternals? They have no distinction in that; and in it lies the collapse of what they based (their argument) upon.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a brief objection from the opponents regarding the application of attributes (awṣāf) to accidents such as blackness and whiteness, followed by the author's refutation. The opponents claim that these attributes inhere in black and white not due to the essences themselves nor due to any external cause, yet the essences of black and white are only known through these attributes. The author responds by turning the argument back on them: why do they not also say that the Eternal (God) and His knowledge are eternal not due to themselves nor due to a cause, even though their essences are only known as eternals? The opponents have no valid distinction to make here, so their argument's foundation collapses.

Key Terms
أوصاف
سواد
بياض
قديم
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributes are necessarily caused by the essence; the opponents here argue that attributes exist without cause, but they wrongly limit this to accidents.
Correction: If uncaused attributes are possible for accidents, they are also possible for God's attributes. The opponents cannot consistently reject divine attributes while accepting accidental ones.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani refute the opponents' claim that attributes apply to black and white without cause?
A) He denies that attributes apply to black and white at all.
B) He applies the same reasoning to God and His knowledge, showing the opponents have no distinction.
C) He argues that black and white have no attributes.
D) He accepts their claim and offers no refutation.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by showing its logic applies equally to God and His knowledge, leaving the opponents without a valid distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life do you apply one standard to yourself and a stricter standard to others, or vice versa? Commit today to identifying one such double standard and correcting it with the same consistency Baqillani demands of the opponents.
?
When you face objections to your beliefs, do you look for genuine, principled responses, or do you rely on convenient distinctions that do not hold up under scrutiny? What is one area where you need to be more intellectually honest, and what step will you take this week?
?
The opponents had no valid distinction. Think of a disagreement you have with someone about a matter of faith. Are you sure your position is not based on an arbitrary distinction? Are you willing to apply the same logic to your own stance and see if it survives? If it does not, what will you do?

The Doubt about God's Knowledge and Its Epistemological Flaw
Baqillani presents and refutes a doubt about divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doubt that God cannot have knowledge because it would be similar to human knowledge, and how Baqillani refutes it by questioning whether the similarity criterion is known necessarily or by reasoning.

Core Idea
The opponents' claim of similarity is not self-evident nor proven.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a doubt raised by opponents who argue that if God had knowledge, it would have to resemble human knowledge. He begins by examining the basis for asserting similarity between two knowledges, asking whether it is known necessarily or through reasoning. This initial dialectical move undermines the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and response
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how the doubt is presented and Baqillani's first response.
Focus on the first part: from 'شبهة لهم أخرى' until 'أباضطرار علمتم هذا أم بنظر واستدلال؟'.
2
Analyze the two options
Baqillani asks whether the similarity criterion is known necessarily or by reasoning. Consider the implications of each answer.
If necessarily, they are silenced; if by reasoning, they must provide proof.
3
Identify the flaw
Reflect on why this question poses a problem for the opponents. They cannot prove the criterion without circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة لهم أخرى
وإن هم قالوا : الدليل على أن الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلم أنه لو كان له علم لوجب أن يتعلق بالمعلومات على وجه تعلق علومنا بها ولو كان كذلك لوجب أن يكون علمه من جنس علومنا لأن العلمين إنما يجب تماثلهما لتعلقهما بمعلوم واحد على وجه واحد فلما لم يجز أن يكون علمه من جنس علومنا ثبت أنه لا علم له يقال لهم : لم قلتم إن طريق العلم بتماثل العِلْمين المُحْدَثين هو أن يكون مُتَعَلِّقهما واحداً على وجه واحد؟ أباضطرار علمتم هذا أم بنظر واستدلال؟ .
فإن قالوا : باضطرار أمسك عنهم أو قُلبَ الكلام عليهم في منع تماثل ما هذه سبيله وادعِي فيه عِلمُ الاضطرار وإن قالوا : بنظر، قيل لهم : وما هو؟ فإن قالوا : هو عِلْمُنا بتماثل كلِّ عِلْمين من علومنا إذا كان مُتَعَلَّقُهما واحداً على وجه واحد، قيل لهم وما في هذا من الدليل؟ وما أنكرتم : أنهما لم يتماثلا لهذه العلة ولكن لأنفسهما فقط ومن حيث عُلم أنه لا صفة جازت على أحدهما إلا وهي جائزة على الآخر ولا صفة لأحدهما إلا وهي واجبة للآخر وليس كذلك سبيل علم القديم وعلم المحدث . ثم يقال لهم : لو كان جهة العلم بتماثل ما له تَعَلُّق بغير أن يكون مُتَعَلَّقُهما واحداً على وجه واحد لوجب أن تكون الإرادة المتعلقتان بالشيء الواحد المقدور والمراد على وجه الحدوث متماثلين لتعلقهما بمتَعَلَّقٍ واحداً على وجه واحد فلما بطل هذا من قولنا وقولكم بطل اعتباركم الذي إليه استندتم .
ثم يقال لهم : فيجب على اعتلالكم هذا إذا كان القديم سبحانه عالماً لنفسه وبنفسه أن تكون نفسه كنفس علومنا لأنها متعلقة بالمعلومات كتعلق علومنا بها فلما لم يجز ذلك لم يجز أن يكون عالماً بنفسه . فإن قالوا : نحن لا نقول : إنه عالم بالمعلومات بنفسه على أنه بنفسه يَعْلَمُها وأن المعلومات مُتعَلَّقَةٌ بها وإنما نريد بذلك أنه عالم بها لا لمعنى يقارن نفسه فعبرنا عن هذا المعنى بأنه عالم بنفسه قيل لهم : وكذلك نحن لسنا نريد بقولنا إن القديم تعالى يعلم المعلومات بنفس علمه أن علمه آلة له ومتعلّق بالمعلومات تعلق الحبل بالحبل والجسم تَعَلَّقَ بالجسم وإنما نعني بقولنا إنه يعلم المعلومات بنفس علمه أنه يعلمها لا لمعنى يقارن العلم فعبرنا عن ذلك بأنه يعلم بنفس العلم وكذلك كل شيء
Another doubt of theirs: And if they say: The evidence that Allah Almighty cannot be knowing by a knowledge is that if He had knowledge, it would necessarily have to relate to known things in the manner of our knowledge relating to them, and if that were so, His knowledge would have to be of the same kind as our knowledge, because two knowledges are only necessarily similar due to their relation to one known thing in one manner. So when it is impossible that His knowledge be of the kind of our knowledge, it is established that He has no knowledge. It is said to them: Why do you say that the way of knowing the similarity of two created knowledges is that their objects are one in one manner? Did you know this necessarily or by reasoned investigation? If they say: Necessarily, they should be silenced or the argument is turned against them regarding the denial of similarity of that which is like this, and it is claimed in it that the knowledge of necessity. And if they say: By reasoning, it is said to them: And what is it? If they say: It is our knowledge that every two knowledges from among our knowledges are similar when their objects are one in one manner, it is said to them: And what is in this as evidence? And what prevents you that they are not similar due to this cause but only due to themselves, and from the perspective that it is known that no attribute is permissible for one of them except that it is also permissible for the other, and no attribute for one except that it is necessary for the other, and that is not the case for the eternal knowledge and the temporal knowledge. Then it is said to them: If the mode of knowing the similarity of that which relates were other than their objects being one in one manner, then it would be necessary that two wills relating to the same thing which is predetermined and intended in a temporal manner would be similar due to their relation to one object in one manner. But when this is false according to us and you, your consideration upon which you relied is false. Then it is said to them: According to your reasoning, if the Eternal Almighty is knowing by Himself and by His essence, then His essence would have to be like our knowledges because it relates to known things as our knowledges relate to them. But when that is not possible, it is not possible that He be knowing by Himself. If they say: We do not say that He is knowing of the known things by His essence in the sense that He knows them by His essence and that the known things are related to it; rather we mean by that that He is knowing of them not by a meaning that accompanies His essence, so we expressed this meaning by His being knowing by His essence. It is said to them: Similarly, we do not mean by our saying that the Eternal Almighty knows the known things by His knowledge that His knowledge is an instrument for Him and related to the known things like the attachment of a rope to a rope and a body attaching to a body. Rather, we mean by our saying that He knows the known things by His knowledge that He knows them not by a meaning that accompanies the knowledge, so we expressed that by His knowing by the knowledge. And likewise everything.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy refutation of a doubt (shubha) raised by the opponents against the necessity of God having an attribute of knowledge (ʿilm). The opponents argue that if God had knowledge, it would have to relate to things like human knowledge does, and thus be similar; but since God's knowledge cannot be like ours, He has no knowledge. Baqillani responds by first questioning the basis for claiming similarity of two knowledges: is it known necessarily or by reasoning? If necessarily, the opponents are silenced; if by reasoning, their argument is flawed because similarity might be due to the knowledges themselves, not just their relation. He then shows that their criterion leads to contradictions: if similarity were solely based on relation to one object, then two wills relating to the same object would also have to be similar, which is false. Finally, he clarifies a misunderstanding about what it means for God to be knowing by Himself (bi-nafsihi) or by His knowledge (bi-ʿilmihi). The opponents misinterpret 'by Himself' as meaning the essence directly knows like an instrument, but the correct meaning is that there is no separate cause accompanying the attribute. Similarly, 'by His knowledge' does not mean knowledge is an instrument; it means God knows without a cause that accompanies the knowledge. This removes the basis of the doubt.

تماثل

قديم

اضطرار
نظر

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the first flaw in the opponents' argument that God has no knowledge?
A) They assume God's knowledge must relate to objects like human knowledge.
B) They cannot prove that similarity of knowledges is solely due to relation to the same object.
C) They rely on a false premise that God exists.
D) They ignore the distinction between created and eternal knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' claim that similarity of knowledges is determined solely by relation to the same object is not established and can be refuted by the possibility that similarity is intrinsic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as similar to your own limited understanding? This lesson exposes the fallacy of that assumption. What specific steps will you take to purify your conception of Allah's attributes from any traces of anthropomorphism?
?
The opponents based their doubt on a single criterion without verifying its validity. Are there any beliefs or doubts you hold about your faith that you have accepted without proper investigation? Will you commit to studying the foundational proofs of those beliefs?
?
Baqillani distinguishes between necessary and reasoned knowledge. As you examine your own faith, which of your beliefs rest on solid, necessary knowledge grounded in revelation, and which have you accepted through weak reasoning? Take a moment to identify one area and strengthen it.

The Will Analogy and Clarifying Divine Knowledge Terminology
Baqillani refutes the doubt using a will analogy and clarifies divine knowledge terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a counterexample with will to refute the opponents' criterion, and how he clarifies the meaning of God being knowing 'by Himself' and 'by His knowledge'.

Core Idea
The opponents' criterion leads to absurdities, and divine knowledge is not an instrument.

Baqillani continues his refutation by showing that the opponents' criterion for similarity, if applied consistently, would force them to admit that two wills relating to the same object must also be similar—which is false. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding about what it means for God to be knowing by Himself or by His knowledge, emphasizing that these phrases do not imply instrumentalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterexample
Read from 'ثم يقال لهم : لو كان جهة العلم بتماثل...' until the end of the passage. Notice the analogy with will.
Baqillani uses a parallel argument to show inconsistency.
2
Analyze the will analogy
If similarity were based solely on relation to one object, then two wills to the same thing would be similar. But they are not necessarily similar, so the criterion is false.
3
Understand the clarification
Opponents might claim God is knowing by Himself, meaning His essence knows directly. Baqillani explains that 'by Himself' does not mean the essence is an instrument; similarly, 'by His knowledge' does not mean knowledge is a separate tool.
The correct meaning is that there is no separate cause accompanying the attribute.

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy refutation of a doubt (shubha) raised by the opponents against the necessity of God having an attribute of knowledge (ʿilm). The opponents argue that if God had knowledge, it would have to relate to things like human knowledge does, and thus be similar; but since God's knowledge cannot be like ours, He has no knowledge. Baqillani responds by first questioning the basis for claiming similarity of two knowledges: is it known necessarily or by reasoning? If necessarily, the opponents are silenced; if by reasoning, their argument is flawed because similarity might be due to the knowledges themselves, not just their relation. Finally, he clarifies a misunderstanding about what it means for God to be knowing by Himself (bi-nafsihi) or by His knowledge (bi-ʿilmihi). The opponents misinterpret 'by Himself' as meaning the essence directly knows like an instrument, but the correct meaning is that there is no separate cause accompanying the attribute. Similarly, 'by His knowledge' does not mean knowledge is an instrument; it means God knows without a cause that accompanies the knowledge. This removes the basis of the doubt.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying God knows 'by Himself' means His essence directly knows as an instrument, or that 'by His knowledge' means knowledge is a separate tool.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that these phrases indicate the absence of a separate cause accompanying the attribute, not instrumentalism.

How does Baqillani refute the opponents' criterion for similarity?
A) By showing it leads to the conclusion that two wills to the same object must be similar, which is false.
B) By claiming God's knowledge is beyond comparison.
C) By arguing that similarity is irrelevant.
D) By quoting scripture.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' criterion for similarity leads to absurd consequences, and theological terms like 'by Himself' and 'by His knowledge' require careful understanding to avoid misunderstanding.

Refuting Similarity of Divine and Human Knowledge
Baqillani argues that if divine and human knowledge were alike in relation, absurdities follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani argues that divine knowledge is fundamentally different from human knowledge by showing that assuming their similarity leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
If divine and human knowledge were alike in relation, then two wills would be identical—an impossibility.

Imam Baqillani begins by asserting the clear distinction between God's knowledge and created knowledge. He then constructs a double reductio ad absurdum: first, if the two knowledges were similar in their relation to objects, then two wills relating to the same thing would have to be identical—which is false. Second, he shows that if God were knowing by His essence (as some claim), His essence would have to be like the essences of our knowledges, which is impossible. This logical refutation establishes the uniqueness of divine knowledge without anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...but since this is invalid...'
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two separate arguments. The first concerns similarity of relation between divine and human knowledge. The second concerns the claim that God knows by His essence.
Look for the phrases 'يقال لهم' which mark each new argument.
3
Trace the logical flow of the first argument
Baqillani assumes that if the aspect of knowledge were similar in relation, then two wills relating to the same thing would be identical. Since that conclusion is false, the premise must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Trace the logical flow of the second argument
Baqillani assumes that if God were knowing by His essence, then His essence would be like the essences of our knowledges. Since that is impossible, God cannot be knowing by His essence in that sense.
This argument targets those who claim God's knowledge is identical to His essence in a way that implies similarity to human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأحدهما إلا وهي واجبة للآخر وليس كذلك سبيل علم القديم وعلم المحدث . ثم يقال لهم : لو كان جهة العلم بتماثل ما له تَعَلُّق بغير أن يكون مُتَعَلَّقُهما واحداً على وجه واحد لوجب أن تكون الإرادة المتعلقتان بالشيء الواحد المقدور والمراد على وجه الحدوث متماثلين لتعلقهما بمتعَلَّقٍ واحداً على وجه واحد فلما بطل هذا من قولنا وقولكم بطل اعتباركم الذي إليه استندتم . ثم يقال لهم : فيجب على اعتلالكم هذا إذا كان القديم سبحانه عالماً لنفسه وبنفسه أن تكون نفسه كنفس علومنا لأنها متعلقة بالمعلومات كتعلق علومنا بها فلما لم يجز ذلك لم يجز أن يكون عالماً بنفسه .
One of the two is only necessarily for the other, and the way of ancient knowledge and contingent knowledge is not like that. Then it is said to them: If the aspect of knowledge were similar in what it relates to, without their relation being one and in one mode, then it would be necessary that the two wills relating to the one thing that is willed and willed in the mode of temporality would be similar because they relate to one relation in one mode. But since this is invalid in our statement and your statement, your consideration upon which you relied is invalid. Then it is said to them: According to your reasoning, if the Ancient, exalted is He, is knowing by Himself and by His essence, then His essence must be like the essence of our knowledges because it relates to known things as our knowledges relate to them. But since that is invalid, it is invalid that He be knowing by His essence.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a proof that refutes the claim that divine knowledge and human knowledge are similar in their relation to objects. Baqillani begins by asserting that ancient knowledge (God's knowledge) and contingent knowledge (human knowledge) are not alike. Then he argues: if the aspect of knowledge were similar in its relation to objects, such that the relation of each to its object is identical in mode, then two wills relating to the same thing in the mode of temporality would be identical. But this is false by consensus, so the premise is false. He then extends the argument: if God is knowing by His essence (as opponents claim), then His essence would be like the essences of our knowledges, which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot be knowing by His essence in the sense that His essence is identical to knowledge.

Key Terms

تعلق
متماثل
وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that divine and human knowledge are alike in their relation to objects, implying they are essentially the same.
Correction: Baqillani shows that such similarity leads to absurdities, proving that while both are knowledge, their modes of relation and essences are fundamentally different.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue would follow if the relation of divine knowledge to its objects were identical to that of human knowledge?
A) God's knowledge would be created.
B) The two knowledges would be identical.
C) God would not know anything.
D) Human knowledge would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that divine and human knowledge are similar in their relation, thereby affirming the transcendence of God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge of your actions, do you secretly imagine Him processing information like a human mind, just faster or more perfectly? What does this lesson force you to admit about that picture?
?
Are you tempted to measure God's knowledge by your own, finding comfort only when you can relate to it? How can you train your heart to trust a knowledge that is utterly unlike yours?
?
This argument says two wills cannot be identical if they relate to the same thing in the same mode. Have you ever assumed that your will and God's will must 'agree' on a matter for your prayer to be answered? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?

Clarifying 'By His Essence' and 'By His Knowledge'
Baqillani clarifies the intended meaning of 'knowing by His essence' vs 'knowing by His knowledge'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent disagreement over whether God knows 'by His essence' or 'by His knowledge' is terminological only, as both formulations ultimately deny any accompanying cause (ma'na).

Core Idea
Both sides mean God knows without a cause—the dispute is only verbal.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses an objection from opponents who claim that saying 'God knows by His essence' is different from saying 'He knows by His knowledge'. He shows that both expressions reduce to the same meaning: that God knows without any additional attribute or cause that accompanies either His essence or His knowledge. Understanding this clarifies a key principle in the Sunni doctrine of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the parallel structure of the objection and the response.
Pay attention to the terms بنفسه (by His essence) and بنفس علمه (by His knowledge).
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents say that when they say 'He knows by His essence', they do not mean His essence is an instrument. They only mean He knows without a ma'na (meaning) that accompanies His essence. Identify this in the first part.
The phrase 'لا لمعنى يقارن نفسه' is key.
3
Understand Baqillani's response
Baqillani agrees and explains that likewise, when he says 'He knows by His knowledge', he does not mean knowledge is an instrument like a rope. He means He knows without a ma'na accompanying knowledge. The two statements are parallel.
Note the analogy: 'like the relation of a rope to a rope' is an example of what they do not mean.
4
Generalize the principle
Baqillani generalizes: whenever we say something is described by something 'for Himself', we mean it is described without a cause (علة). This resolves the apparent disagreement.
The term علة (cause) is broader than ma'na but related.
5
Internalize the key takeaway
Reflect on how this passage shows that careful terminology is needed in theology, and that many disputes are merely verbal. The underlying doctrine is that God's attributes are not caused by anything distinct from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : نحن لا نقول : إنه عالم بالمعلومات بنفسه على أنه بنفسه يَعْلَمُها وأن المعلومات مُتعَلَّقَةٌ بها وإنما نريد بذلك أنه عالم بها لا لمعنى يقارن نفسه فعبرنا عن هذا المعنى بأنه عالم بنفسه قيل لهم : وكذلك نحن لسنا نريد بقولنا إن القديم تعالى يعلم المعلومات بنفس علمه أن علمه آلة له ومتعلق بالمعلومات تعلق الحبل بالحبل والجسم تعلق بالجسم وإنما نعني بقولنا إنه يعلم المعلومات بنفس علمه أنه يعلمها لا لمعنى يقارن العلم فعبرنا عن ذلك بأنه يعلم بنفس العلم وكذلك كل شيء قلنا فيه إنه موصوف بما وُصِفَ به لنفسه إنما نعني به أنه موصوف به لا لعلة فلم يجب ما قلتم .
For if they say: We do not say that He is knower of known things by His essence in the sense that by His essence He knows them and that known things relate to it; rather we mean by that that He is knower of them without a meaning that accompanies His essence, so we expressed this meaning by saying He is knower by His essence. It is said to them: Likewise we do not mean by our statement that the Ancient, exalted, knows known things by His knowledge that His knowledge is an instrument for Him and a relation to known things like the relation of a rope to a rope and a body to a body; rather we mean by our statement that He knows known things by His knowledge that He knows them without a meaning that accompanies knowledge, so we expressed that by saying He knows by knowledge. And similarly, everything we said about it that He is described by what He is described by for Himself, we only mean thereby that He is described by it without a cause, so what you said does not apply to us.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer that clarifies the terminology used by both sides. The opponents say: When we say 'God knows by His essence', we do not mean that His essence is a knowing substance like our knowledge; we mean that He knows without any added attribute (ma'na) that accompanies His essence. The answer: Similarly, when we say 'God knows by His knowledge', we do not mean that knowledge is an instrument like a rope or a body; we mean that He knows without any added attribute that accompanies the knowledge. So both sides agree on the underlying meaning: that God knows without a distinct cause (la li-illa). The opponents' objection is therefore not a real difference.

Key Terms
بنفسه
بمعنى
آلة
علة
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'knowing by His essence' and 'knowing by His knowledge' refer to different doctrines—one implying no distinct attribute, the other implying an attribute as an instrument.
Correction: Baqillani shows both phrases mean the same: God knows without any accompanying cause (ma'na). Neither phrase implies an instrument or a distinct cause.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's response, what is the common meaning between 'knowing by His essence' and 'knowing by His knowledge'?
A) Both affirm an instrument.
B) Both deny an instrument and affirm a cause.
C) Both deny an accompanying cause (ma'na).
D) Both affirm that knowledge is an attribute distinct from essence.

Key Takeaway
Both 'knowing by His essence' and 'knowing by His knowledge' ultimately deny any accompanying cause (ma'na) or instrument, making the apparent dispute merely terminological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you speak of God's attributes, do you ever hide behind theological formulas without truly understanding what they mean? How can you ensure that your words reflect genuine belief rather than empty phrases?
?
How often do you engage in debates where you insist on your own wording without realizing the other person might mean the same thing? What does this lesson teach you about humility in theological discussion?
?
If the dispute between 'by His essence' and 'by His knowledge' is merely verbal, what does that say about the danger of arguing over terminology? Have you ever caused division by insisting on a particular phrase? What will you do differently?

Reductio: The Absurdity of 'Without Cause'
Baqillani refutes the claim that 'by His essence' means without any cause via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a reductio argument to refute the claim that 'by His essence' means without any cause.

Core Idea
Defining 'by itself' as 'without cause' leads to absurd consequences.

This passage presents a powerful logical argument from Imam Baqillani. He shows that if the phrase 'by His essence' is interpreted to mean 'without any cause,' then everything would have to be self-caused, which is absurd. Let's see how this reductio works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's position
Baqillani targets those who say 'the Creator is knowing by His essence' means He is knowing without any cause (illa). Recognize that this is a specific interpretation of divine attributes.
2
Follow the reductio logic
Baqillani extracts the logical consequence: if being 'by itself' means 'without cause', then by the same logic, a contingent thing would be contingent by itself, and a thing would be a thing by itself. Identify the absurdity.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
See the self-defeating conclusion
Notice that this forces the opponent to abandon their own doctrine of attributes that are due neither to the essence nor to a cause. The argument exposes an internal contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إن كان معنى أن الباري عالم بنفسه أنه عالم لا لمعنى يقارن نفسه فيجب أن يكون المُحْدَثُ مُحدّثاً لنفسه والشيء شيئاً لنفسه لأنه مُحْدَثٌ لا لعلة وشيءٌ لا لعلة وكذلك يجب أن تجعلوا كل وصف يُستَحَقُّ لا لعلة مُستَحَقاً لنفس الموصوف به وهذا ترك قولهم بأوصاف تُستَحَقُّ لا للنفس ولا لعلة
Then it is said to them: If the meaning of the Creator being knowing by His essence is that He is knowing without a meaning that accompanies His essence, then it must be that the contingent is contingent by itself and the thing is a thing by itself, because it is contingent without a cause and a thing without a cause. And likewise, you must make every attribute that is due without a cause as due to the essence of the described itself; and this abandons their statement about attributes that are due neither to the essence nor to a cause.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a reductio argument. Baqillani argues that if 'by His essence' means 'without any cause (illa)', then by the same logic, a contingent thing would be contingent by itself (since it is contingent without a cause), and a thing would be a thing by itself. This would force the opponents to hold that all attributes that are due without a cause are due to the essence itself, which contradicts their own doctrine that some attributes are due neither to the essence nor to a cause. Thus their interpretation is self-defeating.

Key Terms
علة
استحقاق
نفس
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Baqillani is arguing that every attribute must have a cause.
Correction: He is not arguing that. He is showing that the opponents' interpretation leads to logical absurdity, not that all attributes are caused.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say would follow if 'by His essence' meant 'without any cause'?
A) God would not be knowing.
B) Contingent things would be contingent by themselves.
C) Attributes would be caused.
D) The essence would be knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's reductio shows that interpreting 'by His essence' as 'without a cause' leads to absurdity and contradicts the opponents' own position.

This lesson warns against interpreting 'by His essence' in a way that leads to absurdity. In what areas of your faith are you tempted to rationalize beyond what revelation allows? Commit to examining one of your understandings of Allah's attributes today and seek clarity from reliable sources.

Will you ask Allah to purify your heart and mind from any inconsistency, no matter how uncomfortable?

Refuting the Objection on the Mode of Relation
Baqillani begins to refute the objection about the mode of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the claim that the relation of God's essence to objects differs from the relation of knowledge.

Core Idea
The factor that makes one a knower is identical whether it is essence or knowledge.

The opponents argue that God's knowing by His essence is not like our knowing by knowledge because the relation is different. Baqillani counters that the property of being a knower is a single, invariable aspect, so whatever causes it must be similar. This passage sets up the confusion of the opponents before Baqillani's full argument, though the text cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the flow of objection and response. Then read the translation.
Notice the structure: 'For if they say... It is said...'
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents claim that the relation of God's essence to objects is like that of a knower, while the relation of knowledge is like that of sciences. Understand why they think this creates a difference.
3
Analyze Baqillani's response
Baqillani calls the objection 'confusion and lack of religion'. He argues that being a knower is a single property, so the cause must be similar whether it is essence or knowledge.
Focus on the phrase 'حد متساو' (a single limit).
4
Recognize the incomplete argument
The passage ends abruptly. Note that Baqillani's full refutation continues beyond this page. Reflect on how the argument might conclude.
The OCR note indicates the text is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لا يجب إذا علم الباري سبحانه المعلومات بنفسه أن تكون نفسه كنفس علومنا لأن تَعَلَّق نفسه بالمعلومات تَعَلُّق العالمين وتَعَلَّقَ العلم بها تعلُّقُ العلوم قيل هذه حيرة وقلة دين وإيثار للتخليط وذلك أن كون العالم عالماً بالمعلومات بعلمه هو عندنا وعندكم بمعنى كونه عالماً بالمعلومات بنفسه لو ثبت أنه عالم بنفسه وكونه عالماً بعلمه لا يختلف ولا يتزايد فيجب أن يكون ما أوجب كونه عالماً بالمعلومات متماثلاً إن كانت نفسه وإن كانت علة لا يقال هي علم أو حال متساوياً متماثلاً لأن المعتبر في ذلك بكون العالم عالماً حد متساو و وجب تماثل ما أوجب هذه الصفة المتساوية فقولكم بعد هذا إن نفس الباري سبحانه تَتَعَلَّقُ بالمعلوم تعلق العالمين ونفس العلم تتعلق تعلق العلوم تخليط وإبهام أن كون العالم عالماً
For if they say: It is not necessary, when the Creator, exalted, knows the known things by His essence, that His essence be like the essences of our knowledges, because the relation of His essence to known things is the relation of knowers, and the relation of knowledge to them is the relation of sciences. It is said: This is confusion and lack of religion and preference for mixing. That is because the fact that the knower is knower of known things by His knowledge is, for us and for you, in the same sense as His being knower of known things by His essence, if it is established that He is knower by His essence. And His being knower by His knowledge does not differ or increase. So it must be that what makes Him knower of known things is similar, whether it is His essence or whether it is a cause that is not called knowledge or state, equal and similar. Because the consideration in that, concerning the knower being knower, is a single limit, and it is necessary that what makes this equal attribute be similar. So your statement after this that the essence of the Creator, exalted, relates to the known thing as the relation of knowers, and that the essence of knowledge relates as the relation of sciences, is confusion and obscurity concerning the fact that the knower is knower...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents object: It is not necessary that God's essence be like our knowledge because the relation of His essence to objects is like the relation of a knower (who knows by essence) while the relation of knowledge to objects is like the relation of sciences (which are distinct). The answer: This is confusion. The fact that a knower knows by his knowledge is, for both parties, the same as his knowing by his essence, since God's knowing by His essence is accepted. Being a knower is a single, invariable aspect; therefore, whatever makes one a knower must be identical, whether it is the essence or a distinct attribute, because the criterion is that it makes one a knower. Thus, if the opponents claim different relations, they are mixing things up.

Key Terms

عالم
علم
حد
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mode of relation between God's essence and objects differs from the mode of relation between knowledge and objects, so God's essence is not comparable to human knowledge.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the property of being a knower is single and invariable, so whatever causes that property—whether essence or knowledge—must be similar; the mode of relation is the same.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say the opponents' objection about different relations leads to?
A) A correct distinction.
B) Confusion and lack of religion.
C) An affirmation of divine simplicity.
D) A proof of createdness of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani establishes that the property of being a knower is single and invariable, so the cause of that property—whether essence or knowledge—must be similar, refuting the objection of different relations.

In your own pursuit of knowledge about Allah, are you content with a fragmented understanding, or do you strive for a unified understanding of His attributes? What specific step will you take this week to resolve a contradiction you have accepted?
?
Baqillani argues that the cause must be similar because the effect is the same. Consider an area in your life where you have different reasons for the same action (e.g., praying for show vs. for Allah). How does this principle of 'same effect, same cause' challenge you to purify your intentions? What single cause will you anchor your actions on?

Refuting God's Knowledge by Essence Alone
Baqillani refutes the claim that God's knowledge is by essence without cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument against the claim that God knows by His essence without an attribute, and why such a position leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If 'knowing by essence' means no cause, then everything would be self-caused.

Baqillani tackles a subtle objection: some claim God's knowledge is by His essence alone, without a real attribute. He shows that this notion collapses under logical scrutiny. The passage unfolds as a crisp dialectical exchange, where Baqillani first clarifies terms, then uses a reductio argument, and finally dismantles the opponent's defense. Prepare to follow a tight chain of reasoning that defends the necessity of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial clarification
Notice how Baqillani begins by defining what 'described for Himself' means—it means without a cause, not that the attribute is the essence.
This sets the stage for the reductio.
2
Follow the reductio argument
If 'knowing by essence' equals 'knowing without a cause', then by analogy, a contingent thing would be contingent by itself—an absurd outcome.
The opponent cannot accept this consequence.
3
Examine the opponent's objection
The opponent tries to distinguish God's knowledge attachment from human knowledge attachment.
They claim the mode of attachment differs.
4
Analyze Baqillani's response
Baqillani argues that the state of being a knower is a single state; whatever necessitates it must be similar, whether essence or cause.
This refutes the opponent's attempt to differentiate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا فيه إنه موصوف بما وُصِفَ به لنفسه إنما نعني به أنه موصوف به لا لعلة فلم يجب ما قلتم. ثم يقال لهم: إن كان معنى أن الباري عالم بنفسه أنه عالم لا لمعنى يقارن نفسه فيجب أن يكون المُحْدَثُ مُحدّثاً لنفسه والشيء شيئاً لنفسه لأنه مُحْدَثٌ لا لعلة وشيءٌ لا لعلة وكذلك يجب أن تجعلوا كل وصف يُستَحَقُّ لا لعلة مُستَحَقاً لنفس الموصوف به وهذا ترك قولهم بأوصاف تُستَحَقُّ لا للنفس ولا لعلة. فإن قالوا: لا يجب إذا علم الباري سبحانه المعلومات بنفسه أن تكون نفسه كنفس علومنا لأن تَعَلَّق نفسه بالمعلومات تَعَلُّق العالمين وتَعَلَّقَ العلم بها تعلُّقُ العلوم. قيل: هذه حيرة وقلة دين وإيثار للتخليط. وذلك أن كون العالم عالماً بالمعلومات بعلمه هو عندنا وعندكم بمعنى كونه عالماً بالمعلومات بنفسه لو ثبت أنه عالم بنفسه وكونه عالماً بعلمه لا يختلف ولا يتزايد فيجب أن يكون ما أوجب كونه عالماً بالمعلومات متماثلاً إن كانت نفسه وإن كانت علة لا يقال هي علم أو حال متساوياً متماثلاً لأن المعتبر في ذلك بكون العالم عالماً حَدٍ مُتَساوٍ ووجب تماثل ما أوجب هذه الصفة المتساوية. فقولكم بعد هذا إن نفس الباري سبحانه تَتَعَلَّق بالمعلوم تعلق العالمين ونفس العلم تتعلق تعلق العلوم تخليط وإبهام. أن كون العالم عالماً بالمعلوم تارة بنفسه وتارة لمعنى يختلف فإذا لم يجز قلتموه محصول ولا معنى معقول ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك.
We said about it: He is described with what He described for Himself — we only mean by it that He is described with it without a cause, so what you said does not answer. Then it is said to them: If the meaning of the Eternal being knowing by Himself is that He is knowing without a meaning that accompanies His essence, then the contingent must be contingent by itself, and a thing thing by itself, because it is contingent without a cause and a thing without a cause. Likewise, you must make every attribute that is deserved without a cause deserved by the very essence described by it, and this abandons their statement about attributes that are deserved neither for the essence nor for a cause. If they say: It is not necessary, when the Exalted Creator knows known things by Himself, that His essence be like the essences of our knowledges, because the attachment of His essence to known things is the attachment of knowers, and the attachment of knowledge to them is the attachment of knowledges. It is said: This is confusion, lack of religion, and preference for disorder. That is because the state of the knower knowing known things by his knowledge — according to us and according to you — is in the sense of his being knowing of known things by Himself, if it is established that He is knowing by Himself, and his being knowing by his knowledge does not differ or increase. So what necessitates his being knowing of known things must be similar, whether it is His essence or whether it is a cause — it is not said that it is knowledge or a state — equal and similar, because what is considered in that is the state of the knower being knowing: a single limit. So similarity is necessitated of what necessitates this equal attribute. So your statement after this that the essence of the Exalted Creator attaches to the known thing with the attachment of knowers, and the essence of knowledge attaches with the attachment of knowledges, is disorder and vagueness. That the state of the knower being knowing of the known thing sometimes by Himself and sometimes by a meaning differs; and if it is not permissible, then what you have said is unintelligible and not rationally meaningful, and they have no answer for that.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's position on God's knowledge. Baqillani first clarifies that when we say God is described with what He describes for Himself, we mean it is without a cause. Then he argues: if being knowing by essence means knowing without a concurrent cause, then logically a contingent thing would be contingent by itself, and a thing would be a thing by itself — which is absurd. The opponent objects that God's knowledge is unlike human knowledge, so the analogy fails. Baqillani responds that this objection is confusion. He explains that being a knower (whether by essence or by an attribute) is a single state; whatever necessitates that state must be similar. So whether it is essence or a cause, the necessitating factor is the same. The opponent's attempt to distinguish the attachment of essence vs. knowledge is meaningless. The conclusion: the opponent's claim is incoherent and they have no valid answer.

لا لعلة

تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God knows by essence, then there is no need for an attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Baqillani shows that this leads to absurdities, proving that the attribute of knowledge is a real distinct entity.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue would follow if God were knowing by His essence without a cause?
A) The contingent would be contingent by itself.
B) His knowledge would be created.
C) He would have multiple knowledges.
D) He would be similar to humans.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the claim that God knows by essence without an attribute leads to logical absurdities, thus affirming the necessity of a real attribute of knowledge.

What will you do today to live in light of that reality?
?
The opponent in this passage tried to save their view by making a vague distinction. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah where you rely on comfortable but unclear ideas rather than pressing for clarity? What is one belief about Allah you have been avoiding examining deeply, and what will you do this week to seek clarity?

Baqillani calls the opponent's objection 'confusion and lack of religion.' When you encounter objections to Islamic teachings, do you react with emotional defensiveness, or do you strive for a rational, grounded response? Which of your own beliefs about Allah might actually be weaker than you think because you have not thought through their implications? Commit to investigating one such belief today.
