Refuting the Claim that God's Knowledge Must Be Like or Unlike Him
Baqillani refutes the objection that God's knowledge must be like or unlike Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that God's knowledge must be either like or unlike Him by demonstrating that attributes are not independent entities subject to such categories.

Core Idea
Attributes are not separate entities; the categories of 'like' and 'unlike' do not apply.

The opponents argue that if God had knowledge, it would have to be either like or unlike Him, leading to absurdities. Baqillani responds by questioning the necessity of this dichotomy, using analogies to show that attributes are not independent entities that can be compared in such a way. This lesson unpacks that argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opponent's argument carefully: if God had knowledge, it must be either like or unlike Him; both lead to unacceptable conclusions (another god or another eternal entity).
This is known as a 'dilemma' argument.
2
Baqillani's counter-question
Notice how Baqillani immediately questions the premise: 'Why do you say it must be agreeing or differing?' He challenges the assumption that the categories apply.
3
Examine the analogies
Baqillani gives examples: a verse and its surah, a line and its poem, a part and its whole, one of ten. In each case, the part is not 'like' or 'unlike' the whole because they are not distinct, independent entities.
These analogies illustrate the logical flaw.
4
Draw the conclusion
Realize that God's knowledge is an attribute, not a separate entity. Therefore, the categories of 'like' and 'unlike' do not apply, and the objection collapses.
This is a key insight in Islamic theology about the nature of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن شبهة أخرى قالوا: الدليل على أنه لا علم لله سبحانه أنه لو كان له علم لم يَخلُ من أن يكون مِثْلاً للقديم تعالى أو مخالفاً له فإن كان مماثلاً له وجب أن يكون رباً إلهاً عالماً قادراً كهو وهذا كفر من قائله وإن كان مخالفاً له وجب أن يكون غيراً له وأن يكون معه في القدم غيراً له وذلك باطل باتفاق فوجب أنه لا علم له. يقال لهم: لم قلتم إنه لا بد أن يكون علمه إذا ثبت موافقاً له أو مخالفاً؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكون محالاً أن يقال فيما ليس بغيرَين إنهما متفقان أو مختلفان كما يستحيل أن يقال إن الباري جلّ اسمه مِثْل للأشياء كلها أو مخالف لها كلها وكما يستحيل أن يقال ذلك في الآية من السورة والبيت من القصيدة والجزء من الجملة والواحد من العشرة من حيث استحال أن يكون أحد المذكورين هو الآخر أو غيره؟ فما الذي به تدفعون هذا؟
Then another specious argument: They said: The evidence that God the Exalted has no knowledge is that if He had knowledge, it would not be free from being either a likeness of the Eternal Most High or different from Him. If it is like Him, then it must be a lord, a god, knowing, powerful like Him — and this is disbelief from its speaker. And if it is different from Him, then it must be other than Him and that it exists with Him in pre-eternity as other than Him — and that is false by consensus. So it must be that He has no knowledge. It is said to them: Why do you say that His knowledge, if established, must necessarily be agreeing with Him or differing from Him? And why do you deny that it is impossible to say of two things that are not two distinct entities that they are agreeing or differing? Just as it is impossible to say that the Exalted, may His name be glorified, is a likeness of all things or different from all of them, and just as it is impossible to say that in the case of a verse of a surah, a line of a poem, a part of a whole, and one of ten — because it is impossible that one of the mentioned things is the other or other than it? So with what do you repel this?
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The opponent argues: If God had knowledge, that knowledge must be either like the Eternal (i.e., God) or different from Him. If like, then it would be another god; if different, then there would be another eternal entity — both impossible. Therefore, God has no knowledge. Baqillani refutes this by pointing out that the categories of 'agreement' and 'difference' apply only to two distinct, independent entities. But God's knowledge is not a distinct entity; it is an attribute. He draws analogies: we do not say a verse is agreeing with or differing from its surah, nor a line from its poem, nor a part from the whole — because they are not separate entities. Similarly, God's knowledge is not a separate thing to be compared as like or unlike. Thus the objection is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God had knowledge, that knowledge would have to be a separate entity that is either like or unlike God.
Correction: Baqillani shows that attributes are not separate entities; they are inherent to the subject. Therefore, the categories of 'like' and 'unlike' do not apply to them.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw Baqillani identifies in the opponents' argument about God's knowledge?
A) They assume God has no knowledge.
B) They treat an attribute as a separate entity that can be like or unlike God.
C) They deny God's power.
D) They claim knowledge is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by showing that the categories of 'like' and 'unlike' apply only to independent entities, not to attributes inherent in a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The opponents assumed knowledge must be a separate entity. In your own understanding, do you ever treat God's attributes as if they are independent beings? What step will you take today to understand that they are inherent to Him?
?
Baqillani uses simple analogies to expose a flawed premise. Think of a doubt you have had about a divine attribute. Are you willing to question its underlying assumptions like he did? Commit to studying refutations of such doubts.

Refuting the Likeness-Otherness Objection to Divine Knowledge
Baqillani answers the objection that God cannot have knowledge due to likeness or otherness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the objection that God cannot have knowledge because it would either be like or unlike Him, and why the relationship between God and His attributes is unique.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are neither like nor unlike God in the way creatures are.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a sophisticated objection against divine knowledge. The opponents argue that if God had knowledge, it would either be like Him (making it a second god) or different from Him (making it a separate eternal entity). Baqillani dismantles this false dichotomy by showing that God and His attributes are not two independent things that can be compared in such a way. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection (شبهة) as presented by the opponents. Notice how they frame it as a dilemma: divine knowledge must be either like or unlike God, both impossible.
This is a common pattern in kalam: opponents pose a logical dilemma.
2
Identify the false premise
Baqillani's first move is to question the assumption that every pair of things must be either like or unlike. Find his examples (verse-sura, line-poem, part-whole, one-ten) that show this binary does not always apply.
These analogies are meant to show that God and His attributes are not two distinct things in the relevant sense.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'different'
Baqillani distinguishes two senses of 'different' (مخالف). The first (separate entity, separable) is false for divine knowledge; the second (different in attributes and status) is correct in meaning but the wording is prohibited.
This nuance is crucial: the opponents' objection equivocates between these senses.
4
Grasp the final point on meanings vs. words
Baqillani concludes that the debate is about meanings (معاني), not verbal expressions. Even without language, rational proofs lead to correct knowledge. Clinging to labels shows weakness.
This emphasizes the primacy of rational theology over terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن شبهة أخرى قالوا : الدليل على أنه لا علم الله سبحانه أنه لو كان له علم لم يَخلُ من أن يكون مِثْلاً للقديم تعالى أو مخالفاً له فإن كان مماثلاً له وجب أن يكون رباً إلهاً عالماً قادراً كهو وهذا كفر من قائله وإن كان مخالفاً له وجب أن يكون غيراً له وأن يكون معه في القدم غير له وذلك باطل باتفاق فوجب أنه لا علم له. يقال لهم : لم قلتم إنه لا بد أن يكون علمه إذا ثبت موافقاً له أو مخالفاً ؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكون مُحالاً أن يقال فيما ليس بغَيْرَين إنهما متفقان أو مختلفان كما يستحيل أن يقال إن الباري جلّ اسمه مِثْلُ للأشياء كلها أو مخالف لها كلها وكما يستحيل أن يقال ذلك في الآية من السورة والبيت من القصيدة والجزء من الجملة والواحد من العشرة من حيث استحال أن يكون أحد المذكورين هو الآخر أو غيره ؟ فما الذي به تدفعون هذا؟ ثم يقال لهم : إن أردتم بقولكم إن علم القديم سبحانه مخالف له أنه غَيْرُ له وأنه من جنس والباري سبحانه من جنس غير جنسه كما يقال ذلك في السواد والبياض فذلك محال لقيام الدليل على أن الله سبحانه ليس بغَيْرِ له من حيث لم تجز مفارقته له بزمان أو مكان أو الوجود أو العدم وقد ثبت أن معنى الغَيْرين وحقيقة وصفهما بذلك أنه ما جاز افتراقهما على أحد هذه الثلاثة الأوجه وكذلك فقد دلّ الدليل على أن القديم سبحانه وعلمه ليسا بجنسين ولا مُختلِفين ولا متفقين. وإن عنيتم بخلاف القديم سبحانه لعلمه بُعْدَ شَبَهه منه وأنه لا يسد مسده ولا ينوب منابه ولا يستحق من الوصف ما يستحق ولا يجوز عليه من الأوصاف ما جميع يجوز عليه فهذا صحيح في المعنى وإن كانت العبارة ممنوعاً منها لا تجوز باتفاق أو سمع أو دليل أوجب ذلك إن قام عليه وليس الكلام في الإطلاقات والعبارات، وإنما الكلام في المعاني . لأننا لـو لم نعرف لغة أصلاً ولم نتكلم بها، لصح علمنا بالقضايا العقلية عند التأمل والنظر في الأدلة والتعلق بذكر الخلاف وإلزام التسمية به عجز وتقصير عن الخوض فيما يجب أن يكون معلوماً بالأدلة .
Then another objection: they said: The evidence that God (سبحانه) has no knowledge is that if He had knowledge, it would not be free from being either like the Eternal (تعالى) or different from Him. If it is like Him, then it must be a lord, a god, knowing, powerful like Him, and this is disbelief from its speaker. And if it is different from Him, then it must be other than Him, and be with Him in pre-eternity as other than Him, and this is false by consensus, so it is necessary that He has no knowledge. It is said to them: Why did you say that His knowledge, if established, must necessarily be agreeing with Him or differing from Him? And why do you deny that it is impossible to say of what is not two things that they are agreeing or differing, just as it is impossible to say that the Creator (جلّ اسمه) is a likeness for all things or different from all things, and just as it is impossible to say that about the verse of the sura, the line of the poem, the part of the whole, and the one of the ten, insofar as it is impossible for one of the mentioned to be the other or other than it? So with what do you repel this? Then it is said to them: If you mean by your statement that the knowledge of the Eternal (سبحانه) is different from Him that it is other than Him, and that it is of a genus and the Creator (سبحانه) is of a different genus, as is said regarding blackness and whiteness, then that is impossible because the proof has been established that God (سبحانه) is not other than it in the sense that its separation from Him by time or place or existence or non-existence is not permissible. And it has been established that the meaning of 'two others' and the reality of describing them as such is that it is permissible for them to separate on one of these three aspects. Likewise, the proof has indicated that the Eternal (سبحانه) and His knowledge are not two genera, nor differing, nor agreeing. And if you mean by the difference of the Eternal (سبحانه) from His knowledge the remoteness of its likeness to Him, and that it does not take His place, nor substitute for Him, nor deserve the description that He deserves, nor are the attributes that are permissible for Him all permissible for it, then this is correct in meaning, even if the expression is prohibited, not permissible by consensus or hearing or proof that necessitates that, if it is established. And the discussion is not about unrestricted expressions and phrases; rather the discussion is about meanings. For if we knew no language at all and did not speak with it, our knowledge of rational propositions upon reflection and consideration of the proofs would be valid. And clinging to the mention of disagreement and obligating naming is impotence and deficiency in delving into what should be known by proofs.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument about God's attribute of knowledge. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, presents a shubha (objection) raised by opponents who deny that God has knowledge, then refutes it in detail. The objection runs: If God had knowledge, that knowledge would either be similar to God (making it a second deity) or different from Him (implying a second eternal entity). Both are impossible, so God cannot have knowledge. The answer begins by questioning the assumption that knowledge must be either like or unlike God. Baqillani points out that not every pair of things can be described as agreeing or differing; there are cases where such descriptions are inapplicable, such as a verse and its sura, a line and a poem, a part and a whole, or one and ten. In these cases, it is impossible to say that one is the other or other than it. Similarly, God and His attributes are not two independent entities that could be called 'like' or 'different' in the usual sense. The answer then distinguishes two possible meanings of 'different'. If the opponents mean that knowledge is a separate entity (ghayr) in the sense of being separable in time, place, or existence, that is false because God's knowledge is inseparable from Him. The definition of 'two things' (ghayrayn) is that they can be separated in one of these three ways; since God and His knowledge cannot be separated, they are not two things in that sense. If instead they mean that knowledge is unlike God in attributes and does not share His status, then this is correct in meaning, but the wording 'different' is inappropriate and forbidden by consensus or evidence. Baqillani concludes that the real issue is the meanings (ma'ani), not the verbal expressions. Even if one had no language at all, rational reflection on proofs would lead to correct knowledge. The focus on terminology and labeling is a weakness that hinders understanding what must be known through proofs.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine attributes, like knowledge, must be either exactly like God (making them deities) or completely different (making them separate co-eternal entities).
Correction: Baqillani shows that this binary does not apply; God and His attributes are not two independent things that can be compared in terms of likeness or otherness. They are inseparable, and the discussion is about meanings, not crude categories.

B) The objection relies on a different definition of 'knowledge' that is not used in Islamic theology.
C) The objection denies the existence of any attributes for God.
D) The objection is based on a corrupted text of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes the objection by demonstrating that God and His attributes are not two things subject to the categories of 'like' or 'unlike' in the ordinary sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objection assumes that anything related to God must be either exactly like Him or completely different. In your own relationship with Allah, where might you be falling into a similar trap—either making God too remote or too familiar? What would change if you held the balance Baqillani teaches?

Refuting Difference of God's Knowledge and Clarifying Its Meaning
Baqillani refutes the claim of difference and clarifies the meaning of God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the claim that God's knowledge is different from Him in a way that implies separability, and you will learn the correct meaning of attributing knowledge to God as an essential attribute.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is neither separate nor identical in kind; it is an essential attribute of His self.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses two critical issues: first, he dismantles the misconception that God's knowledge is 'different' from Him in a way that would imply two separate entities. He then explains that saying 'God has knowledge' does not mean it is His property, action, or an accident, but rather that He is knowing by an attribute inherent to His essence. This lesson will walk you through his logical arguments and clarify the kalam understanding of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize that the claim 'God's knowledge is different from Him' can have two distinct meanings. Baqillani first examines what the opponent might intend.
This sets up the refutation.
2
Analyze the refutation
Baqillani shows that if 'different' means a separate genus (like black and white), it is impossible because God and His knowledge cannot be separated in time, place, or existence. If it means 'not a likeness', the meaning is correct but the expression is not permitted.
Focus on the three modes of separation.
3
Grasp the positive explanation
When asked what it means that God has knowledge, Baqillani answers that it means He is knowing by a knowledge that belongs to His essence—not as property, act, or accident. He gives an analogy of will and motion.
This clarifies the nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: إن أردتم بقولكم إن علم القديم سبحانه مخالف له أنه غير له وأنه من جنس والباري سبحانه من جنس غير جنسه كما يقال ذلك في السواد والبياض فذلك محال لقيام الدليل على أن سبحانه ليس بغير له من حيث لم تجز مفارقته له بزمان أو مكان أو الوجود أو العدم وقد ثبت أن معنى الغيرين وحقيقة وصفهما بذلك أنه ما جاز افتراقهما على أحد هذه الثلاثة الأوجه وكذلك فقد دل الدليل على أن القديم سبحانه وعلمه ليسا بجنسين ولا مختلفين ولا متفقين وإن عنيتم بخلاف القديم سبحانه لعلمه بعد شبهه منه وأنه لا يسد مسده ولا ينوب منابه ولا يستحق من الوصف ما يستحق ولا يجوز عليه من الأوصاف ما يجوز عليه فهذا صحيح في المعنى وإن كانت العبارة ممنوعا منها لا تجوز باتفاق أو سمع أو دليل أوجب ذلك إن قام عليه وليس الكلام في الإطلاقات والعبارات، وإنما الكلام في المعاني. لأننا لو لم نعرف لغة أصلا ولم نتكلم بها، لصح علمنا بالقضايا العقلية عند التأمل والنظر في الأدلة والتعلق بذكر الخلاف وإلزام التسمية به عجز وتقصير عن الخوض فيما يجب أن يكون معلوما بالأدلة. فإن قالوا فما معنى قولكم إن لله تعالى علما أتعنون بذلك أنه ملكه أو فعله أو حال فيه أو أنه عالم بنفس العلم الذي أضيف إليه؟ قيل لهم: معنى ذلك أنه علم بالنفس التي علم له فقط. وليس ذلك من معنى أنها ملكه أو فعله أو حالة فيه بسبيل. وهذا كما نقول نحن وأنتم إن الإرادة إرادة له، وإن الحركة حركة للمتحرك، لا بمعنى أنها ملكه أو فعله أو حالة فيه، لأنه قد يحصل فيه ما ليس بحركة ولا إرادة له. وكذلك فقد يسريد [؟] ويتحرك بما ليس بملك له ولا فعل له ويحل فيه ما ليس بحركة له. وبالله التوفيق.
Then it is said to them: If you mean by your statement that the knowledge of the Eternal, exalted is He, is different from Him, that it is other than Him and that it is of a genus, and the Creator, exalted is He, is of a genus other than its genus, as that is said regarding blackness and whiteness, then that is impossible because the proof indicates that the Exalted is not other than Him insofar as He cannot separate from Him in time, place, existence, or nonexistence. And it has been established that the meaning of 'the two others' and the reality of describing them as such is that it is possible for them to be separated in one of these three ways. Likewise, the proof has indicated that the Eternal, exalted is He, and His knowledge are not two genera, nor different, nor similar. But if you mean by the difference of the Eternal, exalted is He, from His knowledge the remoteness of His likeness to it, and that it does not take its place nor substitute for it, nor does it deserve the description that He deserves, nor are the descriptions that are permissible for Him permissible for it, then this is correct in meaning, even if the expression is prohibited and not permissible by consensus, by transmission, or by proof that necessitates that if it is established. And the discussion is not about linguistic usages and expressions; rather, the discussion is about meanings. For if we did not know a language at all and did not speak with it, our knowledge of rational propositions through reflection and examination of proofs would be correct. And occupying oneself with mentioning disagreement and insisting on naming it is inability and deficiency in delving into what should be known through proofs. Then if they say: What do you mean by your statement that God, exalted is He, has knowledge? Do you mean thereby that it is His property, or His act, or an accident in Him, or that He is knowing by the knowledge itself that is attributed to Him? It is said to them: The meaning of that is that it is knowledge by the self that has knowledge only. And that is not at all from the meaning that it is His property, His act, or an accident in Him. And this is just as we and you say that will is will of Him, and that motion is motion of the mover, not in the sense that it is its property, its act, or an accident in it, because there may occur in it what is not motion nor will of it. Likewise, it may be [؟] and move by what is not its property nor its act, and there may occur in it what is not its motion. And by God is success.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and clarification regarding the nature of God's knowledge (علم). It consists of two parts: first, addressing the claim that God's knowledge is 'different' (مخالف) from Him, and second, explaining what it means to say 'God has knowledge' (لله تعالى علم). 

In the first part, Baqillani considers the hypothetical objection: if someone claims that God's knowledge is 'different' from Him, he examines two possible meanings. If they mean that knowledge is another entity ('غير') of a different genus from God, analogous to how blackness and whiteness are different, then this is impossible (محال). The proof (دليل) shows that God cannot be separated from His attribute in time, place, existence, or nonexistence. The definition of two things being 'other' (غيرين) in this context is that they can separate in one of these three ways (time, place, existence/nonexistence). Since God and His knowledge are not separable, they are not two genera, nor different, nor similar in this sense. If, however, they mean that knowledge is not a likeness of God, that it does not take His place or deserve His descriptions, then this is correct in meaning (صحيح في المعنى), even if the specific expression is not permitted. Baqillani emphasizes that the real discussion is about meanings (معاني), not linguistic expressions (إطلاقات). He asserts that even without language, rational propositions are known through reflection on proofs. Therefore, insisting on naming or linguistic disagreement is a sign of weakness (عجز وتقصير).

In the second part, Baqillani answers an objection: when you say 'God has knowledge', do you mean it is His property (ملكه), His act (فعله), or an accident (حالّ) in Him? He replies that it means He is knowing by a knowledge that belongs to His self (علم بالنفس التي علم له). It is not property, act, or accident. He gives an analogy: we say 'will is will of Him' and 'motion is motion of the mover', not meaning that they are property, act, or accident. A thing may have things that are not its motion or will. Thus, attributing knowledge to God does not imply composition or contingency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying God's knowledge is 'different' from Him implies it is a separate entity like two distinct things.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that 'difference' (غيرية) in kalam means separability in time/place/existence, which does not apply to God and His attributes. They are not two independent things.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the correct meaning of saying that God has knowledge?
A) It means knowledge is an accident in God.
B) It means knowledge is God's property.
C) It means He is knowing by a knowledge that belongs to His essence.
D) It means knowledge is a separate entity from God.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is an essential attribute that is neither a separate entity nor a mere property; it is part of His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Baqillani says that insisting on using the wrong expression is a sign of weakness. Is there a belief you hold that you insist on expressing in a certain way, even when the meaning is what matters? How can you shift your focus from words to truth?
?
If Allah's knowledge were an accident or a possession, it could be gained or lost. Does your heart truly feel that His knowledge is eternal and inseparable from Him? What would change in your life if you fully internalized that?

The Meaning of God’s Knowledge as an Attribute
Baqillani explains the meaning of God's knowledge and refutes three misinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defines the attribute of divine knowledge and distinguishes it from improper interpretations like possession, action, or inherence.

Core Idea
God’s knowledge is a unique attribute, not a possession, action, or accident.

Baqillani addresses a common question: what do we really mean when we say God has knowledge? The objection imagines that if God has knowledge, it must be either His property, His action, or an accident inhering in Him. Baqillani rejects all three and offers a more precise understanding. He uses an analogy from everyday language to show that attributing an attribute to a subject does not require any of those mistaken implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the three interpretations being rejected: possession, action, and inherence.
Focus on the precise wording of the answer: 'علم بالنفس التي علم له فقط'.
2
Understand the correct meaning
Baqillani clarifies that knowledge is an attribute uniquely related to God, not as an added entity. Understand the phrase 'العلم الذي أضيف إليه'.
This is the key positive definition.
3
Analyze the analogy
Consider the analogy of movement and will. How does attributing movement to a mover not imply possession, action, or inherence? Apply this to God's knowledge.
The analogy shows the ordinary way we speak of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا فما معنى قولكم إن لله تعالى علماً أتعنون بذلك أنه ملكه أو فعله أو حالّ فيه أو أنه عالم بنفس العلم الذي أضيف إليه؟ قيل لهم: معنى ذلك أنه علم بالنفس التي علم له فقط. وليس ذلك من معنى أنها ملكه أو فعله أو حالة فيه بسبيل. وهذا كما نقول نحن وأنتم إن الإرادة إرادة له، وإن الحركة حركة للمتحرك، لا بمعنى أنها ملكه أو فعله أو حالة فيه، لأنه قد يحصل فيه ما ليس بحركة ولا إرادة له. وكذلك فقد يريد ويتحرك بما ليس بملك له ولا فعل له ويحل فيه ما ليس بحركة له. وبالله التوفيق.
If they say: 'What is the meaning of your statement that Allah Almighty has knowledge? Do you mean by that that it is His possession, or His action, or a state inhering in Him, or that He is knowing by the very knowledge that is attributed to Him?' It is said to them: The meaning of that is that it is knowledge of the self that is known to Him only. That is not in any way the meaning that it is His possession, or His action, or a state inhering in Him. This is like when we and you say that will is a will for Him, and movement is a movement for the mover, not in the sense that it is his possession, or his action, or a state inhering in him, because there may occur in him what is not a movement or a will for him. Likewise, he may will and move with what is not his possession, nor his action, and there may settle in him what is not a movement for him. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the meaning of God's attribute of knowledge (ʿilm). The author presents a hypothetical objection: if someone asks what is meant by saying 'God has knowledge,' do we mean that knowledge is His property, His action, or something that inheres in Him? The answer is none of these; rather, it means that God is knowing by a knowledge that is related to Him alone, not as an accident or possession. To clarify, Baqillani uses an analogy from ordinary language: we say 'the mover's movement' and 'God's will' without implying that the movement or will is a separate property or action of the subject. A moving body may have a movement that is neither its property nor its act, yet we still attribute movement to it. Similarly, God's knowledge is uniquely His without the implications of ownership, action, or inherence. The passage concludes with an invocation of divine success.

Definition of Attribute and Its Classification into Temporal and Eternal
Baqillani defines attribute, distinguishes it from description, and classifies attributes into temporal and eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's definition of 'attribute' (ṣifa) and the distinction between temporal and eternal attributes, especially as applied to Allah.

Core Idea
Attribute is the real entity; description is the verbal epithet derived from it.

Baqillani begins by clarifying a terminological confusion: What is an attribute (ṣifa) and how does it relate to a description (waṣf)? He then divides attributes into two kinds: those that come and go, changing the subject, and those that are eternal, distinguishing the subject without causing change. This foundational distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Baqillani's definitions and classifications.
Pay attention to the technical terms.
2
Identify the definition of ṣifa
Notice that ṣifa is defined as the real thing found with the described, while waṣf is the verbal epithet (naʿt) derived from it.
This distinction is key to avoiding confusion.
3
Understand temporal attributes
Temporal attributes exist sometimes and are absent other times, changing the subject's state. Examples: knowledge, ignorance, blackness, will.
These are accidents that come and go.
4
Understand eternal attributes
Eternal attributes are permanent and do not change the subject because they have always been present. Applied to God's attributes like life, knowledge, power.
God's attributes never cease, so they do not cause change.
5
Clarify the misconception
Some think eternal attributes change God, but Baqillani explains that since they are eternal, they never bring about a new state.
This is a common misunderstanding in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في معنى الصفة وهل هي الوصف أم معنى سواه
إن قال قائل : ما الصفة عندكم، وما الوصف، وهل هما واحد أم لا؟ قيل له : أما الصفة فهي الشيء الذي يوجد بالموصوف أو يكون له، ويُكسبه الوصف الذي هو النعت الذي يصدر عن الصفة . فإن كانت مما يوجد تارةً ويعدم أخرى غيرت حكم الموصوف وصيّرته عند وجودها على حكم لم يكن عليه عند عدمها وذلك كالسواد والبياض والإرادة والكراهة والعلم والجهل والقدرة والعجز وما جرى مجرى ذلك مما يتغيّر به الموصوف إذا وُجد به ويُكسبه حكماً لم يكن عليه . وإن كانت الصفة لازمة، كان حكمها أن تُكسب من وُجدت به حكماً يخالف حكم من ليست له تلك الصفة. وذلك نحو حياة الباري سبحانه وعلمه وقدرته وكلامه وإرادته وما عدا ذلك من صفاته الثابتة الموجبة له مفارقة من ليس على هذه الصفات، وإن لم يتغيّر القديم سبحانه بوجودها به عن حالة كان عليها. إذ كانت لم تزل موجودة، ولم يكن قط سبحانه موجوداً وليس بذي حياة ولا علم ولا قدرة ولا سمع ولا بصر، ثم وُجدت هذه الصفات بعد أن لم تكن له ولا يجوز أيضاً أن يوجد وقتاً ما وليس له هذه الصفات، إذ كان العدم عليها مستحيلا. وإنما يتغيّر بوجود
Chapter on the discussion about the meaning of attribute (al-sifa) and whether it is the description (al-wasf) or something other than it. If a questioner says: 'What is the attribute with you, and what is the description, and are they the same or not?' It is said to him: As for attribute, it is the thing that is found with the described (al-mawsuf) or belongs to it, and it imparts to it the description, which is the epithet (al-na't) that issues from the attribute. If it is among things that exist sometimes and are absent at other times, it changes the ruling of the described and makes it, when it is present, in a ruling it was not upon before its absence. That is like blackness and whiteness, will and hate, knowledge and ignorance, power and incapacity, and what runs along these lines of things by which the described is changed when it is found with it and imparts to it a ruling it was not upon. And if the attribute is perpetual (lazima), its ruling is that it imparts to the one with whom it is found a ruling contrary to the ruling of one who does not have that attribute. That is like the life of the Creator (al-Bari) - exalted is He -, His knowledge, His power, His speech, His will, and besides that from His established attributes that necessitate for Him distinction from those who do not have these attributes, even though the Eternal (al-Qadim) - exalted is He - does not change by their existence with Him from a state He was upon. Since they have never ceased to exist, and He - exalted is He - was never existing without having life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, then these attributes existed after not being for Him. It is also not permissible that He exists at any time without having these attributes, since their nonexistence is impossible. And change occurs only by existence
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'attribute' (ṣifa). The author begins by distinguishing between ṣifa (attribute) and wasf (description/noun of attribution). The attribute is the real entity that exists in the subject (mawṣūf) or belongs to it, while the description is the verbal expression (naʿt) derived from that attribute. The attributes are then divided into two categories: (1) temporal (mutaghayyira) attributes, which come and go and thereby change the state of the subject—examples include accidents like blackness, whiteness, knowledge, ignorance, etc.; (2) eternal (lāzima) attributes, which are permanent and do not cause change in the subject because they never cease, as in the case of God's attributes (life, knowledge, power, etc.). For God, these attributes have always existed with Him, so they do not produce a change in His essence, even though they distinguish Him from others. The argument emphasizes that it is impossible for God to lack these attributes at any time, because their non-existence would imply change in the Eternal, which is impossible. The passage ends in mid-sentence explaining that change only occurs with existence (of new attributes), implying that God's attributes are not new.

Misconception: Eternal attributes cause change in God's essence when they are present.
Correction: Baqillani states that eternal attributes have always been present with God, so they never bring about a new state or change.

C) An eternal attribute does not change the subject because it has always been present, while a temporal attribute changes the subject when it appears.
D) An eternal attribute is a description (wasf), while a temporal attribute is a real entity.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defines attribute (ṣifa) as the real entity in the subject that gives rise to a description, and classifies attributes into temporal (changing the subject) and eternal (permanent, as in God's attributes).

How does the constancy of God's attributes challenge you to seek stability in your relationship with Him, rather than in fleeting traits? Will you today ask Allah to make your attachment to His eternal attributes a source of steadfastness?

Eternity of God's Attributes and the Definition of Wasf
Baqillani proves eternity of attributes and distinguishes wasf from sifah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attributes must be eternal and how description (wasf) differs from the attribute (sifah) itself.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal; describing Him as knowing is not the same as His knowledge attribute.

Imam al-Baqillani begins by establishing a rational proof for the eternity of God's attributes, showing that temporal origination would lead to impossible consequences. He then clarifies a crucial distinction: the 'description' (wasf) we utter is speech that can be true or false, while the 'attribute' (sifah) is a real entity in God that does not admit truth-values. This lesson unpacks both arguments and definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a concise style, moving from proof to definition.
2
Follow the proof of eternity
Identify the assumptions: if attributes were originated, they would resemble creatures' attributes and imply opposites. Understand why this is impossible for God.
This is a proof by contradiction.
3
Distinguish wasf from sifah
Note Baqillani's definition: 'wasf' is audible speech that can be true/false, while 'sifah' is the real attribute subsisting in God. Use the Zayd example to solidify this.
Do not confuse the two; they are related but distinct.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for talking about God. Our descriptions can be false, but God's attributes are necessarily true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد دللنا فيما سلف على إثبات هذه الصفات الله سبحانه، وبينا أنه لا يجوز حدوثها له . لأن ذلك يوجب أن تكون من جنس صفات المخلوقين، وأن تكون ذات أضداد نصفات المخلوقين، وأن يكون الباري سبحانه قبل حدوثها موصوفاً بما يضادها وينافيها من الأوصاف . ولو كان ذلك كذلك لوجب قدم أضدادها، ولاستحال أن يكون القديم سبحانه موصوفاً هذه الحال، وأن يوجد منه من ضروب الأفعال ما يدل على كونه عالماً بها قادراً حياً. وفي بطلان ذلك دليل على قدم هذه الصفات، وأن الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يتغير بها ويصير له حكم لم يكن قبل وجودها إذلا أول لوجودها . وأما الوصف، فهو قول الواصف الله تعالى ولغيره بأنه عالم حيّ قادر منعم مفضل. وهذا الوصف الذي هو كلام مسموع أو عبارة عنه، غير الصفة القائمة بالله تعالى التي لوجودها به يكون عالماً وقادراً ومريداً . وكذلك قولنا زيد حيّ عالم قادر هو وصف لزيد وخبر عن كونه على ما الصفات به، وهو قول يمكن أن يدخله الصدق والكذب. وعلم وجود زيد وقدرته هما صفتان له موجودتان بذاته يصدر الوصف والاسم عنهما ، ولا يمكن دخول الصدق والكذب فيهما . فإن كان الله الواصف لنفسه بأنه حي عالم قادر قديم أزلي ، كان اقتضاه
And we have demonstrated previously the establishment of these attributes of Allah subhanahu, and we have shown that it is not permissible for them to have temporal origination (haduth) for Him. Because that would necessitate that they be of the kind of attributes of creatures, and that they be like the opposites of the attributes of creatures, and that the Creator (al-Bari) subhanahu before their temporal origination be described with what contradicts them and opposes them of descriptions. And if that were so, then the opposites of them would necessarily be eternal, and it would be impossible that the Eternal (al-Qadim) subhanahu be described in this state, and that there exist from Him the kinds of acts that indicate that He is knowing, powerful, and living. And in the falsity of that is a proof of the eternity of these attributes, and that Allah subhanahu cannot change by them and become in a state He was not before their existence, since there is no beginning for their existence. And as for the description (al-wasf), it is the saying of the describer (al-wasif) about Allah ta'ala and about other than Him that He is knowing, living, powerful, beneficent, bountiful. And this description, which is audible speech or an expression of it, is other than the attribute (as-sifah) subsisting in Allah ta'ala by whose existence in Him He is knowing, powerful, and willing. And similarly, our saying 'Zayd is living, knowing, powerful' is a description of Zayd and a report about his being in a state of having attributes, and it is a statement that can admit truth and falsehood. And the knowledge of Zayd and his power are two attributes of his subsisting in his essence, from which the description and name issue, and truth and falsehood cannot enter into them. So if Allah is the one describing Himself as living, knowing, powerful, eternal, pre-eternal, then [the description] necessitates it.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a definition. Baqillani first provides a reasoned argument that God's attributes (life, knowledge, power, etc.) must be eternal, not temporally originated. He argues that if they were originated, they would be similar to the attributes of creatures, imply that God previously had opposites (which are also impossible), and that before their origination God would have been described with contradictory descriptions. This chain of absurdities proves the eternity of these attributes. Furthermore, the existence of acts from God that require knowledge, power, and life shows that He has always had these attributes. Then, Baqillani defines 'wasf' (description) as the speech of a describer—e.g., saying 'Allah is knowing, living, powerful'—which is audible and can be true or false. This description is distinct from the attribute (sifah) itself, which subsists in God and is not subject to truth/falsity. He gives the example of Zayd: saying 'Zayd is knowing' is a description that can be evaluated as true or false, while Zayd's actual knowledge is an attribute that is either present or absent, not true or false. The passage ends with the condition that if Allah describes Himself as eternal and pre-eternal, then the description necessitates the reality of those attributes.

الوصف
الصدق والكذب
الحدوث

Correction: Baqillani shows that 'wasf' is speech that can be true or false, while 'sifah' is the real attribute subsisting in God, which is not subject to truth/falsity.

Key Takeaway

Proof of the Eternity of God's Attributes
Baqillani proves God's attributes are eternal by refuting temporal origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Baqillani argues that God's attributes must be eternal by refuting the possibility of their temporal origin.

Core Idea
If God's attributes were temporal, they would be like creatures' attributes—impossible for the Creator.

This lesson presents Baqillani's argument for the eternity of God's attributes. He begins by dismissing the idea that attributes could be temporally originated or separable from God. Then he shows that temporality would reduce divine attributes to the level of creatures' attributes, leading to absurd consequences. The invalidity of those consequences proves the attributes are beginningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice Baqillani's concise style.
2
Identify the two rejected claims
Find where Baqillani states that attributes could be temporal or separable from God. Understand why he dismisses these as incompatible with divine majesty.
3
Trace the consequence of temporality
Follow the argument: if attributes were temporal, they would resemble creatures' attributes, implying opposites and contradictions. This leads to the impossibility of the Eternal being described in that state.
The proof uses reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the proof from acts
Notice that Baqillani adds that without eternal attributes, God's acts (indicating knowledge, power, life) would be meaningless. This reinforces the necessity of eternal attributes.
This connects divine acts to attribute eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفات من لم تكن له قبل، ومن جاز أن يفارقه الصفات، والله سبحانه يتعالى عن ذلك. وقد دللنا فيما سلف على إثبات هذه الصفات لله سبحانه، وبينا أنه لا يجوز حدوثها له، لأن ذلك يوجب أن تكون من جنس صفات المخلوقين، وأن تكون ذات أضداد صفات المخلوقين، وأن يكون الباري سبحانه قبل حدوثها موصوفاً بما يضادها وينافيها من الأوصاف. ولو كان ذلك كذلك لوجب قدم أضدادها، ولاستحال أن يكون القديم سبحانه موصوفاً هذه الحال، وأن يوجد منه من ضروب الأفعال ما يدل على كونه عالماً بها وقادراً حياً. وفي بطلان ذلك دليل على قدم هذه الصفات، وأن الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يتغير بها ويصير له حكم لم يكن قبل وجودها، إذ لا أول لوجودها.
Attributes which were not before, and that it is possible that attributes separate from Him—and God is exalted above that. We have already demonstrated in what preceded the affirmation of these attributes for God, and we made clear that it is not permissible for them to occur in time for Him, because that would require that they be of the same kind as the attributes of creatures, and that they have opposites of the attributes of creatures, and that the Creator, before their coming into existence, be described by attributes that contradict and oppose them. If that were the case, then their opposites would have to be eternal, and it would be impossible for the Eternal to be described in this state, and that there should proceed from Him kinds of acts that indicate that He is knowing, powerful, living with them. And in the invalidity of that is a proof of the eternity of these attributes, and that God cannot change by them and acquire a rule that did not exist before their existence, since there is no beginning to their existence.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the eternity of God's attributes. Baqillani begins by stating two problematic claims: that attributes could be temporally originated (not existing before) or that they could separate from God—both of which he dismisses as God is exalted above such. He then argues that if attributes were temporal, they would resemble creatures' attributes (which are accidental) and would have opposites, implying God had opposite attributes before they arose—impossible for the eternal God. Moreover, if attributes were temporal, God would not have been described by them eternally, and His acts (which prove His knowledge, power, and life) would be meaningless without eternal attributes. The invalidity of these consequences proves the attributes are eternal, without beginning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributes being eternal means they are separate entities independent of God.
Correction: The proof only establishes their beginninglessness, not multiplicity of eternal beings. Attributes are not separate from God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of God's attributes is proven by the impossibility of their temporal origin, which would lead to absurdities incompatible with divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If God could change by acquiring attributes, then His promises could change too. Can you truly trust a God whose attributes are not eternal? What would that mean for your hope in His forgiveness and mercy?
?
Baqillani says God cannot be described by opposites of His attributes. But in your life, do you ever act as if God is indifferent or unaware of your struggles? How does knowing that His attributes are eternal and perfect change how you approach Him right now?

God's Self-Description Is Not Other Than Essence Attributes
Baqillani argues God's self-description with essence attributes is not distinct from them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's self-description with essence attributes is not a distinct entity from the attributes themselves, based on the inseparability of eternal attributes.

Core Idea
God's description by essence attributes is not other than the attributes because eternal attributes cannot be separated.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani addresses a subtle theological point: when God describes Himself as Living, Knowing, Powerful, is that description something distinct from the attributes themselves? He argues that it is not, because the eternal attributes are inseparable. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the three parts: Baqillani states a thesis, gives a reference to a future proof, and provides a key premise about the inseparability of eternal attributes.
2
Identify the thesis and what it denies
Baqillani says the description is a meaning that is neither the attributes themselves (like knowledge) nor something entirely other. Understand what positions he is rejecting.
3
Understand the key premise about distinctness
Baqillani explains that eternal attributes cannot be 'distinct' (mutaghāyira) because they cannot be separated in time, place, or existence. This is what he means by 'two distinct things' (al-ghīrān).
This is the core of the argument.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since the description shares the same eternal nature as the attributes and inseparability, it is not other than them. Baqillani concludes that the description of God by essence attributes is not other than the attributes.
The conclusion is directly based on the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الله الواصف لنفسه بأنه حي عالم قادر قديم أزلي، كان وصفه لنفسه معنى لا يقال هو علمه وحياته وقدرته، ولا يقال هو غير الصفات لقيام الدليل بما سنذكره في باب نفي خلق القرآن على قدم كلامه سبحانه وأنه جارٍ مجرى سائر صفات ذاته. وقد ثبت أن الصفات القديمة لا يجوز أن تكون متغايرة من حيث لم تجز مفارقة شيء منها للآخر بزمان ولا مكان ولا بأن يوجد منها شيء مع عدم الآخر. وكان هذا معنى الغيرين وحقيقة وصفهما بذلك. فثبت بهذه الجملة أن وصف القديم سبحانه لنفسه بصفات ذاته ليس بغير لصفات الذات.
If God is the one describing Himself as living, knowing, powerful, eternal, beginningless, then His description of Himself is a meaning that cannot be said to be His knowledge, life, and power, nor can it be said that it is other than the attributes, due to the proof that we will mention in the chapter on denying the creation of the Qur'an concerning the eternity of His speech and that it runs along the lines of the rest of the attributes of His essence. And it has been established that the eternal attributes cannot be distinct from each other in the sense that it is not permissible for any of them to separate from another in time or place, nor that one of them should exist with the non-existence of the other. And this is the meaning of 'two distinct things' (al-ghīrān) and the reality of describing them as such. Thus it is established by this summary that the description of the Eternal, glorious, of Himself by the attributes of His essence is not other than the attributes of the essence.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument that when God describes Himself with attributes of essence (such as 'living, knowing, powerful, eternal'), His description (waṣf) is not a separate entity from the attributes themselves. Baqillani introduces this by stating that the description is a meaning (ma‘nā) that cannot be identified with the specific attributes (knowledge, life, power) nor claimed to be wholly other, as a future proof on the eternity of the Qur'an will show. The key principle: eternal attributes cannot be 'distinct' (mutaghāyira) in the sense of being separable—they never differ in time or place, nor can one exist without the other. This is what it means for two things to be 'two distinct things' (al-ghīrān) in this context. Therefore, the description of God by these eternal attributes is not other than the attributes themselves.

Correction: Baqillani clarifies that when it concerns essence attributes, the description is not other than the attributes; it shares their eternal, inseparable nature.

Key Takeaway
God's self-description by essence attributes is not other than the attributes themselves because eternal attributes are inseparable.

?
Baqillani argues that eternal attributes cannot be separated from each other. Have you ever pictured God's power as something separate from His knowledge—acting without full awareness? How does this passage correct that fragmented view? What does it mean for your trust in His actions?
?
God's self-description is neither identical to each attribute nor wholly other; this is a mystery. Does your heart rest in the fact that God's essence is beyond complete human comprehension, or do you secretly wish to fully grasp Him? How might you cultivate awe before His transcendence today?

God's Action-Attribute Description Is Other Than the Attributes
Baqillani distinguishes God's description with action attributes from the actions themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani distinguishes between God's description with action attributes and the actions themselves, and how attributes of essence differ from attributes of action.

Core Idea
God's descriptions of Himself as Creator, Provider, etc., are not the same as the acts of creating and providing.

Baqillani delves into a subtle distinction: when God describes Himself with attributes like 'Creator' or 'Just,' that description is not identical to the actions of creating or being just. The actions are created (muhdath), while the description itself is part of God's eternal speech. This passage clarifies why attributes of essence (like speech) are fundamentally different from attributes of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on the distinction between 'waṣf' (description) and 'ṣifāt' (attributes).
Notice that the passage begins with a conditional statement: 'If the description of God... is a description by the attributes of His actions...'
2
Identify the two categories
Identify the two categories: attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-af‘āl). Observe the examples given: 'I am Creator, Provider, Just, Beneficent, Gracious.'
The speech 'I am Creator' itself is an attribute of essence, while the act of creation is an attribute of action.
3
Understand the argument for distinctness
Baqillani argues that essence attributes existed even when actions did not exist, proving they are distinct by essence, not by temporal or spatial separation.
This is a key point: separation in time or place only indicates distinctness; it is not the cause of it.
4
Reflect on the generalization
Consider the general principle: any two distinct things (ghīrān) are distinct by their essences. Reflect on how this applies to divine attributes.
The passage ends with a note about a created being describing himself, which parallels the divine case but with creation involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان وصف الله سبحانه لنفسه وصفاً بصفات أفعاله نحو قوله تعالى: إني خالق ورازق وعادل ومحسن ومتفضل، وما جرى مجرى ذلك كان وصفه لنفسه بهذه الصفات غير صفاته التي هي الخلق والرزق والعدل والإحسان والإنعام لأن هذه الصفات هي أفعال الله صفات أفعاله والكلام الذي هو قوله تعالى، وهي محدثات. إني خالق عادل متفضل محسن من صفات ذاته. وصفات الذات غير صفات الأفعال، لأنها قد كانت موجودة مع عدمها فوجب أن يسدل ذلك على تغايرها لأنفسها. وكذلك كل غيرين إنما يتغايران لأنفسهما. وافتراقهما بالزمان والمكان والوجود والعدم إنما يدل على تغايرهما ويكون تفسيراً لوصفهما بأنهما غيران؛ وليس من هذه المفارقات ما هو علة في كون الغيرين غيرين. وإن كان الواصف لنفسه محدّثاً، فإن وصفه لنفسه أيضاً هو قوله:
And if the description of God, glorious, of Himself is a description by the attributes of His actions, such as His saying: 'I am Creator, Provider, Just, Beneficent, Gracious,' and what runs along that, then His description of Himself by these attributes is other than His attributes which are creation, provision, justice, beneficence, and graciousness, because these attributes are the actions of God, attributes of His actions, and the speech which is His saying—they are created. 'I am Creator, Just, Gracious, Beneficent' is among the attributes of His essence. The attributes of essence are other than the attributes of actions, because they (the attributes of essence) existed while the actions did not exist, so it is necessary that this indicates their distinctness by their essences. Likewise, every two distinct things are distinct only by their essences. Their separation in time, place, existence, and non-existence only indicates their distinctness and is an explanation of describing them as being two distinct things; not from these separations is there a cause for the two things being distinct. And if the describer of himself is a created being, then his description of himself is also his statement:
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction when God describes Himself with attributes of action (ṣifāt al-af‘āl), such as 'Creator, Provider, Just.' Here, God's description (waṣf) is other than the attributes themselves (the acts of creation, provision, etc.), because those acts are created (muḥdathāt). Notably, the utterance 'I am Creator, Just, Gracious' itself is part of God's speech, which is an attribute of essence. Thus, attributes of essence (like speech) are different from attributes of action (like creating) because essence attributes exist even when no actions occur. Baqillani then generalizes: two distinct things (ghīrān) are distinct by their essences, not merely by temporal or spatial separation—those separations are signs, not causes, of distinctness. Finally, he notes that if a created being describes himself, his description is likewise his statement.

محدثات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that action attributes are eternal like essence attributes.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that action attributes are created (muḥdathāt) and distinct from eternal essence attributes by their very essence.

Key Takeaway

The Description with Attributes of Essence is Not Other
Baqillani argues that description with essential attributes is not other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani holds that God's description of Himself with essential attributes like knowledge and power is not considered 'other' (ghayr) than those attributes, and the reasoning involving pre-eternity and inseparability.

Core Idea
When God describes Himself with essential attributes, the description is not other than the attribute.

Baqillani begins by addressing a key question: does God's self-description with attributes like knowledge and life count as something distinct from those attributes? He argues that for essential attributes, the description is not 'other' because the pre-eternal attributes are inseparable in time and place. This sets the foundation for his larger distinction between attributes of essence and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of Baqillani's argument.
Notice the nuanced claim: description is neither the attribute itself nor other.
2
Understand the claim
Baqillani states that the description with essential attributes is not said to be the attribute itself nor other. This is the thesis.
3
Examine the evidence
He references the argument about pre-eternity of God's speech and the inseparability of essential attributes in time, place, and existence.
This evidence shows that pre-eternal attributes cannot be mutually distinct.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, the description of God with essential attributes is not other than the attributes themselves.
This is a key principle for understanding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفه لنفسه معنى لا يقال هو علمه وحياته وقدرته، ولا يقال هو غير الصفات لقيام الدليل بما سنذكره في باب نفي خلق القرآن على قدم كلامه سبحانه وأنه جارٍ مجرى سائر صفات ذاته. وقد ثبت أن الصفات القديمة لا يجوز أن تكون متغايرة من حيث لم تجز مفارقة شيء منها للآخر بزمان ولا مكان ولا بأن يوجد منها شيء مع عدم الآخر. وكان هذا معنى الغيرين وحقيقة وصفهما بذلك. فثبت بهذه الجملة أن وصف القديم سبحانه لنفسه بصفات ذاته ليس بغير لصفات الذات. وإن كان وصف الله سبحانه لنفسه وصفاً بصفات أفعاله نحو قوله تعالى: إني خالق ورازق وعادل ومحسن ومتفضل، وما جرى مجرى ذلك كان وصفه لنفسه بهذه الصفات غير صفاته التي هي الخلق والرزق والعدل والإحسان والإنعام لأن هذه الصفات هي أفعال الله صفات أفعاله والكلام الذي هو قوله تعالى، وهي محدثات. وصفات الذات غير صفات الأفعال، لأنها قد كانت موجودة مع عدمها فوجب أن يدل ذلك على تغايرها لأنفسها. وكذلك كل غيرين إنما يتغايران لأنفسهما. وافتراقهما بالزمان والمكان والوجود والعدم إنما يدل على تغايرهما ويكون تفسيراً لوصفهما بأنهما غيران؛ وليس من هذه المفارقات ما هو علة في كون الغيرين غيرين. وإن كان الواصف لنفسه محدثاً، فإن وصفه لنفسه أيضاً هو قوله: إني حي قادر منعم متفضل عالم. وذلك القول الذي هو الوصف هو غير صفاته التي هي من أفعاله ومن غير فعله. لأن الإنسان محدثة. وكلامه الذي هو وصفه لنفسه بما وصفها به محدث. وكل محدثين ليسا بجملة ولا داخلين تحت جملة فهما غيران. وقولنا «وصف» و «كلام» ليس بوصف واقع على كلام الإنسان وشيء من صفاته الكلام. فيستحيل أن يكون الوصف غيراً لتلك الصفة، فوجب أن يكون وصف الإنسان غير سائر صفاته التي ليست بوصف ووجب تفصيل ذلك في وصف القديم سبحانه وصفاته على ما قلناه وبيناه من قبل.
His description of Himself is a meaning that is not said to be His knowledge, life, and power, nor is it said to be other than the attributes due to the evidence we will mention in the chapter on negating the creation of the Qur'an regarding the pre-eternity of His speech, and that it is in the same category as the rest of the attributes of His essence. And it has been established that the pre-eternal attributes cannot be mutually distinct (mutaghāyira) in the sense that nothing among them can separate from another in time or place, nor by any of them existing while the other is absent. And that is the meaning of 'two others' (al-ghayrayn) and the reality of describing them as such. So by this summary it is established that the description of the pre-eternal (al-Qadīm), exalted is He, of Himself with the attributes of His essence is not other than the attributes of the essence. But if the description of God, exalted is He, of Himself is a description with attributes of His actions, such as His saying: 'I am Creator, Provider, Just, Beneficent, and Munificent', and what runs in that vein, then His description of Himself with these attributes is other than His attributes which are creation, provision, justice, beneficence, and favor, because these attributes are the actions of God, attributes of His actions, and the speech which is His saying, exalted is He, and they are originated things (muḥdathāt). And the attributes of the essence are other than the attributes of actions, because they [attributes of essence] existed when the latter were absent, so that necessarily indicates their mutual distinctness in themselves. Likewise, every two others (ghayrān) are mutually distinct only in themselves. Their separation in time, place, existence, and non-existence only indicates their distinctness and is an interpretation of their being described as 'two others'; and none of these separative factors is the cause of the two others being other. And if the one describing himself is originated (muḥdath), then his description of himself is also his saying: 'I am living, powerful, beneficent, munificent, knowing.' And that speech which is the description is other than his attributes which are from his actions and from other than his actions, because the human being is originated, and his speech which is his description of himself with what he described himself is originated. And every two originated things that are neither a whole nor included within a whole are two others. And our saying 'description' (waṣf) and 'speech' (kalām) is not a description occurring on human speech and something of his attributes is speech. So it is impossible for the description to be other than that attribute, so it is necessary that the description of the human be other than the rest of his attributes which are not description, and it is necessary to detail that in the description of the pre-eternal, exalted is He, and His attributes in the way we have said and explained before.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the relationship between God's self-description and His attributes, distinguishing between attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-afʿāl). The author begins by stating that when God describes Himself with essential attributes like knowledge, life, and power, this description is not considered 'other' (ghayr) than those attributes. Evidence for this comes from the discussion on the pre-eternity of God's speech (kalām), which is like other essential attributes. He then argues that pre-eternal attributes cannot be mutually distinct in the sense of separation in time or place, so the description being other is negated. However, when God describes Himself with action attributes like 'Creator,' 'Provider,' etc., the description is other than the actions themselves because those actions are created (muḥdathāt). The attributes of essence existed before actions, proving their distinctness. The author extends this to created beings: a human being's self-description (e.g., 'I am living, powerful') is his speech, which is created and thus other than his attributes (like actual living and power), because created speech and created attributes are not part of a whole. The key point is that for God, description with essential attributes is not other, but with action attributes it is other.

Key Terms
وصف (waṣf)
صفات الذات (ṣifāt al-dhāt)
غير (ghayr)
محدث (muḥdath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that God's description of Himself (e.g., 'I am Knowing') is a created speech act separate from His eternal attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Baqillani argues that for essential attributes, the description is not other; the pre-eternal speech of God is inseparable from His essence.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani in this passage, why is the description with essential attributes not 'other'?
A) Because the description is the attribute itself.
B) Because essential attributes cannot separate in time or place.
C) Because essential attributes are created.
D) Because the description is a separate action.

Key Takeaway
For essential attributes, God's self-description is not other because the attributes are inseparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is All-Knowing' or 'God is All-Powerful', do you sometimes mentally separate His knowledge from His power, as if they are different 'parts' of God? How does this lesson challenge that inclination?
?
If God's essential attributes are inseparable from each other and from His essence, what does that mean for how you approach Him in supplication? Are you calling upon a God whose knowledge and mercy are distinct, or a single unified reality?
?
Reflect on a moment when you doubted God's wisdom or power. Could that doubt have stemmed from treating those attributes as separate 'things' that might fail independently? How does Baqillani's argument about inseparability strengthen your trust?

7 min
Description with Attributes of Action is Other
Baqillani proves that description with action attributes is other, and extends to human.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's distinction that when God describes Himself with attributes of action (like Creator, Provider), the description is other than the action, and how this applies to human self-description.

Core Idea
God's description with action attributes is other than the actions themselves, unlike essential attributes.

In contrast to essential attributes, when God describes Himself as 'Creator' or 'Provider', the description is not the same as the act of creating or providing. Baqillani explains that these action attributes are created, so the description is other. He then extends this to humans to illustrate the principle that two distinct originated things are 'other'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on action attributes
Read the Arabic and translation for the section on attributes of action.
Pay attention to the contrast with the previous lesson.
2
Understand why description is other
Baqillani says the description is other because the actions (creation, etc.) are originated, while the description is pre-eternal speech.
3
Grasp the general principle
He states that two things are 'other' in themselves; separation indicates otherness but is not the cause.
4
Examine the human analogy
A human's self-description (speech) is created, so it is other than his actual attributes because they are separate originated entities.
This reinforces the logic for the divine case.
5
Synthesize the distinction
For God, description with essential attributes is not other, but with action attributes it is other. For humans, description is always other because both description and attributes are created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفه لنفسه معنى لا يقال هو علمه وحياته وقدرته، ولا يقال هو غير الصفات لقيام الدليل بما سنذكره في باب نفي خلق القرآن على قدم كلامه سبحانه وأنه جارٍ مجرى سائر صفات ذاته. وقد ثبت أن الصفات القديمة لا يجوز أن تكون متغايرة من حيث لم تجز مفارقة شيء منها للآخر بزمان ولا مكان ولا بأن يوجد منها شيء مع عدم الآخر. وكان هذا معنى الغيرين وحقيقة وصفهما بذلك. فثبت بهذه الجملة أن وصف القديم سبحانه لنفسه بصفات ذاته ليس بغير لصفات الذات. وإن كان وصف الله سبحانه لنفسه وصفاً بصفات أفعاله نحو قوله تعالى: إني خالق ورازق وعادل ومحسن ومتفضل، وما جرى مجرى ذلك كان وصفه لنفسه بهذه الصفات غير صفاته التي هي الخلق والرزق والعدل والإحسان والإنعام لأن هذه الصفات هي أفعال الله صفات أفعاله والكلام الذي هو قوله تعالى، وهي محدثات. وصفات الذات غير صفات الأفعال، لأنها قد كانت موجودة مع عدمها فوجب أن يدل ذلك على تغايرها لأنفسها. وكذلك كل غيرين إنما يتغايران لأنفسهما. وافتراقهما بالزمان والمكان والوجود والعدم إنما يدل على تغايرهما ويكون تفسيراً لوصفهما بأنهما غيران؛ وليس من هذه المفارقات ما هو علة في كون الغيرين غيرين. وإن كان الواصف لنفسه محدثاً، فإن وصفه لنفسه أيضاً هو قوله: إني حي قادر منعم متفضل عالم. وذلك القول الذي هو الوصف هو غير صفاته التي هي من أفعاله ومن غير فعله. لأن الإنسان محدثة. وكلامه الذي هو وصفه لنفسه بما وصفها به محدث. وكل محدثين ليسا بجملة ولا داخلين تحت جملة فهما غيران. وقولنا «وصف» و «كلام» ليس بوصف واقع على كلام الإنسان وشيء من صفاته الكلام. فيستحيل أن يكون الوصف غيراً لتلك الصفة، فوجب أن يكون وصف الإنسان غير سائر صفاته التي ليست بوصف ووجب تفصيل ذلك في وصف القديم سبحانه وصفاته على ما قلناه وبيناه من قبل.
His description of Himself is a meaning that is not said to be His knowledge, life, and power, nor is it said to be other than the attributes due to the evidence we will mention in the chapter on negating the creation of the Qur'an regarding the pre-eternity of His speech, and that it is in the same category as the rest of the attributes of His essence. And it has been established that the pre-eternal attributes cannot be mutually distinct (mutaghāyira) in the sense that nothing among them can separate from another in time or place, nor by any of them existing while the other is absent. And that is the meaning of 'two others' (al-ghayrayn) and the reality of describing them as such. So by this summary it is established that the description of the pre-eternal (al-Qadīm), exalted is He, of Himself with the attributes of His essence is not other than the attributes of the essence. But if the description of God, exalted is He, of Himself is a description with attributes of His actions, such as His saying: 'I am Creator, Provider, Just, Beneficent, and Munificent', and what runs in that vein, then His description of Himself with these attributes is other than His attributes which are creation, provision, justice, beneficence, and favor, because these attributes are the actions of God, attributes of His actions, and the speech which is His saying, exalted is He, and they are originated things (muḥdathāt). And the attributes of the essence are other than the attributes of actions, because they [attributes of essence] existed when the latter were absent, so that necessarily indicates their mutual distinctness in themselves. Likewise, every two others (ghayrān) are mutually distinct only in themselves. Their separation in time, place, existence, and non-existence only indicates their distinctness and is an interpretation of their being described as 'two others'; and none of these separative factors is the cause of the two others being other. And if the one describing himself is originated (muḥdath), then his description of himself is also his saying: 'I am living, powerful, beneficent, munificent, knowing.' And that speech which is the description is other than his attributes which are from his actions and from other than his actions, because the human being is originated, and his speech which is his description of himself with what he described himself is originated. And every two originated things that are neither a whole nor included within a whole are two others. And our saying 'description' (waṣf) and 'speech' (kalām) is not a description occurring on human speech and something of his attributes is speech. So it is impossible for the description to be other than that attribute, so it is necessary that the description of the human be other than the rest of his attributes which are not description, and it is necessary to detail that in the description of the pre-eternal, exalted is He, and His attributes in the way we have said and explained before.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the relationship between God's self-description and His attributes, distinguishing between attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-afʿāl). The author begins by stating that when God describes Himself with essential attributes like knowledge, life, and power, this description is not considered 'other' (ghayr) than those attributes. Evidence for this comes from the discussion on the pre-eternity of God's speech (kalām), which is like other essential attributes. He then argues that pre-eternal attributes cannot be mutually distinct in the sense of separation in time or place, so the description being other is negated. However, when God describes Himself with action attributes like 'Creator,' 'Provider,' etc., the description is other than the actions themselves because those actions are created (muḥdathāt). The attributes of essence existed before actions, proving their distinctness. The author extends this to created beings: a human being's self-description (e.g., 'I am living, powerful') is his speech, which is created and thus other than his attributes (like actual living and power), because created speech and created attributes are not part of a whole. The key point is that for God, description with essential attributes is not other, but with action attributes it is other.

Key Terms
صفات الأفعال (ṣifāt al-afʿāl)
غير (ghayr)
محدث (muḥdath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that if God's description with essential attributes is not other, then the same applies to action attributes.
Correction: Baqillani shows that description with action attributes is other because the actions are created.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why is God's description with action attributes other than the attributes themselves?
A) Because the description is pre-eternal while the actions are created.
B) Because the description is the same as the action.
C) Because action attributes are not real.
D) Because the description is created.

Key Takeaway
God's description with action attributes is other because the actions are created, unlike the inseparable essential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as 'generous' or 'patient,' do you treat your words as a separate reality from your actual character? How often do you speak of virtues you do not possess, and what accountability does this distinction create for you?
?
If God's description with action attributes is other because the action is created, then your speech about your deeds is also separate from the deeds themselves. Will you now strive to let your actions speak before your words, or continue to let your descriptions outrun your reality?
?
Baqillani says two created things that are not part of a whole are other. Your self-description ('I am a believer') is one created thing; your actual faith is another. Are you conflating the two? What would it take for your description to become one with your reality in the sight of God?

Divine Attributes
